Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
3	 Quality System Requirements
A well-defined quality system should consist of governance documents which are complemented by a concise set of
tactical or procedural requirements. The overarching documentation matrix should provide the basis of consistency
required for the creation of a sustainable quality system model which enables the ICH Q10 [8] life cycle approach
across the GxP regulated functions within an organization.

### 3.1 Application to Cross-Contamination

The key concepts discussed in this Guide should be used as a framework from which to develop standards, tools,
and approaches to manage the risk of cross-contamination.
A case by case assessment should be performed of the risk factors that the concurrent manufacture of several
products may cause. Risk assessments should be performed to properly understand the implications for both IH and
GxP. While each is normally considered separately, to prevent any significant imbalance, the implications of decisions
taken during an assessment for IH should be checked against those for GxP, and vice versa.

*[Figure 3.1: Quality Management System]*

=== PAGE 24 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

#### 3.1.1 Suggested Quality Policies/Standards for Managing the Risk of Cross-Contamination

While many aspects for managing the risk of cross-contamination are covered in various quality documents such as
cleaning and cleaning validation, line clearance, etc., it is suggested that overarching quality policies, standards and
SOPs are put in place to manage the risk of cross-contamination that tie together these various aspects.
Other quality systems that are impacted or related to managing the risk of cross-contamination include:
1.
Technology Transfer (New Product Introduction)
Firms should have a well-documented procedure for assessing potential products prior to introducing them into
the facility. This should include:
a.
A mechanism that triggers an assessment of the potential cross-contamination risks prior to bringing the
product/compound into the facility. The outcome of this assessment is to determine if the product (the
hazard) can be adequately controlled in a shared facility and/or what level of dedicated equipment and/or
facility may be required.
b.
The hazards of the product(s) should be identified typically via an ADE monograph.
c.
Cleaning process development prior to introducing the product into the facility. In situations where this
is not possible, a robust risk assessment that considers all available historical information and cleaning
performance data to assess the risk from the cleaning process, should be performed. Verification of the
cleaning should also be performed after each campaign of the new product introduced when cleaning
development activities are not able to be completed.
d.
Analytical methods sensitive enough to meet the required acceptance criteria
2.
Risk Management
The Risk Management quality system should provide a common understanding of risk and how to assess it.
Senior management should provide guidance on how to determine acceptable and unacceptable risk. It is
suggested that this be established at the corporate level so that there is a common understanding across the
organization that helps to facilitate sharing of information and comparison of different facilities.
3.
Change Control
It is suggested that the change control process includes a trigger for risk review when the change may impact
the risk of cross-contamination. Such changes may include new product introduction, the ADE value of product,
maximum daily dose for products, equipment changes, facility changes including changes to HVAC or water
quality/pressure, process changes (e.g., scale, formulation, etc.), or procedure changes.
4.
Corrective and Preventative Action (CAPA)
An outcome of the risk assessment activities may be to implement some risk reduction controls. The CAPA system
may be used to track the risk mitigation measures until implemented and confirmation of performance is complete.
5.
Product Quality Review
Results from the risk management process may become part of the product quality review as appropriate.
In addition, requalification or monitoring programs should also provide an indication that the risk of cross-
contamination is in a controlled state.
6.
Training
It is important that basic GMP training for all relevant staff includes practices that are designed to avoid cross-
contamination as well as understand how cross-contamination occurs. Staff involved in managing the risk of cross-
contamination should have further training in both how to avoid cross-contamination and quality risk management.

=== PAGE 25 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 3.2 The Logic Diagram

A logic diagram is a simple tool that can be used to guide a team through a process to determine the adequacy
of existing controls or the need for dedicated facilities. (Section 3.6.1 should be read in conjunction with the flow
diagram in Chapter 14.) Determining the need for dedicated facilities should be performed by a multidisciplinary team,
so that all required areas are addressed appropriately.
The logic diagram is divided into two, addressing both GMP/Regulatory logic and IH logic. Both are equally important
as they deal with aspects of risk associated with the different populations that may be exposed to the hazard under
review (left side – product, right side – worker).
Where dedicated facilities are implemented for business reasons, the risk assessment should clearly state that this is
a business decision and not a cross-contamination issue.

#### 3.2.1 Considering the GMP/Regulatory Factors

See Figure 3.2. For a larger version of the diagram, see Chapter 14.

*[Figure 3.2: GMP/Regulatory Factors]*

=== PAGE 26 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
3.2.1.1 Gathering Hazard Information (e.g., Health Based Criteria)
Both the IH and GMP criteria are drawn from common toxicological or clinical data sources. Each should have a
science-based approach to risk management and clear strategies for the assessment of potential exposure, (e.g.,
monitoring). The ADE value is normally the health-based criterion used in assessing GMP concerns related to cross-
contamination. The OEL is normally the health-based criterion used in assessing airborne product for worker protection.

##### 3.2.1.2 Specific Requirements for Handling Product in a Dedicated Facility

“Is there a written regulation that would preclude the co-manufacture of this product with other non-related
materials?”
This is the first question of the logic process.
The usual example quoted is that of the beta-lactam class of antibiotics where some current GMPs, e.g., 21 CFR
211.42(c)(d) [13] require separate facilities for their manufacture, processing, and packing. The updated EU
EudraLex Vol. 4 Section 3.6 [4] requires dedicated facilities if:
1.
“the risk cannot be adequately controlled by operational and/or technical measures”
2.
“scientific data from the toxicological evaluation does not support a controllable risk (e.g. allergenic potential from
highly sensitizing materials such as beta lactams)”
3.
“relevant residue limits, derived from the toxicological evaluation, cannot be satisfactorily determined by a
validated analytical method.”
These issues are not necessarily understood at this point in the logic diagram. It is expected that by following the
logic diagram these aspects will be understood.
In addition to the criteria above that reflect the hazard and likelihood and severity of harm there are numerous
reasons to dedicate a single product to a single facility, including:
•
A consequence of a product specific agency inspection
•
Internal QA recommendation
•
Manufacturing technology may be unique to the product
•
Sales demand may be sufficiently high to fully utilize available capacity
Operational reasons for dedicated facilities should not be used to set a precedent for similar materials in different
settings; this should be determined on a case by case basis.
It may not be possible to reduce the degree of risk to an acceptable level without incurring time or cost penalties. In
this case, the pragmatic approach may be to dedicate a facility. The situation should be revisited if key factors change
(manufacturing or containment technology improves, demand changes, etc.).

##### 3.2.1.3 Determining an ADE

“Can an ADE be determined?”
Based on the information from the Gather Hazard Information it will be known if the scientific data from the
toxicological evaluation supports the determination of an ADE. See Chapter 5 for more information.
If the information supports the determination, then an ADE value should be determined and passed on to the other
stakeholders for further assessments by following the logic diagram.

=== PAGE 27 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
If the information does not support the determination of an ADE value, then a dedicated facility is required. As noted
above, this is where the beta-lactam family of products would be addressed.

##### 3.2.1.4 Detection of Residue Limits

“Can relevant residue limits be detected?”
Proper utilization of equipment requires knowledge of the potential for retention or carryover from one product to
another. This is a characteristic based on the chemical and physical nature of the compound and the design and
materials of construction of the equipment. The acceptable level of retention or carryover should be determined,
on a case by case basis, taking into account the hazard(s) presented by the product and the nature and route of
administration of the product that will next be processed in the equipment, suite, or facility. An appropriate safety
margin should be incorporated when establishing acceptable levels. See Chapters 5 and 6 for further information.
Compounds that can be denatured/degraded/deactivated require studies to ensure the effectiveness of the process
to render the residues as safe. The basis for this study should include development of an ADE value to assess the
margin of safety of the remaining residues that could potentially carryover to the next product’s production.
Once the relevant cleaning residue limits are known, validated analytical methods should be established in order to
detect that the cleaning residue limits have been met.
If residue limits for other purposes are used (e.g., for air monitoring), validated analytical methods may also be required.
If analytical methods cannot be validated to the appropriate level, then a dedicated facility is necessary.

##### 3.2.1.5 Technical and Operational Controls

“Do technical and operational controls manage the risk?”
Each of the modes of cross-contamination should be assessed to determine whether technical and operational
controls can manage the risk.
Care should be taken when assessing manual operations and the controls in place to detect manual errors. A strategy
to reduce the risk of manual errors is required.

##### 3.2.1.6 Cleaning (Retention) Aspects

“Can cleaning be carried out to meet the required criteria?”
Factors to be considered when assessing whether the cleaning criteria can be met or detected, include:
•
Feasibility: if a very low level of acceptable carryover is defined, are there analytical methods that will achieve
the level of detection required? If an aggressive cleaning agent (e.g., caustics or acids) will be used, what effects
will this cleaning agent have on the equipment or materials of construction? Will there be pitting or corrosion
of surfaces with long-term use? Will equipment parts become corroded or damaged over time (e.g., gasket
materials)? The risk assessment should consider such possibilities.
•
Practicability: is the cleaning method consistent and easy to complete (automated versus manual)? For manual
cleaning are the procedures detailed sufficiently for consistent cleaning regardless of the variability of the
personnel? Would the effort required to perform cleaning to the defined level be practical? Would the facility need
to be shut down for an extended period? Would extra personnel be required to undertake the cleaning? Would
high volumes of solvent or other chemicals be required presenting a different risk scenario (e.g., exposure of
workers to the cleaning materials, containment and cost of disposal of solvents)?

=== PAGE 28 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Cost: are the direct and indirect costs associated with an extended clean down acceptable? If this is a once a
year scenario the answer may be “yes.” However, if this is an exercise that may need to be conducted weekly, it
may not be an economically feasible option.
•
Patient: the above considerations do not supersede the needs of the patient who ultimately will be administered
or consume the drug product. It is important to note that risk to the patient is one of the primary focuses of the
risk assessment.
If these factors can all be addressed satisfactorily, then the use of a multiproduct facility with shared equipment may
be a viable option for the processing or manufacture of the material or product under consideration. If the answer is
no or not easily to any of the above questions, then some element of dedication should be considered.
A number of additional questions should be addressed before confirming the feasibility of a multiproduct facility. The
logic diagram poses further questions, which should be answered on a case by case basis to determine the degree of
dedication that may be required.
3.2.1.7	 Cleaning/Retention Resolution
“Can the cleaning criteria be modified?”
For example, if the current method cannot be validated to the appropriate sensitivity, consideration to improving the
method is an option.
If the cleaning criteria are hard to achieve, improving or altering the cleaning procedure and/or cleaning agents is an
option.
If a worst-case approach is used, and the new product to be introduced is a new worst case and is difficult to achieve,
excluding the new compound from the worst case and treating it as a separate condition may be an option.

##### 3.2.1.8 Partial Cleaning/Retention Resolution

“Can the cleaning criteria be met for some of the stages?”
If these factors cannot be addressed easily, dedication may need to be considered. Total dedication of an entire
facility or equipment train may be unnecessary. Instead, dedicating or using single use technology for some of the
equipment train, to help make the cleaning residue criteria more manageable, may be a cost-effective option.

##### 3.2.1.9 Mix-Up Aspects

“Are procedures, controls, and facilities designed such that mix-up is avoided?”
Factors to be considered when assessing whether mix-up can be avoided include:
•
Procedures/SOPs: identify areas where the existing procedures may fail or the consequences if the procedures
are not followed. Are additional procedures needed? Are the existing procedures too complicated to follow? Are
the procedures verified by suitable means?
•
Controls: identify areas where the existing controls may fail. Are additional controls needed?
•
Facility Design: is the facility layout such that there are no shared process flows or areas of process overlap? If
not, can the procedures and controls effectively prevent mix-up?
If these factors can all be addressed satisfactorily, then the use of a multiproduct facility may be a viable option for the
processing or manufacture of the material or product under consideration. If the answer is no or not easily to any of
the above questions, then some element of dedication should be considered. See Chapter 9 for further information.

=== PAGE 29 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 3.2.1.10 Mix-up Resolution

“Can procedures/controls or design elements be introduced or modified to avoid mix-up?”
It may be necessary to conduct a number of specific, low level, assessments in order to be confident that any risks
associated with the accommodation and processing of the material under consideration can be managed or reduced
to an acceptable level. In this situation, it should be possible to use a multiproduct facility.

##### 3.2.1.11 Partial Mix-up Resolution

“Can procedures/controls or design elements be introduced or modified to avoid mix-up for some of the
stages?”
If these factors cannot be addressed easily, dedication may need to be considered. Total dedication of an entire
facility may be unnecessary, however, even if achievable without incurring a significant extra cost. Instead, a more
limited approach to segregation or dedication may be a cost-effective option.

##### 3.2.1.12 Mechanical Transfer

“Is the potential for mechanical transfer to be controlled to safe predetermined levels?”
Factors to be considered when assessing whether the mechanical transfer criteria can be met include:
•
Process: is the process closed? Can material escape the process and be transported around the facility via
equipment, product, intermediates, supplies, and personnel moving through the facility?
•
Procedures: are procedures and/or controls in place to minimize mechanical transfer, e.g., gown changes prior
to leaving processing rooms? Are procedures and/or controls in place that states equipment is to be wiped down
prior to leaving the processing rooms? Are there means to prevent transfer from feet or wheels?
•
Facility: are there other GxP issues that might introduce risk? For example, would movement of personnel or
materials within the facility or inadequate differential pressures between manufacturing zones give cause for
concern? A more specific risk assessment to review these aspects may be warranted.
•
Operational Standards: is the facility being operated to a standard that is adequate for the processing of the
materials under consideration? Is specialist knowledge required? Do process and maintenance workers need
specialist training prior to handling the product? Is containment managed through engineering controls alone or
are additional procedural controls used? Are these adequate? If there is a reliance on engineering controls does
this introduce issues at changeover from one product to another? Such questions may be particularly relevant if
considering the use of a third-party Contract Manufacturing Organization (CMO).
If these factors can all be addressed satisfactorily, then the use of a multiproduct facility may be a viable option for
the processing or manufacture of the material or product under consideration. If the answer is no or not easily to
any of the above questions, then appropriate dedication strategies should be considered. See Chapter 9 for further
information.

##### 3.2.1.13 Mechanical Transfer Resolution

“Can modifications or procedures be put in place to control mechanical transfer to safe predetermined
levels?”
It may be necessary to conduct a number of specific, low level assessments in order to be confident that any risks
associated with the accommodation and processing of the material under consideration can be managed or reduced
to an acceptable level. In this situation, it should be possible to use a multiproduct facility.

=== PAGE 30 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 3.2.1.14 Partial Mechanical Transfer Resolution

“Can modifications or procedures be put into place to control mechanical transfer to safe predetermined
levels for some of the stages?”
If these factors cannot be addressed easily, dedication may need to be considered. Total dedication of an entire
facility may be unnecessary; however, even if achievable without incurring a significant extra cost. Instead, a more
limited approach to segregation or dedication may be a cost-effective option.

##### 3.2.1.15 Airborne Transfer

“Is the potential for airborne transfer to be controlled to safe predetermined levels?”
Factors to be considered when assessing whether the airborne transfer criteria can be met include:
•
Process: is the product exposed to the room environment during processing, transfer, or cleaning? Is the process
closed? Can material escape the process?
•
Facility: are there other GxP issues that might introduce risk? For example, would movement of personnel or
materials within the facility or inadequate differential pressures between manufacturing zones give cause for
concern? Are material and personnel airlocks provided? If so, are they adequate at controlling airborne transfer?
A more specific risk assessment to review these aspects may be warranted.
If these factors can all be addressed satisfactorily, then the use of a multiproduct facility may be a viable option for the
processing or manufacture of the material or product under consideration. If the answer is no or not easily to any of
the above questions, then some element of dedication should be considered. See Chapter 9 for further information.

##### 3.2.1.16 Airborne Transfer Resolution

“Can modifications or procedures be put into place to control airborne transfer to safe predetermined
levels?”
It may be necessary to conduct a number of specific, low level assessments in order to be confident that any risks
associated with the accommodation and processing of the material under consideration can be managed or reduced
to an acceptable level. In this situation, it should be possible to use a multiproduct facility.

##### 3.2.1.17 Partial Airborne Transfer Resolution

“Can modifications or procedures be put into place to control airborne transfer to safe predetermined levels
for some of the stages?”
If these factors cannot be addressed easily, dedication may need to be considered. Total dedication of an entire
facility may be unnecessary; however, even if achievable without incurring a significant extra cost. Instead, a more
limited approach to segregation or dedication may be a cost-effective option.

=== PAGE 31 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 3.2.1.18 Accommodating Product in a Multiproduct Facility

“Can the product be accommodated in a multiproduct facility, but with more specific dedication?”
When dealing with highly hazardous materials, application of a thorough risk management approach to a specific
accommodation or manufacturing proposal, may allow the need for total dedication to be reasonably challenged.
However, the risk assessment(s) may not provide sufficient confidence to allow co-manufacture with all other
products currently being handled within the same facility. In such cases, it is worth considering some limited
dedication or segregation to address those aspects of the handling or process seen as still posing an unacceptable
level of risk:
•
Dedicated Suite/Room in a multiproduct facility: dedicating a defined suite, room, or area may be sufficient to
address local risks associated with cleaning and/or other GxP concerns unique to the materials and processes
under consideration.
•
Dedicated Equipment: dedication of an individual vessel or item of processing equipment, or contact parts (e.g.,
feed chutes, compression tooling, and filling heads) may be all that is required to reduce the specific area of risk
under review. Consideration should be given to the cleaning, storage, and segregation of such items.
•
Disposable Equipment: it may be more economical to dispose of and replace small items of equipment (e.g.,
filling tubing, filters, or glassware), an option which is being utilized increasingly in the biotechnology sector. In
this situation, it is still necessary to consider the cleaning/decontamination requirements and the consequences
of disposal. How will the items be transported and disposed of? This should be the subject of a further, specific
risk assessment.

=== PAGE 32 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
3.2.2 Considering the Health and Hygiene Factors (Applicable to Personnel)
See Figure 3.3. A larger version of the diagram is provided in Chapter 14.

*[Figure 3.3: Health and Hygiene Factors]*

=== PAGE 33 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
3.2.2.1	 Gather Hazard Information (e.g., Health-Based Criteria)
Both the IH and GMP criteria are drawn from common toxicological or clinical data sources. Each should have
a science-based approach to risk management and clear strategies for assessment of potential exposure, (e.g.,
monitoring). The OEL is normally the health-based criterion used in assessing airborne levels of API from bulk or
finished product for protection of workers.

##### 3.2.2.2 Assess Exposure Potential

Exposure potential may differ depending on the way a material is processed or the facility where processing occurs.
Risk is a function of both hazard and exposure. Evaluating the likelihood of exposure is a key part of the risk
management process. Several factors should be considered:
•
Who is at risk of exposure? – Workers at the line, maintenance personnel, general personnel in the area, or QA
personnel when sampling product?
•
What factors might influence exposure? – Is it a dusty powder? Is it a liquid? Is it a closed or open process? How
well trained are the personnel? Is it a routine or frequent task?
•
What is the pathway for exposure? – Inhalation, ingestion, or skin absorption?
•
When is exposure most likely to occur? – Do workers need to directly intervene in the process for sampling, at
process breakdown, clean down, or product changeover?

##### 3.2.2.3 Control of Exposure

“Are the exposures adequately controlled?”
Information that is already available should be reviewed to help determine how well exposure is controlled:
•
Is there experience with the material under review? – Have any adverse health effects been reported? Does the
nature of the material (e.g., wet, lumpy, solid) or the process (e.g., closed) suggest that risk is unlikely to be high?
•
Are there any hard data? – Are there any monitoring data available? Are there similar or identical processes
being operated in other facilities?
•
What is known about the exposure profile for the specific product and process as it is operated, or might be
operated, in a particular facility?
The controls that are applied should be commensurate with the risk and should be considered in the context of all the
requirements of the manufacturing process, e.g., whether a particular engineering control would make it difficult to
intervene if the process runs out of control or makes it very difficult to clean without affecting efficient plant utilization.

##### 3.2.2.4 Process Changes

“Is elimination, substitution, or process change possible?”
While process or formulation changes are often difficult to make with established products, the opportunity to
reduce exposure potential by this means is feasible if considered early enough in the product development process.
Examples where this has been shown to be effective include:
1.
Removal of a solid recovery step between stages in a chemical process

=== PAGE 34 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
2.
Change to a wet granulation process from a direct blend process for tablet manufacture
3.
Addition of a film coat to a tablet in order to reduce the risk of exposure during packing
4.
Formulate as a liquid/semisolid in a capsule rather than as a conventional tablet or powder filled capsule
Each of these examples addresses only one step in a process chain and potential for exposure may still exist in other
steps.

##### 3.2.2.5 Selection of Engineering Controls

Once risk factors have been investigated, control measures can be considered. The correct control system should
be determined and should consider such factors as cost, feasibility, ergonomics, ease of cleaning, and failure mode
operation, i.e., what to do if the control measure fails.
Thought needs to be given about converting the knowledge on exposure potential into the performance requirements
for engineering controls. How much confidence can be placed on Assigned Protection Factors (APFs)? What
experience is there in-house of using similar control options?

##### 3.2.2.6 Level of Exposure Risk

“Is the exposure risk still too high?”
Having selected a control to address a measured exposure, what happens if the exposure risk is still at an
unacceptable level? Is it reasonable to add in a secondary system, such as an extract system, to reduce the
exposure potential to an acceptable level?
Is routine use of Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) acceptable for an activity of short duration? There is no clear
answer and such options should be discussed with operating personnel, plant management, and HSE specialists. In
some cases, operators may prefer PPE if a more permanent rigid barrier makes the process step difficult to perform
from an ergonomic perspective.

##### 3.2.2.7 Emergency Recovery

“Is emergency recovery required?”
What happens if the primary containment system fails? What knowledge is there of the possible failure modes
and their frequency for a given system? Is failure acceptable under any circumstances? Such factors should be
considered as part of the risk assessment.
•
What will need to be recovered, how, and by whom?
•
What supporting infrastructure will be required? For example:
-
Would PPE need to be provided?
-
Would change areas, showers, storage areas for PPE be required?
-
Would a separate air supply be required to service any PPE?

=== PAGE 35 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 3.2.2.8 Secondary PPE

“Is secondary control or PPE required?”
Where available, the rationale for the use of secondary PPE should be well understood and documented. Is it
provided to supplement a primary control measure and reduce an assessed exposure potential to an acceptable
level? Is it provided for emergency use only?

#### 3.2.3 Consequences of Control Strategy

The consequences of adopting any particular exposure control strategy should be rechecked against the GxP/
regulatory logic to ensure plant operability is not comprised.

=== PAGE 36 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

=== PAGE 37 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
4	 Risk Assessment
Risk assessment as described in ICH Q9 [1] encompasses three steps: risk identification, risk analysis, and risk
evaluation. Each of the three steps helps to answer the three fundamental questions:
•
What might go wrong?
•
What is the likelihood (probability) it will go wrong?
•
What are the consequences (severity)?

*[Figure 4.1: Risk Assessment]*

The risk assessment steps may be considered the most important aspects of an overall quality risk management
process. If the risks are not identified, analyzed, and evaluated properly, the decisions on how to control the risk
cannot be made adequately.
The criteria for judging acceptability of risk for cross-contamination and operator exposures differ, although GxP
and IH considerations are linked because they share the same underlying toxicological data as the starting point
from which they are both derived. In addition, the application and interpretation of the criteria in the risk assessment
process will be different.

=== PAGE 38 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Part of the risk assessment process is to consider if there are realistically any plausible pathways that might give rise
to an increased risk of exposure. For example, when considering airborne transfer, the following risk questions should
be asked:
•
Can cross-contamination from an airborne pathway occur? Specifically, can product A be directly distributed via
the air stream or resuspended back into the air (after being deposited) in a room/suite/facility, and then settle or
get redeposited directly into product B or onto contact parts to be used for the manufacture of product B?
•
Can this occur in sufficient amount that the ADE for product A might be exceeded should a patient be
administered the now contaminated product B?
•
Is it plausible for product that has been deposited on equipment or adjacent work surfaces to be redispersed into
the air stream and present an exposure risk for operators via inhalation?
Similar questions should be asked to understand the risks associated with mix-up, retention, and mechanical transfer.
Consideration of plausible pathways should be on a case by case basis as part of the overall risk assessment. These
pathways, typically, are different for patients and operators.
At the conclusion of the risk assessment, risk should be expressed in quantitative or qualitative terms depending on
the data available from the three steps of the risk assessment.
This information should then be used in the decision making to accept or reduce the risks under the risk control steps.

=== PAGE 39 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
5	 Risk Identification
This Chapter describes risk identification, which starts with qualitative hazard identification, i.e., the process by which
existing hazards are identified, initially without assigning magnitude. All available animal and human data should
be reviewed to address the question: “What could go wrong?” This first step in the risk assessment process also
includes identifying possible consequences. It serves as a prelude to later steps of risk analysis and risk evaluation
that ultimately lead to appropriate exposure control and management of risk.

*[Figure 5.1: Risk Identification]*

### 5.1 Definition of Hazard

Hazard is defined in ICH Q9 [1] as a potential source of harm. ICH Q9 also includes the severity of harm in
the definition of risk. Within the context of this Guide, hazard describes the inherent property of a compound to
produce adverse effects, e.g., in patients that may be exposed to the compound as a trace contaminant in another
pharmaceutical product. The compounds typically considered include:
•
APIs
•
Process intermediates
•
Solvents

=== PAGE 40 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Excipients
Other compounds that may need to be considered include impurities, byproducts, and degradants. Each compound
has its own inherent ability to cause adverse effects (i.e., toxicity) – effects that may be well documented in the case
of the API, but may be unknown for other compounds.

### 5.2 Hazard Continuum and Prioritization for Risk Assessment

Depending on the hazardous properties of an individual compound (see Figure 5.2), resulting from its mechanism-
of-action and potency, health-based limits, such as the ADE values, can span several orders of magnitude. It
is preferable to derive these health-based values for all pharmaceutical compounds, regardless of potency, to
ensure the protection of patients. Similarly, there is a need to focus attention on those compounds with the lowest
health-based exposure limits because these may be the compounds that are the most difficult to control, and
the consequences of overexposure may be the greatest. In general, special controls may be needed to maintain
exposures to these compounds below acceptable levels. As part of an overall risk management approach, it is
useful to have a qualitative tool to help determine the need for, and to prioritize, comprehensive quantitative risk
assessments. This tool should ideally incorporate the severity of the effect/impact on quality of life, reversibility of the
effect and likelihood of death.

*[Figure 5.2: Hazard Continuum]*

One approach is to apply a set of criteria to identify the most hazardous drugs in a health care facility. Some
pharmaceutical organizations have developed criteria to identify compounds in the same or similar categories
highlighted in various GxP guidance documents, i.e., “certain steroids, certain antibiotics, certain cytotoxics, and other
highly active compounds.” [33] Other pharmaceutical organizations use a control banding approach to categorize
compounds into categories of increasing severity based on their inherent pharmacological and toxicological
properties. Sussman et al. (2016a) [34] provide guidance on how to identify and assess highly hazardous drugs
within a quality management system.
The following criteria can be used to identify highly hazardous drugs and should be used as just one input of a
comprehensive risk assessment/risk management program. Identification of a compound as a hazardous drug should
not automatically lead to strict control solutions (e.g., a dedicated facility) because it addresses only hazard although,
because of potential risks to operators, these compounds tend to have more control than low hazard drugs. A risk
analysis, as described in Chapter 6, should be completed before any conclusions can be made about risk and the
need to modify controls.
Criteria for identifying hazardous drugs and prioritizing risk assessments include:
1.
Genotoxic compounds that are known to be or highly likely to be carcinogenic to humans

=== PAGE 41 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
2.
Compounds that can produce reproductive and/or developmental effects at low dosages*
3.
Compounds that can produce serious target organ toxicity or other significant adverse effects at low dosages*
*For example clinical doses < 1 to 10 mg/day or animal doses < 0.1 to 1 mg/kg/day
The use of Occupational Exposure Limits (OELs) and Occupational Exposure Bands (OEBs) can be used to estimate
ADE values for the purpose of prioritization in risk assessments, which also includes an assessment of potential
exposure. Use of OELs and OEBs has the advantage of capturing concerns for potency, severity and reversibility
of effect, and alteration of the quality of life and length of life. Extrapolating an OEL to an ADE value, in its simplest
sense, is accomplished by multiplying the OEL (μg/m3) by 10 m3, the volume of air breathed by a worker in 8 hours
(ADE (μg/day) = OEL (μg/m3 × 10 m3). The simple estimation of an ADE using an existing OEL is meant only to
facilitate risk ranking. This does not mean a formal ADE should not be derived if an OEL exists. If an OEL has
been established, it means that sufficient data were available to derive a numerical value. The same data can be
used to derive a formal ADE and this activity should be routinely performed and documented to support formal risk
assessments as part of a quality risk management program.
Many companies assign new compounds to an OEB, or equivalent designation corresponding to airborne
concentration ranges, to provide guidance on safe handling before sufficient data are available to establish a
numerical OEL. The OEB is assigned based on the compounds’ inherent pharmacological and toxicological
characteristics, including the intended use, mechanism of action, dose-response, and data from available studies.
One option is to use the OEBs to estimate ADE values for the most hazardous subset of compounds (i.e., those with
the most restrictive OEBs). Estimating ADE values from OEBs involves using the same approach used for OELs, as
applied to the lower end of the range of airborne concentrations associated with the OEB (see example in Table 5.1).
There are some limitations regarding the use of OELs and OEBs to estimate ADE values due to potential differences
in the target subpopulation(s), critical effects, and differences in bioavailability. For this reason, a qualified toxicologist
should be involved in these estimations.
Table 5.1: Estimating ADE Values Using Occupational Exposure Bands (OEBs) – An Example
The approaches described in this Guide for estimating ADE values using OELs and OEBs for purposes of prioritizing
risk assessments, may also be useful early in drug development before sufficient data are available to set formal ADE
values, e.g., when GMP material is being manufactured for initial clinical trials. In all cases, these approaches should
be considered an interim strategy until formal, fully documented ADE values can be developed.
OEB
Concentration Range
Low End of the Band
Estimated ADE*
≥ 1 < 5 mg/m3
1 mg/m3
10 mg/day
≥ 0.1 < 1 mg/m3
0.1 mg/m3
1 mg/day
≥ 10 < 100 µg/m3
10 µg/m3
100 µg/day
≥ 1 < 10 µg/m3
1 µg/m3
10 µg/day
< 1 µg/m3
< 1 µg/m3
< 1 µg/day
* Estimated ADE = Low End of the Band × 10 m3

=== PAGE 42 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 5.3 Establishing Health-Based Exposure Limits

The foundation of a risk-based approach for manufacturing pharmaceutical products comprises:
•
The identification of hazards
•
Assessment of dose-response relationships
•
Establishment of health-based limits
The identification of hazards and assessment of dose-response relationships are collectively referred to as hazard
characterization.
Having an understanding of how a compound can cause harm, and at what dose, is crucial to evaluating risks and
deciding on appropriate exposure controls.
In addition to identifying what could go wrong, this step in the risk assessment process also provides important
information on the consequences of exposure. Such consequences are considered in the establishment of a health-
based limit (e.g., ADE) taking both the dose-response relationship and the severity of effect into account.
The approach used to derive ADE values is consistent with a number of guidance documents on setting health-based
exposure limits that are available (e.g., WHO 2005 [35]).
Industry toxicologists also have published a number of peer reviewed articles on setting health-based exposure limits
(Sargent and Kirk, 1988 [36]; Conine et al., 1992 [37]; Galer et al., 1992 [38]; Naumann and Weideman, 1995 39];
Schwartz, 1995 [40]; Silverman et al., 1999 [41]).
More recently, several reviews have been published summarizing these earlier publications and incorporating, and
building on, the information on setting health-based exposure limits provided in the first edition of this Baseline®
Guide (Sargent, et al., 2013 [42]; Bercu et al., 2013a [43]; Sussman et al., 2016b [44]). The EMA guidance document
on setting Permitted Daily Exposure limits (PDEs) based on the ICH Q3C(R6) [9] guidance for residual solvents
utilizes a similar set of adjustment (assessment) factors. As mentioned above, the PDE is essentially synonymous
with the ADE. Where applicable, similarities and differences, albeit minor, will be identified below.
The guidance provided in this Chapter reflects the general principles and selected specific recommendations from
these guidance documents and peer reviewed articles. It also describes the basic elements of a toxicological
evaluation, as this term is used in the EMA guidance on setting health-based exposure limits and the EU GMPs (Vol.
4, Chapters 3 and 5 [4, 5]). In practice, the methods used to derive health-based exposure limits may differ slightly,
but it is the responsibility of the organization to ensure that all relevant sources of uncertainty and variability are
addressed, regardless of which adjustment factor covers them, and that their recommended limits are scientifically
defensible and adequately protective.

#### 5.3.1 Data Collection and Analysis

For hazard identification, a formal review of all available relevant animal and human data should be performed
for each compound. This review should be done by a qualified toxicologist. This is an individual that has many
years of experience setting health-based limits such as ADE values and OELs, preferably in the pharmaceutical
industry. Specific training in toxicology is necessary, whether at MS or PhD level, but degrees in related fields such
as pharmacology or medicine may be sufficient when coupled with appropriate specific training and experience.
Certification in general toxicology is desirable but not required.
For the innovator companies that developed the API, the data used in the analysis should include the data submitted in
the regulatory filing. Contract Manufacturing Organizations (CMOs) should request this information, and the rationale
for the ADE that may have already been derived, from the innovator company asking them to manufacture the drug.
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 43 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
The reviewer should have access to the full range of preclinical and clinical data required for approval of the drug.
For non-proprietary compounds, all relevant information on the potential hazards of the material should be obtained
through reliable sources. Standard toxicology and pharmacology reference texts should be consulted and a literature
search (e.g., on PubMed1) should be performed. The internet is also a useful source for information on generic drugs
provided trusted sources are used. Searching on the generic name of the drug often surfaces much of the information
needed to derive a health-based exposure limit. Drug approval agencies (e.g., FDA, EMA, National Competent
Authorities) are good sources for summary documents on APIs and associated marketed products. Caution should
be used when utilizing Safety Data Sheets (SDSs) as a reference source, since the credibility of the information
may vary. The toxicologist performing the hazard assessment and calculating the health-based exposure limit is
responsible for assessing the gathered data from the publicly available sources with regard to relevancy, adequacy,
and reliability of the collected information. The hazard characterization process should commence once all of the
relevant information has been compiled.

#### 5.3.2 Identification of the Critical Effect

The purpose of a hazard evaluation is to identify all possible hazards associated with a compound and to rank them
according to their severity. When combined with a dose-response assessment, the critical effect can be defined. This
is typically the first clinically significant adverse effect that is observed as the dose increases.
The critical effect should be used for the derivation of an ADE. Ideally, the critical effect should be derived from
studies for the same route that the ADE value will be used to evaluate. Extrapolation from data for other routes may
be necessary if relevant route specific data are not available. Exposures below the No-Observed-Adverse-Effect-
Level (NOAEL) for the critical effect should not, by definition, lead to other effects, as these will only occur at higher
doses. The reviewing toxicologist should determine which of the effects reported at low dosages is the critical effect. It
may be necessary to evaluate several endpoints, which may involve different sets of adjustment factors. A justification
should be made considering the final ADE value, which is based on the most sensitive effect relevant to humans. The
same approach for evaluating animal data is used for risk assessments involving veterinary products.
Due to differences in the route of exposure, the toxicological profile of the compound in question and the potential for
local effects at the site of contact, the critical effect may be different for patients and operators.
Professional judgment is required to discern between biological effects that may be physiological or adaptive changes
or subclinical pharmacological effects of the drug, which would have associated Lowest-Observed-Effect Levels
(LOELs). These biological effects should be distinguished from clinically significant effects which are considered
adverse effects.
Clinically significant effects should be considered adverse effects for anyone not under the supervision of a physician
prescribing this drug for a specific medical condition. These would have associated Lowest-Observed-Adverse-Effect
Level (LOAELs). Quite often, the LOAEL for these effects is used as the Point-of-Departure (PoD) for derivation of the
ADE value.

#### 5.3.3 Dose-Response Assessment

During the hazard characterization process, all effects potentially attributed to a compound should be documented.
Each effect, whether considered adverse or not, has a dose-response relationship. In general, as the dose increases,
the incidence and severity of the effect increases.
For systemic toxicity endpoints, there is a dose below which no effects are expected. This is often referred to as the
threshold for toxicity.
1	 PubMed.gov is part of the US National Library of Medicine (NIH). PubMed comprises more than 19 million citations for biomedical literature from
MEDLINE, life science journals, and online books. Citations may include links to full text content from PubMed Central and publisher websites. http://
www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed.

=== PAGE 44 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
For some endpoints such as cancer, due to direct damage to DNA, it is often assumed that a threshold does not
exist and some effects (e.g., mutations) are possible at low doses. In this case, it is necessary to determine the
acceptable level of response for the effect (e.g., a 1/100,000 excess cancer risk). It should be noted that this may be
viewed as a conservative approach given that there are DNA repair mechanisms that may reverse the mutations and
immunosurveillance mechanisms that prevent cancers from developing.

#### 5.3.4 Establishment of the NOAEL or Benchmark Dose

For most toxicity endpoints, there is a clear dose-response relationship for the critical effect. The aim is to define
the NOAEL for the critical effect, which may be used to derive the ADE. The application of adjustment factors (see
Section 5.3.5) results in ADE values that are unlikely to produce any undesirable compound-related effects.
It may not be possible to define the NOAEL for some toxicology data sets, and therefore, it may be necessary to
derive the health-based limit using a LOAEL. This occurs when statistically significant adverse effects are observed at
the lowest dose used in a study. The Benchmark Dose (BMD) can be used in this situation.
The BMD is a value, equivalent to a NOAEL, that is derived mathematically by fitting a curve to the dose-response
data for the critical effect. (Software to derive a BMD is available on the US EPA Website [45]. The BMD10 is defined
as the dose that corresponds to a 10% response rate, the minimum level of response typically detected by standard
toxicology studies. The statistical lower bound on the BMD10 (BMDL10) is often used in risk assessments as a
surrogate for a NOAEL. Caution should be taken when extrapolating well below the observable range. (A tutorial and
a technical guidance document on the use of the BMD are available on the EPA website.)

#### 5.3.5 Application of Adjustment Factors

Health-based exposure limits, such as ADE values, are derived by dividing the NOAEL for the critical effect (after
adjusting for body weight, e.g., 50 kg) by various adjustment factors (also termed assessment, safety, or uncertainty
factors) to extrapolate to the “true” no effect level in the subpopulation of interest (e.g., elderly patients). Depending
on the assumptions about the composition of these subpopulations, different adjustment factors may be used;
however, the goal is to protect the most susceptible individuals.
The application of adjustment factors to NOAELs is an accepted approach for non-carcinogens, but is also used for
compounds that cause cancer by non-genotoxic mechanisms (see discussion below). It is a policy decision within an
organization as to whether an adjustment factor or linear extrapolation approach should be used in a given situation.
Regardless of the approach employed, only peer reviewed methods that have been validated through use and
experience, should be used to set safe levels of exposure.
The NOAEL is typically derived from a single key study of a relatively small number of animals or humans. Adjustment
factors have been defined for each of the main sources of uncertainty and variability as described in this Chapter.
These are multiplied together to form a Composite Adjustment Factor (AFC) that is used in the denominator of the
general equation used to set health-based limits (see below).
The ADE is used to derive swab or rinse limits for cleaning validation purposes as well as for other assessments of
cross-contamination. In order to apply ADE values to specific subpopulations, further adjustments may be required
to address various sources of uncertainty and variability, as well as differences in bioavailability when extrapolating
between different routes of exposure. Ideally, the ADE is based on the data for the route that it will be applied to in the
evaluation. If route-to-route extrapolation is necessary, a sound scientific rationale is required to support application to
a different route.
Calculation of the Acceptable Daily Exposure (ADE) Value
PoD × BW
ADE (mg/day) =	______________
AFC × MF × PK

=== PAGE 45 ===

Where:	 ADE = Acceptable Daily Exposure (mg/day)
PoD = Point-of-Departure (e.g., NOAEL, LOAEL) (mg/kg/day)
BW = Body Weight (kg)
AFC = Composite Adjustment Factor
MF = Modifying Factor
PK = Pharmacokinetic Adjustment(s)
For comparison, the following equation is used in the EMA guideline on setting health-based exposure limits.
Individual assessment factors F1 to F5 are included for reference, where appropriate, in Table 5.2.
NOAEL × BW
PDE =	____________________

F1 × F2 × F3 × F4 × F5
A number of publications discuss the scientific basis for the adjustment factors discussed below and the process
for deriving ADEs (Naumann and Weideman, 1995 [39]; Sargent et al., 2013 [42]; Sussman et al., 2016b [44]).
Alternative factors may be used if supported by available data for a specific compound and a sound scientific
rationale. The use of specific default values for adjustment factors is a policy decision and should be based on
generally accepted practices and data in the literature. Toxicological expertise is normally required to determine the
appropriate adjustment factor(s) to apply.

##### 5.3.5.1 Addressing Sources of Uncertainty Using Science-Based Adjustment Factors

Table 5.2 shows the correspondence between the various adjustment factors commonly used in human health risk
assessment and the EMA guidance on setting health based exposure limits. For each factor discussed below, the
similarities and differences, albeit minor, will be identified. The experience of a qualified toxicologist is required to
decipher the nuances implicit within each factor. As pointed out in the EMA guidance [5], deviation from the main
approach to deriving safe levels may be appropriate if adequately justified.

ADVANCED TOPIC
a.
Interspecies Differences (AFA or F1): This adjustment factor is used to extrapolate a NOAEL (or LOAEL) from
animal studies to a human subpopulation. A common approach is to use allometric scaling, which results in
separate values ranging from 2-12 when extrapolating from various species due to differences in body surface
area/volume ratios based on BW2/3 (Davidson et al., 1986 [46]; Mordenti and Chappell, 1989 [47]). The FDA
guidance document on setting doses in initial clinical trials includes specific species extrapolation factors (FDA,
2005 [48]). The US EPA recommends use of BW3/4 as more appropriate for allometric scaling (EPA, 2005 [49]),
corresponds to a range of values from 2 to 7 for the common species used in toxicology studies.
b.
Intraspecies Differences (Interindividual Variability) (AFH or F2): This factor reflects the variability that exists in
the subpopulation of interest (i.e., the most susceptible subgroup) compared to the study population from which
the NOAEL was derived in terms of systemic exposure for a given dose (kinetics) and how the body responds
(dynamics). These may be considered as what the body does to the chemical and what the chemical does to
the body, respectively. The factor for interindividual variability is used to adjust NOAELs derived from animal
and human data. In the case of human data, it may be the only adjustment factor that is required. A default
adjustment factor of 10 is typically used in the absence of chemical-specific data (see this section for further
information) (Dourson et al., 1996 [50]).
c.
Study Duration (AFS or F3) – When the duration of the study used to identify the critical effect is different from
the actual exposure scenario, an additional adjustment needs to be made. Reviews of a large number of high
quality toxicology studies suggest that a factor of 3 is sufficient to account for the possibility that a lower NOAEL
would have resulted if longer term studies were conducted (Weil and McCollister, 1963 [51]; McNamara, 1976
[52]; Lewis et al., 1990 [53]; Lewis and Nessel, 1994 [54]; Kadry et al., 1995 [55]). It should be noted that
upward adjustments (i.e., AFS < 1) of the dose may be appropriate when longer duration studies are used to
set acceptable doses for short-term exposures. When shorter duration human studies are used as the basis
for deriving the ADE, it may not be necessary to adjust for study duration if long-term animal studies show
little concern for chronic exposure. In addition to the augmentation by long-term animal studies, it may not be
necessary to adjust for short-term human studies if no exacerbation of the critical effect would be anticipated
with longer term human exposure/studies (e.g., for a compound that decreases glucose levels). The exposure
scenario for the patient receiving the API as a contaminant in another drug should also be considered, e.g., 90-
day mail order supply from the same batch.
d.
LOAEL to NOAEL Extrapolation (AFL or F5): The Point-of-Departure (PoD) is frequently the low clinical dose; however,
information on pharmacological or toxicological effects below this dose may not be available (e.g., with generic drugs).
When only a LOAEL is available from a key study used to derive the ADE, it may be possible to calculate a benchmark
dose (see above) to extrapolate an existing dose-response curve downward or, in absence of better data, to apply a
factor of 3 to extrapolate from a LOAEL to a NOAEL. This is a best estimate and is derived from analyses of a large
number of quality toxicology studies that, due the dose selection decisions in well conducted animal studies, the ratio
of the LOAEL:NOAEL rarely exceeds this value (Weil, 1972 [56]; Lewis, 1993 [57]; Abdel-Rahman, 1995 [58]).
e.
Database Completeness (AFD): An additional adjustment factor may be required if the overall toxicity database
is incomplete, e.g., if developmental and reproductive toxicity studies have not yet been performed. The EMA
guide also makes reference to the possible need for additional “modifying factors” to account for missing data,
and specifically mention the lack of data on reproductive and developmental toxicity. This factor is intended to
account for the possibility of finding a lower NOAEL once these studies are performed. Available data suggest a
factor of 3 for this purpose (Dourson et al., 1996 [50]).
f.
Modifying Factor (MF or F4 (Severity)): A modifying factor may also be considered if there is a need to address
residual uncertainties not covered by the other factors. It may also be used to account for the severity of effect
or the slope of the dose-response curve. These are covered in adjustment factors F4 and F5 in the EMA guide,
although it does mention the need for additional modifying factors to address residual uncertainties, e.g., missing
reproductive and developmental toxicity data (Note: that this overlaps with AFD so care needs to be taken not
to double count). The modifying factor also allows a way for professional judgment to be applied to address the
overall quality of the database and relevance of available studies to human risk assessment. MFs of < 1 to 10
may be used in these cases (Dourson et al., 1996 [50]).

=== PAGE 47 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
Pharmacokinetic Adjustments (PK): In certain cases, route-to-route extrapolation may be appropriate when
attempting to derive an ADE from a study conducted by a route (e.g., oral) that is different from the potential route
of exposure (e.g., parenteral or inhalation). This need for adjustment is specifically cited in the EMA guide. Although
direct studies using the relevant route of administration are normally expected, extrapolation using an additional
adjustment factor (e.g., bioavailability correction factor often designated as α) may be useful to ensure adequate
protection in some situations. An example is an oral drug that has an oral bioavailability of 50%, with an ADE based
on oral data, being used to derive cleaning limits for a parenteral drug (Product B) where 100% bioavailability is
assumed following administration. In this case, the ADE value would need to be adjusted downward by a factor
of 2 to align the corresponding plasma levels following oral and parenteral exposure and to adjust the ADE value
appropriately. One scenario where this may be required is a chemical manufacturing operation where APIs for oral
and parenteral products are synthesized in the same equipment. Similar adjustments may be necessary when setting
occupational exposure limits (see Section 5.4). For more background on bioavailability correction factors and when
they should be applied, see Naumann and Weideman, 1995 [39]; Pfister et al., 2014 [59].
The ADE value is intended to be protective for all routes of patient exposure. In certain manufacturing scenarios (e.g.,
oral solid dosage form manufacturing), only one route of patient exposure is a concern. In these cases, the ADE
value could be adjusted, but to avoid confusion, this should be done at a later step in the risk assessment process.
Bercu et al., 2013b [60] discuss setting product specific ADE values when information about the subsequent product
is known. If a company chooses to use route or product specific ADEs, they must have a robust method to manage
the information so that when using an ADE value in a different scenario it is clear if the ADE has already been
adjusted for specific scenario(s). Adjustments to acceptance limits may also be needed if the margin of safety is not
large enough for the subpopulation of concern (e.g., pediatric patients).
Additional factor(s) may be needed to adjust health-based limits such as ADE values because of the potential for
bioaccumulation with repeated exposure. For compounds with long elimination half-times, plasma levels increase
with continuous exposure until they reach steady state.
The ADE established using a NOAEL from a short duration study would need to be adjusted downward by a factor
(often designated as S) corresponding to the ratio of the plasma level following a short-term exposure to the expected
plasma level at steady state following long-term exposure. This adjustment is not necessary if the ADE is established
using studies of sufficient length, where the steady state at the maintenance dose is known, since any accumulation
has already been taken into account in this situation.
The adjustment factors described above represent current methodologies that are supported by scientific evidence.
When non-cancer risk assessment methods were being developed (Lehman and Fitzhugh, 1954 [61]; Dourson and
Stara, 1983 [62]), factors of 10 each were considered protective with a high degree of confidence. Despite the fact
that more recent assessments have shown that lower values are still adequately protective, default values of 10 are
sometimes used to set health-based limits. The fact that they are overprotective compared to science-based limits is
not problematic from a health standpoint if the overall goal is to provide protection. However, this practice may lead to
the over-design of manufacturing processes and increased manufacturing costs, with no material benefit to the patient.

##### 5.3.5.2 Use of Chemical-Specific Adjustment Factors for Differences in Pharmacokinetics and Pharmacodynamics

During new product development, a significant amount of data on pharmacokinetics and pharmacodynamics are
generated. These data can be analyzed to provide insights and to quantify interindividual and interspecies differences
in kinetics and dynamics (Silverman et al., 1999 [41]; Naumann et al., 2001 [63]; Sargent et al., 2002 [64]). The use of
Chemical-Specific Adjustment Factors (CSAFs) is particularly important for assessment and protection of susceptible
subgroups, e.g., children, the elderly, or individuals with liver or kidney dysfunction. CSAFs can be derived by dividing
a value two standard deviations above the mean by the mean itself for relevant kinetic and/or dynamic parameters.
Meek et al., 2001 [65] recommended guidelines for proper application of CSAFs in risk assessment. CSAFs are
intended to replace the default factors where sufficient pharmacokinetic and pharmacodynamic data are available
according to the scheme proposed by Renwick (1993, 1999 [66, 67]) and adopted by the World Health Organization/
International Programme on Chemical Safety (WHO/IPCS, 2001, 2005 [68, 35]). Default uncertainty factors, by
definition, should be used only in the absence of relevant chemical-specific data.

=== PAGE 48 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC

##### 5.3.5.3 Derivation of ADEs for Compounds with Limited Data

Dolan et al., 2005 [69] published a paper with recommendations on how the “Threshold of Toxicological Concern”
(TTC) concept could be applied to relatively unstudied compounds encountered in the pharmaceutical industry. It
builds on the approach used by the FDA (termed “Threshold of Regulation”) to determine if new food additives would
require testing before being approved (FDA, 1995; Munro et al., 1996a [71]; Kroes et al., 2000, 2004 [72, 73]). ICH
M7 [7] is also based on this concept as it applies to mutagenic impurities (see below). Bridging from large databases
of well-studied compounds, it was concluded that a daily dose of 1.5 µg of a new food additive consumed in the
diet, was likely to carry no more than a de minimis level of risk. Munro et al., 1996b [74] compiled data for various
endpoints and structural classes and created distributions of thresholds of acceptable human exposure derived by
applying a 100-fold safety factor to the NOAELs.
Dolan et al., 2005 [69] expanded this concept to include three categories of compounds with limited or no toxicity data:
1.
Compounds that are likely to be carcinogenic
2.
Compounds that are likely to be potent or toxic
3.
Compounds that are not likely to be potent, toxic, or carcinogenic
The corresponding ADEs recommended for these three categories are 1, 10, and 100 µg/day, respectively.
These ADEs are intended to be applied when justification is needed to support cleaning validation or extraneous
matter evaluations for compounds with limited or no information. The same estimated ADEs can be used for early
development compounds based on their limited data sets. The TTC is also being suggested for application to
food allergens (Hourihane, 2001 [75]; Flinterman et al., 2006 [76]), skin sensitizers (Keller et al., 2009 [77]), and
reproductive/developmental toxicants (Stanard et al., 2015 [78]).
The use of the TTC approach for new compounds with limited available safety data represents a strategy to err on the
side of caution that applies a tighter control than may be required once more data become available. That way, any
subsequent discovery that new compounds require a lower ADE will already have been addressed. The temporary
use of the TTC concept to support preliminary ADE values should be an interim strategy until a formal ADE value can
be established.

##### 5.3.5.4 Extrapolation to an Acceptable Level of Risk for Genotoxic Compounds

For certain categories of compounds (i.e., direct acting genotoxic compounds), the dose-response curve is assumed
to be linear at low dosages and the assumption is that there is no threshold below which adverse effects would not be
expected. This is based on the theory that a single change in DNA could lead to a mutation and possibly cancer. For
this endpoint, a different approach to the “safety factor” method described above should be used.
It should be noted that the risk-based, linear extrapolation methods described below do not need to be applied to
all compounds with positive genotoxicity data. For example, there are cases where the mechanism of action does
not clearly indicate the compound causes direct DNA damage, and in vivo carcinogenicity studies are negative. This
may also be true for aneugens (compounds that affect the number of chromosomes). In this case, the in vivo data
should take precedence over the in vitro or in vivo genotoxicity assays which, typically, are performed at very high
concentrations or doses. The application of adjustment factors to a NOAEL or BMDL10 from the in vivo carcinogenicity
study would be appropriate.

=== PAGE 49 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
With an alternative risk-based method, it is possible to establish acceptance criteria for genotoxic compounds
based on extrapolation to a dose that corresponds to a de minimis level of risk. Use of linear extrapolation methods
is a policy decision and should be based on validated methods. The EMA, 2006 [79] and Muller et al., 2006 [80]
recommendations on setting acceptable levels for genotoxic impurities in pharmaceutical products assume that 1
cancer case per 100,000 is sufficiently close to background to be considered acceptable, based on a patient risk/
benefit analysis. Using this assumption, a daily dose of 1.5 µg/day for lifetime exposure to a genotoxic impurity, would
be permitted in a new pharmaceutical product that will be chronically administered if it cannot be avoided. Higher
levels are proposed for shorter duration exposure, e.g., up to 120 µg/day for APIs used in clinical trials lasting less
than one month. As discussed above, ICH M7 [7] has adopted this approach for mutagenic impurities. Note that
the 1.5 µg/day value is essentially the same as the 1 µg/day value recommended by Dolan et al., (2005) [69] for
genotoxic compounds that are likely to be carcinogenic.
BMD software (see Section 5.3.4) can be used to model tumor data to derive a Point-of-Departure (PoD) (e.g.,
BMDL10), from which it is possible to extrapolate to an acceptable level of risk (e.g., 1 in 100,000). Fiori and
Meyerhoff, 2002 [81] have also made recommendations for deriving acceptable levels for genotoxic compounds,
including making adjustments for less than lifetime exposure. Bercu et al., 2013b [60] and Callis et al., 2010 [82] have
also provided guidance on how to apply the TTC concept to address special exposure scenarios involving genotoxic
compounds.

##### 5.3.5.5 Setting Acceptable Levels of Exposure for Sensitizers

The term “highly sensitizing compounds” is used in a number of GMP guidelines, usually within the context of product
segregation or dedicated facilities for penicillin and other beta-lactam antibiotics. This section provides a rationale
supporting the manufacture of sensitizers, excluding beta-lactam antibiotics, in a multiproduct manufacturing
facility.
Sensitization should be included in the toxicological endpoints considered when setting an ADE value. For some
types of sensitizers (e.g., dermal sensitizers), it is possible to define a non-sensitizing dose and this NOAEL could
be used to establish an ADE value using the “safety factor” approach, e.g., for topical products. For other types of
sensitization that are more systemic in nature (e.g., respiratory sensitizers and drug allergy), where a NOAEL is not
easily identified, alternative risk-based approaches to establishing a safe level of exposure should be used.
The underlying mechanisms of sensitization reactions, which are immunologically-based responses, are generally
well understood and it is known that there is a dose-response for both induction and elicitation of allergic reactions.
While thresholds are believed to exist for both induction and elicitation, these can be very low, and the challenge is
defining a level of exposure that is not expected to elicit a reaction in the most sensitive individuals.
Dermal and Respiratory Sensitizers
Dermal sensitization is a cell-mediated (Type IV) delayed hypersensitivity response that requires a compound to be
absorbed through the skin and to interact with specialized immune cells (e.g., Langerhans cells and T-lymphocytes)
in the skin and associated lymph nodes. Like humoral immunity, due to memory in T-cells (in this case), an allergic
reaction may result upon subsequent exposure following initial induction of a sensitization response. Data from
Human Repeat Insult Patch Testing (HRIPT) and the murine Local Lymph Node Assay (LLNA) can be used to assess
the potency of dermal sensitizers.
These data also are used to perform risk assessments demonstrating the safety of topically applied pharmaceutical
and cosmetic products. A similar approach can be used to define levels of surface contamination that are unlikely to
induce dermal sensitization.

=== PAGE 50 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
Respiratory hypersensitivity is an immune-mediated humoral allergic response. In this case, it is described as an
IgE-mediated Type I immediate hypersensitivity response, and involves macrophages and B-lymphocytes. Following
induction of sensitization, with generation of specific B-cell memory and antibody generating capability, subsequent
exposure can elicit a response that involves histamine release by mast cells and other accessory cells culminating in
allergic responses that may include bronchoconstriction, angioedema, urticaria, and possibly anaphylaxis and death.
Induction of respiratory hypersensitivity can result from dermal exposure, as in the case of small molecular weight
chemicals such as 2,4-toluenediisocyanate, but also from inhalation or parenteral administration of large molecular
weight compounds such as proteolytic enzymes. Compared to skin sensitizers, it is more difficult to set a safe level
of exposure to prevent induction of respiratory sensitization. There are no validated animal tests available for this
endpoint; however, several models are in development. Epidemiological studies that provide data on antibody titers,
pulmonary function measurements (e.g., FEV1), bronchial challenge, and prevalence of symptoms are required to set
safe levels of exposure.
Beta-lactam Antibiotics
The FDA and other agencies, such as EMA, PIC/s and ANVISA, expect that dedicated facilities are used for the
manufacturing and processing of beta-lactam antibiotics. The prohibition of manufacturing penicillin in the same
facility with other products (even other beta-lactam antibiotics) is based on the concern that trace quantities of
penicillin can potentially cause a serious anaphylactic reaction, and possibly death, in penicillin sensitive patients.
Due to the severity of these effects, regulators consider the risks associated with exposure of sensitive individuals as
a result of human error and other undetected operations or design flaws in a multiuse manufacturing environment, to
be too severe to allow the production of beta-lactam products in such facilities. Notwithstanding, ADE values should
be developed for beta-lactams that, in certain jurisdictions, are allowed to be co-manufactured with other beta-
lactams and for assessing the risk of beta-lactams to other compounds when processed on a multiproduct site.

### 5.4 Setting Occupational Exposure Limits

The primary purpose of this Chapter is to describe methods to set ADE values for patient protection; however, similar
quantitative health-based assessments are performed to set OELs to protect workers handling these substances.
The safe level of exposure for patients may be different from that established for workers. This is because the
critical effect used to derive the limits may be different due to differences in the anticipated route of exposure, and
even where this is the same, the choice of adjustment factors may differ. Workers are generally healthy individuals,
whereas patients may be children or elderly, and by definition, have at least one active medical condition.
Adjustments may be necessary to address route-to-route extrapolation, even if the same critical effect is used. For
example, an OEL derived from the ADE may need to be adjusted for differences in bioavailability between the route
used to identify the critical effect (e.g., oral) and the anticipated route of exposure (e.g., inhalation) (Naumann and
Weidmeman, 1995 [39]; Naumann et al., 2009 [83]; Pfister et al. [59], 2014).
If the same assumptions apply regarding the critical effect, susceptible subpopulations, and bioavailability (e.g.,
100%), an OEL can be calculated directly from the ADE by dividing the dose allowed by the volume of air breathed by
a worker, typically 10 m3, engaged in light work for 8 hours. Likewise, surface target values (e.g., wipe limits), which
are used for worker protection, can also be derived by dividing the dose permitted in 8 hours at the OEL (i.e., OEL ×
10 m3) by a standard surface area (e.g., 100 cm2), but this is often viewed as a convenient, if not arbitrary, approach
for assessing exterior equipment and facility surfaces for worker protection purposes. They are perhaps best used to
evaluate whether containment equipment is preventing contamination of the workspace. The same caveats for OELs
apply to wipe limits with regard to the appropriateness of the direct use of the ADE. While it may be tempting to use
wipe limits to assess the potential for cross-contamination, it is important to emphasize that they are developed for
worker safety purposes. Any other use must be done within the context of a risk assessment for patient safety (cross-
contamination) when considering specific plausible pathways of exposure to product.

=== PAGE 51 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC

### 5.5 Documentation of the Health-Based Exposure Limit

The scientific rationale for the recommended health-based limit (e.g., ADE) should be summarized in a formal written
document. The document should include a summary to facilitate review by stakeholders and the basis for the ADE
should be clearly described. The reader should be able to readily determine the health endpoint (critical effect)
on which the ADE was established, understand the values chosen for adjustment factors, and which sources of
variability and uncertainty they address. In addition, any further adjustments (e.g., bioavailability correction) must be
clearly explained.
The format of the documentation of the ADE does not need to be standardized; however, it should be used
consistently within a given company. The documentation should include sections, regardless of designation,
addressing the following:
1.
Identity information, physico-chemical properties and chemical structure
2.
Intended use and mechanism of action
3.
Pharmacokinetics and pharmacodynamics in animals and humans
4.
Animal data including acute toxicity, local effects, repeat dose toxicity, developmental and reproductive toxicity,
genotoxicity, carcinogenicity
5.
Veterinary use if applicable
6.
Human data including clinical use, adverse reactions, susceptible subpopulations, pregnancy and nursing
mothers
7.
Identification of the critical effect
8.
References
9.
Qualifications of the expert author (e.g., toxicologist)
10. Appendices that provide further details, e.g., equations showing the calculation of the ADE value, chemical-
