specific adjustment factors, etc. The level of detail in the documentation should be commensurate with the level
of hazard and risk associated with the specific compound.

=== PAGE 52 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

=== PAGE 53 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
6	 Risk Analysis
ICH Q9 [1] defines risk analysis (see Figure 6.1) as the estimation of the risk associated with the identified hazards.
As the hazard is inherent to the API and other process chemicals, the exposure to the hazard is the variable that
needs to be analyzed to determine the relative risks. This Chapter describes risk analysis, which is closely related
to exposure assessment, i.e., the process by which exposures are assessed, either qualitatively or quantitatively, to
estimate the risk associated with the identified hazards. The risk analysis looks at the modes of exposure such as
mix-up, retention, mechanical transfer, and airborne transfer for the product, as well as inhalation, dermal, ingestion,
and mechanical transfer for workers, while considering the question “What could go wrong?”. The identified hazard
also should be analyzed in order to answer the questions:
•
“What is the likelihood (probability) it will go wrong?”
•
“What are the consequences (severity)?”
•
“Can the failure be detected?”

*[Figure 6.1: Risk Analysis]*

=== PAGE 54 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 6.1 Holistic Approach to Risk Analysis

While the focus of ICH Q9 [1] is understood, appropriate risk analysis should recognize that the production of
medicines requires that a number of potentially conflicting requirements need to be met, which may impact each
other, e.g., cross-contamination (focus on patient safety), IH (focus on worker safety), process safety, or production
demands. Therefore, effective risk analysis requires a shared understanding to achieve the balance between these
different requirements.
A multidisciplinary approach is beneficial when drawing on expertise from several specialist areas and provides a
holistic and balanced approach. This should include personnel who are knowledgeable about the material hazards
and the products/processes being reviewed, as well as personnel who are well placed to challenge and question the
processes. This group may include:
•
Toxicologists
•
Industrial hygienists
•
Engineers
•
Quality managers
•
Users or operating personnel
•
Independent facilitators
This approach and the correct application of risk management principles may be beneficial in achieving an acceptable
balance.
Although GxP and IH exposure assessments are linked, the application and interpretation of the criteria in the
assessment process are different.
It should be noted that more hazardous compounds will not necessarily lead to higher potential for exposure and
that the risks of exposure with more hazardous compounds are not always more difficult to manage. Some reasons
that more hazardous compounds are not necessarily high risk of cross-contamination is that, typically, higher hazard
compounds tend to be a smaller percentage of the final product and are processed in more closed systems than
lower hazard products. It is acknowledged that the impact of a failure that leads to cross-contamination of a product
with a high hazard compound can be more severe and, therefore, the controls to manage high hazard compounds
tend to be more robust. It should also be noted that the exposure potential may not be the same for manufacture
of the same compound and the same process in a different location as there are other variables such as worker
behavior that also influence the exposure profile.

### 6.2 Tools

Qualitative exposure assessments are used to provide an initial estimate of exposure and to prioritize quantitative
exposure assessment activities. Based on the results of the qualitative exposure assessment it may be necessary
to perform a quantitative exposure assessment involving the collection of data. These data can be compared to
the acceptable levels. The exposure assessments conducted to evaluate the potential for cross-contamination and
worker safety are performed differently; however, in both cases, it is important to identify the most plausible pathways
that could lead to exposure.

=== PAGE 55 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Fundamentally, risk analysis should incorporate both qualitative and quantitative processes to link the likelihood of
occurrence with the severity of harm. The tools should be applied in a manner that is commensurate with the risks.
(For further information, see Chapter 11). In order to have a robust assessment of the risks it is important to use
actual data from the facility such as incident reports, cleaning and cleaning validation results, results from protocol
studies, regulatory action, etc. to assist in categorizing the risk being assessed. Independent of the tools that are
used, the review process and conclusions regarding risk should be clearly documented and communicated.
As there are many steps involved with synthesizing drug substances or manufacturing drug products, it is important
to define what will be analyzed without complicating the process. Typically, the analysis will focus on a defined part
of the process, e.g., charging a vessel or a particular unit operation such as compression. Limiting the focus causes
the conclusions and actions to be specific to a defined task. The entire process should be reviewed (including
unexpected operation situations such as an upset condition such as breach of containment, or a spill) in order to
demonstrate that the process is under control and the potential for cross-contamination is below acceptable levels.

#### 6.2.1 Qualitative Risk Analysis and Prioritization

Qualitative risk analysis is a process that can be used to assess the risk of exposure and prioritize the subsequent
quantitative activities. The results of the qualitative technique will help to determine where to focus available
resources. The output of prioritized qualitative analysis is not necessarily quantitative activities, but possibly further
and more detailed qualitative activities or reference to other systems. There are many different formats and schemes
used to conduct and document these analyses. These formats include, but are not limited to:
•
Flowcharts
•
Check sheets
•
Process mapping
•
Risk ranking and filtering
•
Cause and effect diagrams
A robust qualitative risk analysis process should incorporate the following elements:
•
Hazard characterization using the available health hazard information (e.g., ADEs, OELs, or TLV®s) for the
substances being handled
•
Physical characteristics of the pharmaceutical compound
•
Definition of the unit operations to be assessed including cleaning activities
•
Observation of the operations and discussions with personnel, maintenance personnel, and engineering
•
Review similar existing unit operations, input from vendors, benchmarking data, or use a methodology that
involves collection of key process pharmaceutical product information
•
Exposure profile including identification of plausible pathways and possible sources of exposure, specific activity,
processing equipment, or amount of energy imparted on the activity
•
Checks that existing control measures are functioning properly
The assessment team should use professional judgment to understand the risks, regulations, frequency of exposure,
and number of workers/processes to determine the need for additional analysis and their priority.

=== PAGE 56 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

#### 6.2.2 Quantitative Risk Analysis

Based on the results of the qualitative risk analysis, it may be necessary to perform a quantitative risk analysis
involving the collection and evaluation of data. The quantitative analysis process provides an objective and empirical
basis regarding risk potential. In evaluating exposure, it may be appropriate to collect samples to understand the
sources of exposure or if there is migration of pharmaceutical product from one area to another. It may be necessary
to apply appropriate statistical analysis to multiple point data sets as might be derived from airborne and surface
residue monitoring activities.
Many computer-based statistical packages provide tools to evaluate data such as cleaning residues against an
upper limit such as the swab residue level. Calculations such as the Process Capability Index (Cpk) or the Process
Performance Index (Ppk) can be used to evaluate and graph these data against these limits. For cleaning data, which
is typically non-normally distributed, the Process Performance Index is a preferred technique. These indices can be
used as a quantitative measure of the level of risk to a patient posed by the residues of a product.
After reviewing the risk analysis, equipment/process steps identified as high risk should be complemented by defined
controls which incorporate a statistically-based sampling strategy. The required number of samples can be based on
statistical tools, such as the “power test,” using an upper boundary based on the ADE value for the anticipated levels
found during cleaning process development. If the difference between these values is high, then very few samples
may be required to demonstrate effectiveness and these can be limited to only “difficult to clean” areas.

##### 6.2.2.1 Air Sampling

Workplace air sampling is normally conducted by industrial hygienists as part of an ongoing monitoring program to
ensure worker health protection. In this context, such air sampling typically takes place where there is a potential for
workers to be exposed to the material or product within a room or facility.
Similar air sampling techniques also may be useful as part of an initial risk assessment exercise for Quality
Assurance purposes to help demonstrate that material or product is being adequately contained within a process,
room, or facility. In this case, a monitoring plan should be designed specifically for quality purposes. As part of this
monitoring plan, the monitoring equipment should be situated outside of the room or facility to detect if, or at what
levels, the material or product escapes.
There is no regulatory requirement to perform such air sampling as part of GxP, and containment within a room
or facility routinely should be proven through qualified and measurable physical parameters, such as pressure
differentials and airflow volumes and direction. If, however, an organization decides to use such air sampling
techniques as part of a risk analysis exercise, the following should be considered in the monitoring plan:
•
Pre-agreed acceptance criteria for the air sampling program that are scientifically set at a level with cross-
contamination and patient risk considered.
•
The levels of material or powder outside of a well-designed facility are anticipated to be very low, so it may be
necessary to sample larger volumes of air than normally sampled as part of the worker protection program.
It is important that the Quality Department and industrial hygienists communicate closely and that all air sampling
activities be thoroughly documented.

### 6.3 Routes for Cross-Contamination

The routes for cross-contamination within pharmaceutical processing activities are presented in order of importance.

*[Figure 6.2 contrasts the risk of cross-contamination and worker exposure risk.]*

ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 57 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

*[Figure 6.2: Risk of Cross-Contamination and Worker Exposure Risk]*

#### 6.3.1 Mix-up

Mix-up is frequently cited by regulators as one of the most common causes for regulatory action/product recall.
For the purpose of this Guide, mix-up refers to the contamination at unsafe levels of one product with another. The
highest risks from mix-up normally stem from low-tech GxP failures due to basic human error (e.g., failure to follow
procedure) or system weakness (e.g., poor material labeling).
Obviously, there is an increased potential risk of cross-contamination if two or more products are being handled in the
same area, even with appropriate batch to batch segregation.
The risk analysis to identify potential cross-contamination routes should include a review of basic GxP to confirm that
appropriate systems provide a high level of assurance that mix-up will not occur, such as:
•
Identification and status labeling/controls of materials and products
•
Status labeling/controls of equipment and facilities
•
Receipt and controls of materials and products
•
Physical segregation, process flow, and security during material and product handling, storage, and staging

=== PAGE 58 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Typical examples of mix-up may include, but are not limited to:
•
Inadequate line clearance between products
•
The accidental use of dirty equipment
•
Introduction of another product during sampling
•
Incorporation of the wrong starting material or excipient
•
Mislabeling of equipment and/or materials
•
Unintended transfer of materials or product from one vessel to another containing different product/materials
Human error related factors can result from issues such as:
•
Operator training and understanding of process requirements
•
Ergonomic requirements of activities against operator capabilities
•
Local environment (lights, noise, and heat)
•
Operator workload (too busy, bored)
•
Level of routine involved in an activity
•
Local culture, e.g., is procedural innovation discouraged?
•
Overly complex procedures
•
Ease of error, e.g., a function of design, can an error be made and how easily?

##### 6.3.1.1 Factors Affecting Exposure by Mix-up

The presence of multiple processes within a common facility with shared or overlapping process flows may give
rise to an increased risk of mix-up between products through the increased potential for mistakes in materials
management processes.
Factors to be considered when assessing the risk of mix-up within the process include:
•
Overlapping process flows and transit routes
•
Common dispensary areas
•
Common storage areas for change parts

#### 6.3.2 Retention

Retention is defined as residual material on product contact surfaces after cleaning that can carryover from one
product to another product in the same equipment, having been used in a sequential or campaign manner. Retention
is the mode of cross-contamination directly addressed by the cleaning program which includes cleaning validation.
The factors affecting retention relate to the effectiveness of the cleaning procedure and its application by operating
personnel following completion of a campaign. Development and validation of cleaning procedures that consistently
reduce residuals, both intrinsic (product/process materials) and non-intrinsic (such as cleaning agents) on product
contact equipment to acceptable levels is critical to the success of a cross-contamination control program.

=== PAGE 59 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 6.3.2.1 Setting Health-Based Acceptance Limits for Cleaning

Cleaning validation programs require the establishment of acceptance limits. Basing the acceptance criteria on a
health-based limit such as the ADE (or PDE) value has many advantages over traditional methods (e.g., 1/1000th of
the low clinical dose). The most important is the fact that the ADE value is toxicologically derived. Using a health-
based limit such as the ADE value has the benefit that it is based on data generated for a regulatory filing, is readily
available in the package insert, or was generated by commissioned or published studies. When adequate data
are available, all appropriate adjustment factors have already been applied in deriving the ADE value. The ADE
value is used in calculating an appropriately set “safe level” for determining cleaning residues. From an operational
standpoint, the residue value determined from the ADE calculation permits estimation of the true “Margin of Safety”
when evaluating cleaning residue data.
The reproducibility of cleaning processes should be evaluated against the acceptance criteria derived from a health-
based limit (ADE) to determine the level of risk presented to the patient. Evaluation of the cleaning process and
cleaning data, including cleaning validation data, to ensure that any residuals after cleaning are as low as possible
below the health-based criteria and the risk of cross-contamination is minimized. It should be emphasized that the
health-based limit from the ADE value should not be seen as a limit in the true sense, but as a reference point for
determining the level of risk presented by the residue data. For example residue data that is far from the health-based
acceptance criteria would be considered low risk for cleaning failures whereas residue data that is just below the
acceptance criteria would have a higher risk of cleaning failures.
The acceptance criteria (e.g., swab residue level) determined by the ADE may actually permit the equipment to look
dirty which is obviously unacceptable since equipment surfaces must be visually clean so therefore the acceptance
criteria would be set to visually clean.
At the completion of cleaning process development studies, or for more established processes with historical data,
statistically-based “Process Control Limits” should be established. Much like limits set for temperature, humidity, etc.,
the inner control limits (or alert levels and/or action levels) should be tighter than the out of specification limits so that
when limits are exceeded, the product is not necessarily rejected, but rather there is an indication that the process
may be out of control or needs attention. Refer to Section 6.3.2.6 for more information.
The basic calculations for cleaning validation swab and rinse samples are given below:
Calculating ADE derived residue level, and subsequently capturing and trending residue data against the ADE value,
can assist in quantifiably determining the product cleaning profile, as either suitable for manufacture within a multiuse
production facility or requiring dedicated equipment and spatial separation.
Calculating residue levels for swab and rinse samples would follow the typical calculations currently used, i.e.:
Calculation of Health-Based Cleaning Limits
ADE × SB × TA x RF
Swab Residue Level (mg/swab) =	 ___________________
MDD × SSA
ADE × SB × RA x RF
Rinse Residue Level (mg/L) =	___________________
V × MDD × SSA
Where:	 ADE = Acceptable Daily Exposure (mg/day)
MDD = Maximum Daily Dose (mg/day)
RA = Rinsed Area (cm2)
RF = Recovery Factor2
ADV TOPIC
2	 The RF is either factored into the acceptance criteria as shown in the formulas or is used to adjust the analytical results in which case the RF would
not be included in the acceptance criteria calculation.

=== PAGE 60 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Where:	 SB = Smallest batch size (mg)
SSA = Shared Surface Area (cm2)
TA = Test Area (cm2)
V = Volume of Rinse (L)
Comparison of residue data from a cleaning database to product residue levels, based on ADEs, provides a high
degree of assurance that compound residues or related degradant levels on equipment product contact surfaces
pose no significant risk to patients. A very similar procedure can be used for assessing microbial risk.

##### 6.3.2.2 Inactivation of API during Cleaning and Sterilization

A limitation of the application of the ADE methodology is that it is based on the assumption that the product is active
after cleaning and sterilization. This has important implications for biopharmaceutical manufacturing because the API
is often inactivated during these operations (Sharnez et al., 2013 [88]; Cleaning Validation Guidelines [89]; Kendrick
et al. 2009 [90]). Biopharmaceutical cleaning cycles are generally designed to expose product contact equipment to
extremes of pH (< 2 and > 13) and temperature (60 to 80°C) for several minutes. Under these conditions monoclonal
antibodies, therapeutic proteins, and other biopharmaceuticals are known to degrade and denature rapidly, and
are therefore likely to become pharmacologically inactive. Some small molecules can also be inactivated and this
approach would apply to these as well. The product inactivation approach should therefore be considered when
assessing carryover of products that are likely to be inactivated during cleaning and sterilization.
If the API degrades into pharmacologically inactive fragments, the acceptance limit for the process residue should
be set based on the inactive fragments instead of the active ingredient (Sharnez et al., 2013 [88]). This is consistent
with the expectation that the carryover of an impurity, in this case the inactive fragments and aggregates, into the
subsequently manufactured product should be justified from the standpoint of the safety and efficacy of the product.
This requires studies to determine if the product can effectively be degraded and denatured into inactive fragments.
To determine the effectiveness of the degrading and denaturing processes the ADE is necessary to ensure that the
API is not present in unsafe levels after degrading/denaturing. Approaches for setting safety-based acceptance limits
for the inactive fragments include using an acceptable limit for a reference impurity where the reference impurity is
shown to be comparable to or worse than the inactive fragments.
If residual API is found after the degrading/denaturing process, the acceptance limit should be determined based on
the ADE value of the API.
Note: that if the acceptance limit for the process residue associated with an API cannot be met, or if it is less than the
Limit of Quantitation (LOQ) of the analytical method, the product contact equipment for that API should be dedicated.

##### 6.3.2.3 Use of 1/1000th of the Lowest Clinical Dose and 10 ppm Specification

The use of traditional acceptance limits that rely on a single data point (e.g., the lowest clinical dose) is not the
preferred strategy. Ideally, compound specific, scientifically derived, health-based values should be established
whenever possible using all available data. Alternative approaches are available for compounds with limited or no data
(see TTC discussion above), but should be considered with caution. Lower than necessary cleaning acceptance limits
increase the risk of a cleaning failure. It is expected that companies will have robust cleaning processes that provide as
large a safety margin as possible especially where existing data indicates the ability to clean to very low residue levels.
An example is when the allowable residue level is calculated by dividing the lowest clinical dose by 1000, as
recommended by Fourman and Mullen, 1993 [84]. For many APIs, this approach yields a value that is overprotective
compared to the science-based ADE. The unintended result is a lowered apparent margin of safety when comparing
to projected or measured levels of exposure. In other words, if the actual compound specific animal and human data
were used, the allowable residue limits would likely be higher and the margin of safety relative to process capability
would also be higher. The use of the ADE-based acceptance criteria in this scenario may also permit companies to
use visual detection only to confirm equipment cleanliness for the next product in routine operations. Use of visually
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 61 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
clean as the sole acceptance criteria during verification at product changeover is only acceptable where there is a
significant margin of safety between the visual threshold and the health based exposure limit.  This is necessary to
account for variability such as encountered when determining visual threshold and application of such by personnel
(see Figure 6.3).
Figure 6.3
Note: this situation does not mean that companies should relax their cleaning processes.
It should be noted that health-based exposure limits such as ADE can result in values where visible residues may be
present, however in all cases visually clean is the minimum standard expected.
Conversely, 1/1000th of the low clinical dose for highly hazardous drugs, such as an antineoplastic agent, with a direct
acting, DNA damaging mechanism of action, may not be sufficiently protective with chronic exposure. For example,
1/1000th of a 500 mg/day dose of an oncology drug is 500 µg/day, more than two orders of magnitude higher than the TTC
level of 1 µg/day, per Dolan et al., 2005 [69] or 1.5 µg/day per ICH M7 [16]. Facilities that rely on this method to calculate
cleaning limits should have a rationale for how adequate protection is being provided for highly hazardous drugs.
The use of median Lethal Dose (LD50) values to derive cleaning limits has been recommended for equipment
cleaning agents (e.g., detergents). A factor of up to 1 × 10-5 has been proposed to extrapolate from an LD50 value
to a “safe” dose by Layton et al., 1987, who evaluated the ratios of chronic NOAEL values and oral LD50 values for
compounds with full data sets. Today, the use of LD50 values is not recommended for risk assessment, even where
this is the only value available. An alternative method using the Thresholds of Toxicological Concern concept is
preferred (see Section 5.3.5.3).
Using the LD50 as the starting point for risk assessment can lead to cleaning limits that are not adequately protective
of health since adverse effects are expected long before death. The oral LD50 for the opioid analgesic morphine
sulfate of 461 mg/kg is used to illustrate this approach. After correcting for body weight (e.g., 50 kg) and multiplying
by 0.00001 (1 × 10-5), the resulting dose is 0.2 mg. This is 50 times below the lowest therapeutic dose for morphine.
An ADE established using the low clinical dose of 10 mg and applying appropriate adjustment factors would be similar
in this case. However, Lovsin-Barle et al. 2012 showed that there is a poor correlation between the LD50 in animals
and the lowest pharmacologically active dose in humans.
Another traditional approach for setting cleaning limits is the use of the “10 ppm” specification. Using this approach,
Product B can contain no more than 10 ppm of Product A on a weight basis, i.e., a 1 gram tablet of Product B can
contain no more than 10 µg of Product A. Such limits are arbitrary and not applicable to all compounds. If used, the
10 ppm specification should be supported by data showing that it provides adequate protection of patients from cross-
contamination. Some drugs, such as highly active sex hormones and direct acting, DNA damaging antineoplastic
agents, have very low ADEs and corresponding swab or rinse limits. In these cases, use of the 10 ppm specification
to derive a swab limit may not provide adequate protection if the residue levels exceed the swab limit that would have
been calculated using a health-based limit, such as the ADE. For a 1000 mg tablet, 10 ppm corresponds to 10 µg/
day. This would not be protective for many highly hazardous drugs.

=== PAGE 62 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
It may be tempting to determine the acceptance criteria as the lowest of the three methodologies (health-based
(ADE), 1/1000th of the low clinical dose, or 10 ppm) but there is no scientific justification for this action. The health-
based limit represents a level that is safe for all patient populations and reducing this further does not increase patient
safety and, as discussed above, actually lowers the apparent margin of safety. The preferred scenario is to use the
health-based limit and implement robust cleaning procedures to keep the risk of cross-contamination by retention low
and demonstrate as large a margin of safety as possible.
Traditional calculations such as 1/1000th of the lowest clinical dose or limits such as 10 ppm have been historically
achievable in most cases. Although zero risk can never be achieved, or is even necessary, contamination at any level
is not desirable and, as such, previous limits should be used as process control limits where these have been shown
to be achievable. As always, the equipment must be visually clean and rinse solutions should be clear and colorless.
The only criteria necessary for a robust cleaning process are the health-based, ADE derived limit, a validated
analytical method with a sensitivity below the acceptance limit, that is visually clean. No quantity of residue is to be
visible on the equipment after cleaning procedures are performed. These criteria satisfy the regulatory requirements,
provide for patient safety, and prevent cross-contamination of the subsequent batch.

##### 6.3.2.4 Cleaning Validation

“Cleaning validation” itself is the act of demonstrating that a given cleaning procedure or process can consistently and
with confidence reduce residuals on product contact equipment surfaces to acceptable levels. This allows the use of
that equipment with other products or materials without undue risk of adverse cross-contamination of those products
or materials. Many have historically considered the Quality Unit post-approval of the 3-run “PQ” or “PV” to “complete”
the validation, and be all that is necessary to have their cleaning processes found to be in a “validated state” upon
regulatory inspection.

*[Figure 6.4: Cleaning Validation in the Product Life Cycle]*

The articulation and industry adoption of life cycle approaches to process validation, and the (re)stated expectations
for process control as its ultimate goal, render such a view as inadequate to achieve full compliance. An analogy
can be drawn between the development, process qualification, and ongoing monitoring of a cleaning process and
the 3-stage FDA Process Validation Lifecycle nomenclature. Although the rigor of process design and the timing of
implementation for some “stages” may differ, the science and risk-based methodology described in the FDA guidance
is equally as applicable to cleaning processes” (see Figure 6.4).
Other aspects of the Process Validation Lifecycle apply as well. By the time cleaning validation runs take place,
there must be sufficient product and process understanding of both the type of, and hazards represented by, residual
materials (both intrinsic and non-intrinsic), as well as the proposed cleaning procedure in terms of parameters
necessary to assure efficacy. The development of such product/process knowledge as necessary and the
foundational risk assessments for the cleaning process are roughly analogous to Stage 1 (“Process Design”) activities
in the product life cycle.

=== PAGE 63 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Following completion and approval of cleaning validation runs, a risk-based program of ongoing monitoring should be
established to assure the cleaning process remains consistent and effective.

##### 6.3.2.5 Cleaning Process Development

Cleaning procedures should be developed to provide the right cleaning agent at the right conditions for effective
cleaning. This requires knowledge of the compound’s “cleanability.” Bench scale studies should be conducted to
assess the properties required to remove a compound using various cleaning agents from the desired surface
materials and finishes. Cleaning processes that have been optimized through the selection of the best cleaning agents
and cleaning parameters offer the greatest ability to uniformly reduce product residues to the lowest risk levels.
Prior to use, cleaning procedures should be subjected to risk assessments to identify and evaluate the risk of failure,
improve them as necessary, and make them more reliable and robust.
Understanding the equipment design, including dismantling equipment appropriately, to support the cleaning and to
ensure hidden residues do not remain is an important aspect of cleaning process development.

##### 6.3.2.6 Cleaning Process Performance Capability

As with overall manufacturing process validation, statistical measures of cleaning process capability should be utilized
where they are both feasible and meaningful. It is possible, but not likely, that there will be sufficient data generated
during Process Design (or “Stage 1”) of a new product/new cleaning process to assess process capability prior to
validation and routine use. However, typically, cleaning residue data are non-normally distributed and cannot be
analyzed by standard Process Capability Index (Cpk) calculations. This is due to the LOD of the methods used that
prevent the collection of data lower than this limit, but allow for the collection of data higher resulting in an apparent
one tailed distribution. Therefore, the data may be fitted against a non-normal distribution (e.g., Weibull, Loglogistic,
and Exponential). The residue data can then be compared to the ADE for the product and a Ppk calculated. Other
options include transforming the data to a normal distribution (e.g., Johnson transformation) or simply evaluating the
non-normal data as normal data (The Six Sigma Handbook [87]). The best approach will be dependent on the data
that are presented.
More commonly however, there is insufficient prevalidation cleaning data, or else such data is not representative
of commercial cleaning process conditions (equipment, scale, etc.), so as to be useful in constructing the science
and data-based case for cleaning procedure efficacy. In most situations, “real-world” shop-floor data must be
collected and analyzed for this purpose. The collection, analysis and reporting of cleaning process output data is the
function and purpose of the two subsequent lifecycle stages, Process Qualification (the qualification of any CIP-type
equipment as well as the cleaning validation runs themselves) and Continued Process Verification (a program of
ongoing and risk-based sampling and monitoring of cleaning results).
It is important that the residue data is as far below the health-based residue level as possible. Comparing these data
to the residue level derived from the ADE provides a measure of the “True Margin of Safety” for the cleaning process.
If risks are high (where the residue data is near or above the health-based residue level), then additional measures,
such as improving the cleaning procedure and/or increasing monitoring frequency, should be pursued and
documented. If risks cannot be reduced to acceptable levels or are considered too difficult to implement, then the
equipment being cleaned should be either dedicated or made disposable.

##### 6.3.2.7 Cleaning Evaluations for New Products

The introduction of subsequent products may be significantly accelerated through the utilization of existing data
if such data captures both performance of the cleaning process developed and the understood nature of any
residuals to be removed. For example, a new product can be evaluated against the existing worst-case product(s)
for acceptable residual limits and its cleanability by comparing similarities of formulation and potency with existing
and validated cleaning procedures for existing products. This approach presupposes that the critical steps and
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 64 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
parameters of the cleaning processes in question have been established, and that direct comparisons of actual data
(solubility, Critical Process Parameters (CPPs), etc.) can be made between existing and new cleaning processes.
Caution must be used in interpreting pass/fail in-process cleaning results for existing (validated) procedures as
quantitative and objective indications of the capability of any new, similar procedures. Only actual data is data. If
suitable analytical results are not available to provide information on the performance of existing cleaning procedures,
new ones must be generated to determine acceptability. In addition, if the new product is considered a new worst-
case based on required residual limits or cleanability, cleaning validation is required for the new product otherwise
validation may not be necessary as the existing worst-case scenario would encompass the new product.
In practice, it is often impractical to evaluate the cleanability of a new product at full scale. Some of the factors
that contribute to this limitation are lack of availability of manufacturing equipment, materials, and process soils for
experimental work; difficulty in reproducing worst-case experimental conditions in large equipment; and the time
and resources required to obtain statistically meaningful results at full scale. These issues can be addressed by
evaluating cleanability at smaller (bench or pilot) scales.
If it can be shown that the process soils associated with the new product are easier to clean than the corresponding
soils of the previously qualified product, the new product can be introduced into the facility without requalifying the
cleaning procedures (Sharnez, Part II, 2008 [91]; Sharnez, Part III, 2008 [92]). This principle is illustrated in Figure
6.5 which compares cleanability data for the new process soil (B) to that of the previously qualified process soil (A). If
the time required to clean B (tB) = tB1 is less than the time required to clean A (tA), i.e. B is easier to clean than A, the
cleaning cycle need not be requalified. Conversely, if tB = tB2 is > tA, i.e., B is a harder to clean than A, revalidation is
warranted.

*[Figure 6.5: Comparison of Cleanability Data]*

In addition to streamlining evaluation of cleanability and performance qualification requirements for multiproduct
equipment, small scale cleaning models can also be used to identify suitable cleaning chemistries (Porter, 2009 [93];
Verghese et al., 2009 [94]) and optimize cleaning parameters and processes (Sharnez, 2010 [95]; Sharnez, Part I,
2008 [96]; Sharnez et al., 2009 [97]; Sharnez et al., 2012 [98]).

##### 6.3.2.8 Evaluation of Cleanability at Small Scale

Several experimental models for the evaluation of cleanability at small scale have been described in the literature
(Sharnez et al., Part I, 2013 [99]; Sharnez et al., Part II, 2015 [100]; Sharnez et al., Part III, slated for publication
[101]). In these models, the rate at which the process residue is removed from the surface, i.e., the kinetics of
cleaning or mass transfer rate is measured under simulated cleaning conditions. These models are designed to
evaluate cleanability in convection mediated cleaning, such as in a laminar falling film in a vessel that is cleaned
in place. Nonetheless, the underlying methodology can be readily adapted to develop small scale models for other
means of cleaning as well, such as sonication, scrubbing (manual cleaning), or fluid impingement.

=== PAGE 65 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
A critical step in the development of experimental models for cleaning processes is to identify and scale down the
hardest to clean (worst-case) location in the equipment. For systems that are cleaned in place, the worst-case
location is typically an area with poor circulation, such as a shadowed or occluded area and for manual cleaning, the
worst-case location is typically an area that is relatively difficult to access, such as the interior of a globe valve.
Note: it is not necessary to simulate the entire cleaning process at small scale; instead, it is sufficient to simulate
the worst-case location from the standpoint of cleanability. If the process residue can be adequately removed from
the worst-case location, it follows that it can also be adequately removed from other locations in the equipment.
Thus, with this approach, the cleaning times obtained at small scale would be indicative of those at full scale,
provided that there is adequate spray coverage at all surfaces that need to be cleaned for clean in place systems,
there is adequate access to all surfaces that need to be cleaned for manual systems, and the worst-case location is
appropriately identified and simulated at small scale. If the full-scale equipment is of a different design or presents
other challenges not reviewed during the small scale study, it is important that all variables, such as hard to clean
areas and dismantling instructions are verified on full scale equipment.
Another important consideration in the development of experimental models for evaluating cleanability is to determine
the worst-case mechanism or means of cleaning. For clean in place systems, the worst-case mechanism of cleaning
is typically through diffusion controlled mass transfer in a gravity induced falling film (i.e., fluid flowing only under the
influence of gravity, such as along the underside of an impeller blade) (Sharnez et al., 2004 [102]; Canhoto, 2004
[103]) and for manual processes the worst-case means of cleaning is determined by physical limitations associated
with manually cleaning the worst-case location.
Variables such as soil load and soiling conditions, hold time and drying conditions, sequence, duration and
temperature of rinse water, concentration of the cleaning solution, if applicable, surface characteristics such as the
material of construction and surface finish, and dilution of the process fluid during cleaning (i.e., soil to rinse solvent
or soil to cleaning solution ratio) should be determined in accordance with full scale operating conditions. For Clean In
Place (CIP) systems, setting the average fluid velocity at small scale to the worst-case operating value (VMIN) provides
assurance that if the soil can be cleaned by the simulated cycle at small scale, it can also be cleaned by the actual
cycle at full scale. Other important considerations for manual cleaning processes include dexterity, consistency and
training of operators, and implements used for scrubbing.

##### 6.3.2.9 Scalability of Small-Scale Cleanability Data

The scalability of small scale cleanability data depends on the accuracy with which relevant experimental parameters
at the worst-case location are estimated. Experimental parameters for simulating the worst-case location fall into two
distinct categories (Sharnez et al., Part II, 2015 [100]):
•
Parameters that can be readily determined from equipment specifications or process data, such as:
-
Material of construction and surface characteristics such as roughness and curvature of coupons or parts
used to simulate large scale equipment
-
Post soiling parameters such as hold time, and ambient temperature, humidity and air flow
-
Cleaning parameters such as rinse or wash time, temperature of rinse solvent, and temperature and
concentration of cleaning solution
•
Parameters that cannot be readily determined from equipment specifications or process data. These parameters
are determined from first principles and typically include the following:
-
Dilution of the process fluid during cleaning (i.e., soil to rinse solvent or cleaning solution ratio) Sharnez et
al., Part I, 2013 [99]

=== PAGE 66 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
-
Average velocity of rinse solvent or cleaning solution at the worst-case location (Sharnez et al., Part II, 2015
[100])
-
Soil load and soiling conditions Sharnez et al., Part III, slated for publication [101]

##### 6.3.2.10 Visual Inspection

Unlike analytical sampling and testing, visual inspection can and must be done (to whatever degree possible)
with every cleaning process execution. The visual inspection is an active observation of the visually accessible
product contact surfaces of the pharmaceutical manufacturing equipment after every cleaning and must determine
that the equipment is free from any visible residues in order for the cleaning to be considered adequate and that
manufacturing activities can commence.
In addition to the equipment cleaning personnel, visual cleanliness of cleaned equipment is routinely confirmed
and documented in the cleaning record by a second person. Prior to initiation of manufacturing of the subsequent
batch, equipment is routinely inspected for visual cleanliness by manufacturing personnel and documented in the
manufacturing batch record. It is recommended that any failure of the visual inspection is also recorded so that a true
assessment of the cleaning program can be made. Quality and validation personnel also inspect cleaned equipment
periodically as part of their work function. For these reasons, a useful adjunct to the plant quality assurance and
cross-contamination control program is the establishment of visual detection levels of residues generally visible to
operating personnel, given the site specific variables such as equipment configurations and light conditions on the
shop floor.
A method to determine the detection level is by spiking decreasing amounts of each residue onto stainless steel
coupons, allowed to dry, and then viewed by a group of observers. The lowest residue amount that is visible to all
observers is the lowest visual detection limit for that product. If the health-based residual limit is above this amount,
visual inspection should be adequate for routine monitoring to determine if shared equipment can be released as
clean for the next product and if the health-based residual limit is below this amount, then swab or rinse analysis may
be needed for routine monitoring in order to release shared equipment as clean for the next product.
Assuring adequate removal of non-intrinsic cleaning agents such as surfactants often involves very high health-based
residual limits and the absence of visible residue is a much more stringent criteria than the health-based residual limit.
For other compounds, health-based residual limits may be at or below the limits of visual detection. In such cases,
visual inspection is of only indirect value in determining an absence of active ingredient residue, although visual
checks may still be quite useful in assuring adequate removal on non-active materials like dyes and colors.
Training of personnel for visual inspection of cleaned equipment historically had consisted of on the job training
conducted by experienced personnel. Documentation of the training was, typically, not in a unique, separate training
record, but as part of general training for cleaning, operations or quality. As part of a cleaning validation program,
personnel that take swab samples have been routinely qualified to determine that they can adequately recover
residues from equipment surfaces. Laboratory personnel are qualified for testing of the cleaning samples. Cleaning
and testing procedures are validated to demonstrate consistent execution. A qualification criterion for visual inspection
of cleaned equipment by personnel is a logical extension of this philosophy as part of a cleaning validation execution.
From there, qualification of all personnel that visually inspect cleaned equipment would be an expected outcome.
Following the precepts of ICH Q9 [1], the level of effort and formality should be commensurate with the patient risk,
and therefore the degree of formality around a visual inspection qualification program is best established by site and
product specific risk assessment.

##### 6.3.2.11 Factors Affecting Exposure by Retention

Processes that involve materials that are difficult to clean, either through the formation of wet, sticky solids, or which
dry or form compact cakes on surfaces, should be carefully considered in the cleaning program. Items to assess for
equipment retention include:
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 67 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Use of processes requiring equipment with large surface areas for potential product contact
•
Frequency of changeover and cleaning
•
Use of disposable equipment/product contact parts
•
Any crevices or ledges where product can accumulate and not easily be detected such as in an occluded area
that is not subject to subsequent inspection
•
Rough or unpolished product contact surfaces
•
Drains
•
Material or process safety compatibility with preferred cleaning materials (e.g., strong acids, solvents)
The risk of significant residues within equipment is increased by a number of material factors. These include:
•
Processes creating sticky or caked on solids that may be difficult to clean
•
The use of materials with poor solubility or wetability with the cleaning materials available
•
Materials that dry with hard surfaces will also require special consideration for cleaning
•
Highly viscous or shear thickening materials resistant to turbulent washing regimes
•
Materials that react with common cleaning solutions, especially water, at low or high temperatures to form solid
or sticky gel-like materials

#### 6.3.3 Mechanical Transfer

Mechanical transfer includes all routes by which material can be transferred from contaminated non-product contact
surfaces into or onto the product. This includes product contact surfaces contaminated by contact with contaminated
surfaces (e.g., in cleaning areas), inadvertent or transient contact with other contaminated non-designated product
contact areas, and direct contact of the product with such surfaces as operator apparel and gloves. The major issue
with such routes is the lack of control of contaminated items. The factors which affect the risk of mechanical transfer
are made up of those which influence the:
•
Transfer of the material to the surface
•
Association of the surface with the product at risk
•
Release of the material from the contaminated surface to the product

##### 6.3.3.1 Factors Affecting Exposure by Mechanical Transfer

The risk of mechanical transfer can occur with the colocation of a process with contaminated equipment or items,
e.g., operators wearing contaminated clothing or contact between process equipment contaminated with different
materials.
Factors include:
•
The use of common areas where segregation of processes and equipment is poor, e.g., common manual
cleaning areas where dirty and clean equipment from different processes and production suites may be present
at the same time

=== PAGE 68 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Use of common gowning regimes across areas where multiple products may be present simultaneously
•
Surface to surface contact, e.g., scraping, smearing, or wiping of process equipment
•
Use of common equipment, e.g., hand tools or change parts between different processes
The use of certain equipment may cause operators or mobile equipment to become contaminated. This could then
potentially transit to other process areas without undergoing decontamination procedures. Issues to be considered
include:
•
The presence of highly manual, uncontained/open processes
•
The specification and location of decontamination procedures for users and equipment
•
Routing of equipment and users around facilities especially where there is common routing of products

#### 6.3.4 Airborne Transfer

The direct airborne transfer route assumes the generation of a stable aerosol, which moves to another area where
it deposits in significant quantities on another exposed product. An aerosol is a suspension of fine solid or liquid
particles in air. The operator has a greater risk of exposure than another product because of the inhalation of dust
by the operator in relatively close proximity to the product compared to the passive deposition of dust into or onto
another product distant from the emission source. Products typically are separated by physical barriers, airlocks,
and corridors. Industrial hygiene monitoring data should not be used to quantitatively evaluate potential product
cross-contamination due to these differences. However, high levels of airborne dust identified by monitoring may be
qualitatively indicative of a poorly controlled process, which may give rise to further analysis by other means.
Chemical contaminants do not have the potential to proliferate as is the case with microbial contamination. In
addition, there is generally less potential for airborne contaminants to present a serious risk of cross-contamination
of product at levels above the ADE than there is from other routes, such as mix-up and retention. However, chemical
contamination can increase due to continued deposition from an extended time emission source onto a continuously
exposed at risk product. Such deposition of an aerosol onto a remote surface over extended periods of time may also
give rise to an increased risk of subsequent mechanical transfer.
Factors affecting the scope of airborne aerosol creation and subsequent deposition are related to features such as
compound characteristics, process type, and operational behaviors.
There are a number of questions that can help determine if airborne contamination is a plausible pathway:
•
Can the contaminating process form a stable aerosol?
•
Can the stable aerosol be emitted and move to a position where it may deposit on the at risk product in
significant quantities?
•
Is the at-risk product sufficiently exposed to the aerosol for significant cross-contamination (i.e., above the ADE
value for the product that has aerosolized)?

##### 6.3.4.1 Factors Affecting Exposure by Airborne Transfer

Process issues that affect the risk of cross-contamination by airborne routes include process features that lead to
generation of stable significant aerosols, an available transfer route, and exposure of the contaminated process
material to deposition.

=== PAGE 69 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
For example, airborne contamination of a closed system would be highly unlikely, whereas airborne contamination of
an open vessel with a large open surface area in an open multiproduct area would be more critical.
In addition, any risk analysis should consider the safety and effectiveness of the design and operation of aerosol
containment, collection, and removal of any aerosol arising from the manufacturing process.
The risk analysis also should consider the following for any significant risk of cross-contamination:
•
Open processing activities at both source and receiving points
•
Poorly sealed process equipment with pressure driving forces or energy input which may promote undesired
transfer, e.g., positive pressure at source and/or negative pressure at receiving material positions
•
The repeated transfer of material to or from unit processes through breakable or poorly contained couplings
•
Relative locality of different process streams
•
Provision of rooms and other building features between source and receiving positions
Process materials transmissible as aerosols must have certain physical properties to maintain a stable aerosol for a
significant time period.
Key issues include:
•
Moisture content of solids
•
Liquid viscosity and surface tension
•
Particle size distribution
•
Particle shape
•
Particle density
•
Particle charge, which also may be related to other physical conditions such as relative humidity
Such properties may be considered as a combined factor commonly known as dustiness, which may be described as
a measure of the propensity of the material to remain airborne for extended periods when in an aerosol.

##### 6.3.4.2 Containment Performance Target for Airborne Material

The Containment Performance Target (CPT) (expressed in µg/m3) is a target value that is used as the basis for
plant design and selection of risk control strategies (i.e., containment systems) for protection of the workforce. Many
times, the risk control strategies used to meet the CPT will also provide protection from airborne transfer cross-
contamination.

### 6.4 Probability of Occurrence

A risk analysis designed to identify the probability of cross-contamination should consider all potential routes of cross-
contamination under all operational conditions in a systematic and detailed manner. The analysis should be reviewed:
•
At the design stage of any new or refurbished facility/equipment

=== PAGE 70 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
At the introduction of new products or changes to any process or equipment
•
As part of any relevant change control
•
As part of a regular risk management review
The following are possible examples where the probability of failure or error may be enhanced, and these also should
be considered as part of any review:
•
Manual processes
•
Historically non-robust processes or equipment
•
Specific elements of a process (batch start up, batch changeovers, shift changeovers, post maintenance)
•
New, untrained, or changed personnel

#### 6.4.1 Production Process Activities

It should be noted that the correct application of GxP generally ensures that most processes are reasonably well
contained and controlled with respect to cross-contamination. The production process needs to be fully reviewed to
ensure that all potential exposures are identified and evaluated.

##### 6.4.1.1 Operational Status: Set Up, Operation, Cleaning, Upset

A system that controls product and worker exposure should perform at all times. Many processes are evaluated for
containment performance during optimal operation, such as at Factory Acceptance Testing (FAT) and do not take
account of the following conditions:
•
Set up
•
Operation
•
Intervention for correction, heel recovery, end point determination, scrape down for yield/uniformity, sample
recovery
•
Upset, such as equipment failure
•
Disassembly
•
Cleaning
Each process step should be evaluated to ensure that it does not provide a significant potential for exposure. The
review of each of these items should ensure that the necessary actions can be performed and transfers of tools,
materials, and components to perform the tasks can be done without significant or unacceptable exposure or carry
over.

##### 6.4.1.2 Process and Exposures: A Review of Potential Exposures Created within Processes

A specific process will have a number of features, all of which may have a greater or lesser impact on the risk of
cross-contamination by one or more of the routes. The major factors to consider include:
•
The degree of open handling of the hazardous material (e.g., in bulk manual dispensing)

=== PAGE 71 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Energy in the form of velocity, pressure, and temperature inherent to the process, which may create transmissible
aerosols, e.g.:
-
Milling
-
Micronizing
-
Blending
-
Atomization
-
Fluidization
-
Agitated mixing
-
Compression
•
Transfer: the repeated charging and discharging of unit processes through breakable couplings provides a
possible route of release and the more transfers required, the greater the potential of risk
•
Worker behavior or compliance with SOPs: systems that are highly dependent on operator technique or allow
wide scope for variations in operator performance are more susceptible to failure
•
Frequency: the more often a process is performed the greater the risk of material release or serial releases. It
should be noted that frequency is a function of either repeated single emissions that rapidly diminish or a general
elevated level build up from repeated undiminished small emissions.
•
Scale: the impact of scale may not be linear. The factors affected by scale include worker care (manually
charging 100 kg requires a different approach to charging 100 g), and the volume of material available for
release into the air or the scope of surfaces contaminated, and to what degree, by direct contact.
•
Knowledge of the equipment used, operability, and performance

*[Figure 6.6: Summary of Process Characteristic Factors that Affect the Risk of Cross-Contamination]*

=== PAGE 72 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

#### 6.4.2 Non-Processing Activities

##### 6.4.2.1 Waste Handling

The risk analysis should cover all aspects of waste handling to ensure that items such as soiled clothing, disposable
items (e.g., masks, gloves, and filters), and material/product waste streams are appropriately handled to avoid the
potential for cross-contamination. Shared areas, e.g., common cleaning rooms, potentially containing simultaneously
both dirty and clean equipment from different process streams should be carefully considered.

##### 6.4.2.2 Planned and Unplanned Maintenance

All planned maintenance activities should be reviewed in sufficient detail to identify potential routes for cross-
contamination. Some planned maintenance may take place outside of normal operating hours and by personnel other
than those involved with the batch manufacture. Maintenance is a necessary activity that potentially may increase the
probability of cross-contamination.
The planned maintenance review should consider the following aspects, as a minimum, to establish whether the
maintenance is likely to create a potential cross-contamination risk:
•
Nature of the maintenance (e.g., air handling shutdown)
•
Frequency of the maintenance (e.g., daily, weekly, monthly, annual)
•
Status of production when the planned maintenance occurs (e.g., area clean or dirty)
•
The availability of approved procedures for planned maintenance
•
Necessary qualifications of personnel performing the maintenance activities
•
Appropriate training for those performing planned maintenance
•
The likelihood of powder spillage during maintenance (e.g., emptying local dust extraction and vacuum units)
It is more difficult to predict how unplanned maintenance may affect the probability of cross-contamination.
Key aspects to consider are the management and control systems that are in place to govern such unplanned
maintenance dependent upon the foreseen risks. For example, the level of management control for unplanned
maintenance may be higher for a highly hazardous product.
A proactive review of potential unplanned events should be performed, to predict how to handle many of them in
advance to help to avoid the risk of cross-contamination. This could lead to specific failure mode procedures for some
major events (e.g., HVAC failure).

##### 6.4.2.3 Changeovers

The changeover between one product and the next is an important aspect to be reviewed, especially regarding the
order of such changeover activities. The changeover activities should be appropriately documented as part of a
procedure and clearly indicate the sequence of the changeover work.
Changeover between products may require that equipment change parts have to be removed from the line for one
product and be replaced with change parts for the second product. The handling, cleaning, transport, and storage of
such change parts (e.g., tablet punches) should be carefully considered.

=== PAGE 73 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 6.4.2.4 Cleaning

Cleaning can in itself create a potential risk of cross-contamination if not performed properly. The cleaning procedures
should be reviewed to ensure that they are sufficiently detailed for personnel to be able to follow them consistently
and correctly. This especially is true for any manual cleaning procedures, and should cover the specific cleaning
materials, cleaning agents, and frequency and sequence of cleaning.
The cleaning processes which are developed should incorporate a risk-based approach to assist in the determination
of critical controls and/or critical parameters which are involved in the cleaning process. For example, a Design
of Experiment (DoE) could be performed to determine what parameters have the greatest or least impact on the
cleaning process. Parameters that have an important impact should be controlled and monitored and parameters that
have no or minimal impact could be ignored. Additionally, the cleaning process should be assessed to determine what
level of risk is posed by the cleaning process for a product/API based on residue sampling. Data should subsequently
be analyzed.
Automatic cleaning procedures such as CIP should, in theory, provide much more consistent cleaning than manual
cleaning procedures. The CPPs associated with any CIP system (e.g., times, temperatures, and pressures) and how
these are monitored, controlled, and alarmed should be understood. There also should be a clear understanding of
what happens if these CPPs go outside of limits (e.g., low pressure), as well as the degree of safeguards against
manual intervention (e.g., password control).

#### 6.4.3 Facility and Equipment Design

The design of the facility and equipment should be reviewed when considering the potential risks of cross-
contamination. The review should include consideration of risk elements such as:
•
Surfaces and finishes (e.g., smoothness, accessibility to clean)
•
Air handling philosophy (e.g., filtration levels, fresh air or recirculation, pressure differential regimes, airflows,
monitoring, recording, alarms, back up). The analysis also should review the need for once through air. If air
can be recirculated without undue risk to workers and cross-contamination, this can have a large impact on the
facility design as well as the operating costs. See Section 9.6 for more information.
•
Airlocks
•
Equipment layout (e.g., space, segregation)
•
Material and process flows including common areas for processes and personnel
•
Cleaning philosophy including provision of effective automated cleaning systems, such as CIP and Wash In
Place (WIP)
•
Process equipment design (e.g., closed versus open systems, charging ports)
Actual exposure varies between individual process cycles due to operator performance differences, and the degree
of this variability is often reflected in the method of exposure control. Closed systems have far lower variability than
open systems, and open systems with engineering controls are better controlled than unprotected open processes.
The selection of the specific process equipment may have a major impact on the scope of exposure through careful
design of the processing machine to minimize leaks. This may already be an inherent requirement of the equipment,
e.g., with API reaction pressure vessels during reaction steps or fluid bed dryers during drying. As a result, process
equipment or processes associated with them can be considered to be either “open” or “closed.”
ADV TOPIC

=== PAGE 74 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 6.4.3.1 Closed Process Definition

A process step (or system) is considered closed where the product is not exposed to the immediate room environment
(and vice versa). It is the manufacturer’s responsibility to define and demonstrate closure for a process step.
An example might include a pressurized vessel operation or a process enclosed in an effective “high containment”
process equipment item, e.g., a sealed process system filled and discharged through split butterfly valves.

##### 6.4.3.2 Open Process Definition

Any process step that does not meet the definition of “closed process” is considered “open.” This might include open
material handling during dispensing or where the process system is opened for a particular step, e.g., granulator bowl
scraping or bowl removal from a fluid bed dryer for product recovery. It should be noted that the amount of time the
process is open (whether it is brief or longer) should be considered during the risk assessment.

### 6.5 Product Exposure Risk in Non-Product Contact Areas

In general, the risk of cross-contamination due to mechanical and/or airborne transfer is considered to be low relative
to other possible pathways (e.g., retention or mix-up) although in these areas it may be critical for worker safety. If the
risk assessment indicates that airborne contamination is a potential concern, then further analysis may be necessary
using quantitative assessment tools based on the acceptance limits established reflecting the ADE of Product A and
the batch size and maximum daily dose of Product B.

#### 6.5.1 Non-Product Contact Area

*[Figure 6.7 illustrates the main classifications of non-product contact areas.]*

*[Figure 6.7: Classifications of Non-Product Contact Areas]*

=== PAGE 75 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Barriers physically segregate the product contact surface from the surrounding product room area as well as
from workers. Types of barriers can be closed processing equipment or enclosures such as a pressured vessel,
isolator, or ventilated enclosures such as safety cabinets and fume cupboards.
•
Product room/suite is where one product is processed on a dedicated, batch, or campaign basis where the non-
product contact surfaces include exterior surfaces of enclosed process equipment or barriers and interior finishes
such as walls, floor, ceiling, and doors.
•
Supporting areas are adjacent to the product rooms/suites, where workers from other product rooms may be
present.
•
The GxP area is the common area where the worker is not in direct contact with the product as in the secondary
packaging, shipping/receiving, and warehousing areas.
•
The HVAC system includes ducting, filters, Air Handling Units (AHUs), and control devices for a product room
installed in the GxP area.

#### 6.5.2 Residue on Non-Product Contact Surfaces

It is important that non-product contact surfaces are kept visually clean. Visual monitoring enables a wide area to
be observed quickly. Swab sampling data may be available to permit assessment of potential cross-contamination
of products from these non-product contact surfaces. Particularly where the exposure potential is high, swab
testing against preset limits may be part of a more detailed review/action plan. These preset limits should take into
consideration plausible, worst-case, assumptions of potential exposure pathways, the amount of Product A that
could cross contaminate Product B, and the ADE value for Product A. Some companies may use wipe samples/limits
to assess the potential dermal risk from systematic exposure of employees and this should not be confused with
potential cross-contamination risks from non-product contact surfaces.

#### 6.5.3 Plausibility

Non-product contact parts represent elements within the process boundary which are not in product contact. Typical
elements are the internal surfaces of an isolator or the surfaces of an engineering control device. These surfaces are
far less critical than product contact surfaces and should be cleaned between products.
Surfaces outside this zone are of even less significance, particularly where closed systems are used. Though the
surface concentration taken over the whole surface area may seem a significant number, the risk of all or even a
small fraction of this material being disengaged from the surface and entering the product is relatively insignificant.
For example, a room 5 m × 5 m × 3 m has a surface area of 110 m2. Where the LOD is 5 µg/swab, where the swab
is 25 cm2 and the samples taken are below the level of detection; taking a very conservative approach to estimate
the amount of product that might be available for transfer is to use the LOD. The total available on the surface is 110
m2 × 10,000 cm2/m2 × 5 µg/25 cm2 = 220,000 µg or 220 milligrams. Supposing the potential contamination is to a
batch with 100,000 maximum daily dose units and the ADE is 50 µg/day, the total that could be present in the other
product and be within the limit is 50 µg × 100,000 = 5,000,000 µg or 5 grams. Comparing the 5 grams allowable
carryover with a total of 220 milligrams potentially on all surfaces, the margin of safety is significant. In reality, only a
tiny percentage, if any, could possibly get into the product and the walls would not have a uniform level of product.
Therefore, this would not be considered a significant pathway for exposure.
Another example is how much mass could a stainless steel scoop, that is visually clean, transfer to another product.
If the scoop surface area is 2900 cm2 and visual detection is 4 µg/cm2, then the total amount this scoop could
potentially carry over to another product is 2900 cm2 × 4 µg/cm2 = 11,600 µg or 11.6 milligrams. If we assume the
same product as above is used where 5 grams is the safe carryover limit, then the margin of safety is the ratio of 5
grams and 11.6 mg, i.e., 431. This would, therefore, also be considered a low risk for cross-contamination.

=== PAGE 76 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

*[Figure 6.8: Process Capability for Non-Product Contact Surface Data]*

It should be noted that the smaller the surface area, the smaller the potential risk.

#### 6.5.4 Considerations for Exposure Assessment

The mode of exposure for non-product contact areas is by either mechanical or airborne transfer. Mechanical and
airborne transfers are inextricably mixed so careful analysis may be required to understand the true route of the
exposure. The following should be considered when conducting an exposure assessment for non-product contact
surfaces.
•
Open processes during operation, cleaning, upset, and maintenance
•
Retained or accumulated product inside the barrier or the product room/suite
•
Recirculated HVAC systems for the product room. If recirculation applies, is the air stream effectively protected
from recirculated aerosols?
•
Retained and accumulated product in the ductwork, including airborne transfer due to pressure differentials
•
Leakage of product to areas above the ceiling or interstitial space or into the cavities of the building, such as wall
cavities, technical spaces, or electric spaces
•
Retained or accumulated product during maintenance or repair of the HVAC or utility systems
•
Transfer of product compound from the worker’s clothing, hands, or feet
•
Transfer of product compound from dirty equipment transfer and their wheels
•
Transfer of product compound on the handling media

=== PAGE 77 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Manual cleaning of equipment and/or barrier
•
Emergency or upset situations in the pharmaceutical area
•
Shared HVAC systems serving several rooms or common areas where several different products are handled.
Such systems should be evaluated in relation to each specific hazard in order to see that they provide adequate
protection.

### 6.6 Detection

An essential element in accurately assigning a risk level to a hazard arising from a compound is the degree of
difficulty by which the compound can be detected. A hazard rating may be high, but if the method of detection is
sufficient, linked to health-based limits and to appropriate control measures, then the subsequent risk arising from
that hazard can be managed and low.
The application of methodologies which possess the degree of selectivity and sensitivity necessary to detect a
compound of interest is key to any detection-based control of risk from a hazard. Such controls, when optimally
embedded into those points of the process which present the highest level of potential concern can effectively control
the risk.
In situations where the method of detection is visual only, it is important to understand the visual acuity of the staff
and what level of residue is considered safe. If the safe level is below the visual acuity of the staff then the risk
of failure not being detected may be considered high whereas if the safe level is well above (several orders of
magnitude) the visual acuity of the staff the risk of failure not being detected may be considered low.
The degree of variability in product contact equipment cleaning processes should be understood and considered as
a primary factor in ongoing monitoring decisions. As an example, automated CIP processes present less risk from
a variability perspective than manual cleaning methods, and may therefore justify less ongoing monitoring (once
mechanical reliability is proven) than a manual process.

#### 6.6.1 Developmental Controls Providing Process Knowledge

During the course of pharmaceutical discovery and development activities, one focus is on the development of
analytical methodology aimed at accurate identification and assessment of both drug substance and potential
synthesis and degradation impurities. The drug substance itself and the potential preliminary toxicological profiles are
established for each impurity as well as for the drug substance.
Traditionally, once analytics have been established, efforts have turned to the optimization of test methods, an
exercise which culminates in a “validation exercise.”
Process Analytical Technology (PAT) is increasingly being applied to processes for the purpose of acquiring
information to facilitate process understanding and allowing risk control strategies to be established. Once
established, these should be maintained and continually improved under the Pharmaceutical Quality System (ICH
Q10 [8]).
As such, the development, convergence, and subsequent integration of analytical technologies such as Near Infra-
Red (NIR) Raman Spectroscopy and endpoint monitoring/control tools should be considered essential developmental
activities. Complementary controls, such as barrier systems, also may be seen as suitable mechanisms to control a
risk arising from any particular hazard.
Subsequent scale up activities should embed continued refinement of PAT enablers. Such continual improvement
initiatives should become institutional requirements within organizations, much akin to historical efforts of yield
optimization and process train mapping.
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 78 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
A potential challenge to the holistic approach presented may be encountered where traditional demarcation exists
between research and commercial functions within the industry. This should not prevent existing methodologies being
either discounted or replaced by new techniques or a technology developed by units supporting the progression of
the compound throughout its life cycle.

#### 6.6.2 Compendia Comparison

Prior to starting the development of complementary analytics, units concerned should assess the basis of current
referee methodology utilized by regulatory agencies. The methods, both microbiologically and analytically derived,
typically assist the agencies in the evaluation of a variety of sample collection analyses: to include challenging
materials for the presence of compounds, such as penicillins and beta-lactams. The caveat for any internally
developed analytical or microbial method is that it should formally demonstrate at least an equivalent level of
sensitivity as the compendial (e.g., USP, EP, JP) or referee methodology. Reasons for developing an alternative
methodology may include improved sensitivity, robustness, application to PAT technologies, or ease of use.

#### 6.6.3 Adequacy of Controls

Throughout the product life cycle, the containment technologies, equipment, and analytical testing which are utilized
should continue to be evaluated for applicability, currency, reproducibility, and robustness. Without consistency of
the controls which are employed, multiple variables will exist which will complicate root cause investigations should a
detection technique identify a breach to the acceptable threshold of control which has been established.
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 79 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
7	 Risk Evaluation
ICH Q9 [1] places risk evaluation (see Figure 7.1) at the conclusion of risk assessment (risk identification/risk
