analysis/risk evaluation). Risk evaluation compares the identified and analyzed risk against given risk criteria. This
step is designed to provide an estimation of risks to patients via product cross-contamination or to workers based
on the hazard identification, dose-response assessment, and exposure assessment, along with consideration of
the assumptions, limitations, and sources of uncertainty and variability in the analysis. All available information is
evaluated in order to determine the robustness of the data sets used to assess the risk. Based on this evaluation, a
conclusion can be made as to whether potential exposures are above or below acceptable levels.

*[Figure 7.1: Risk Evaluation]*

Incomplete knowledge about the hazard of a compound, the potential routes for mechanical and airborne transfer,
and resulting variation in exposures brought about by design of process or equipment, are all sources of uncertainty
whose potential impact should be considered in assessing both risk to patients by cross-contamination and risk
to workers by direct exposure to the compound. If the data sets do not provide sufficient information, it may be
determined during this step that additional data gathering is necessary. The output of the risk evaluation is either
a quantitative estimate (e.g., distance between acceptance criteria and data gathered (true margin of safety)) or a
qualitative description (e.g., high, medium, a numerical probability, or low) of a range of risk.

=== PAGE 80 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
All aspects of risk assessment, not just the risk evaluation, should be evaluated and documented in a manner
proportionate to the risk (the higher the risk, the more indepth the evaluation and documentation process). The
evaluation should be completely transparent so that the user fully understands the application and limitation(s) of the
conclusions drawn. A fully transparent risk assessment can be used in the ongoing risk review process.

### 7.1 Defining Acceptable Risk

It is important for senior management to set guidelines on how to determine acceptable risk, and more importantly,
unacceptable risk. Unacceptable risk requires that processing should cease until the risk is reduced to acceptable
levels. Typically, there will be three action ranges:
•
Acceptable risk: no action required/monitor only
•
Unacceptable risk: cease actions until risk is reduced to acceptable levels/monitor to confirm effectiveness of risk
reduction
•
Reduce risk if possible: between acceptable risk and unacceptable risk, there is a range where risk reduction
measures should be employed but actions can continue to be monitored to ensure risk is not increased to
unacceptable
Risk can be categorized in qualitative terms such as high, medium or low risk, red, yellow, green, or in quantitative
terms where the data is compared to acceptance criteria consisting of numerical limits.
Caution should be exercised in the use of qualitative risk terms as these are indicative of the potential for risk and
not a definitive risk outcome. All identified risks, whether qualitative or quantitative, high or low, should be reviewed
periodically, based on the level of risk, to ensure that the risk profile has not changed.

=== PAGE 81 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
8	 Risk Control
ICH Q9 [1] states that risk control (see Figure 8.1) is the process of decision making to reduce and/or accept risks.
Risk acceptance and risk reduction should be considered as iterative steps and be subject to constant review,
feedback, and communication. If the risk is still unacceptable, further risk reduction steps should be identified. A Risk
Priority Number (RPN), from an FMEA is an example of a qualitative risk evaluation output and any action taken as a
consequence is part of the risk control steps. More details can be found in the examples provided in Chapter 14.

*[Figure 8.1: Risk Control]*

It is important to consider control strategies that will address concerns in both IH and GxP settings, and any control
strategy used to maintain an acceptable level of risk for one setting should not add risk to the other. For example,
a strategy that focuses on reducing exposure in the immediate workplace may have a negative, and perhaps
unforeseen, impact on GxP issues. Worker exposure might be successfully reduced by the use of a totally closed
system, close coupling of unit operations, or by the addition of redundancy in the form of gloveboxes. However, this
may increase the risk of product retention and cross-contamination of subsequent products if the processing train is
more difficult to clean.
Once a strategy has been selected for one setting, a risk analysis of the other should be performed before accepting
the control strategy. Similarly, before embarking on the implementation of risk reduction strategies, a qualitative
risk assessment should be undertaken using risk ranking and FMEA tools or other appropriate tools or techniques.
These tools are used to evaluate the proposed risk reduction scheme, and where inadequacies are shown to exist,
alternative strategies should be evaluated. Once control strategies have been implemented, quantitative risk
assessments should be made to demonstrate the performance capability of the system.

=== PAGE 82 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 8.1 Holistically Balanced Approach to Risk Control

The fundamental objective for a risk management program is to ensure that exposure both of the patient through
contamination of one product with another, and for a worker via direct contact/inhalation is maintained below the ADE
value or other acceptable health-based limit, e.g., OEL for worker exposure and is visually clean, where appropriate.
When working in multiproduct facilities, zero risk of cross-contamination of one product with another is neither realistic
nor scientifically achievable. The degree to which such cross-contamination is controlled within the normal bounds
of GxP should be commensurate with the risk identified to the patient, considering hazard, probability, and severity.
Similar considerations should be applied within either multipurpose or dedicated facilities toward the potential
exposure of workers to the compound concerned. In either case, the controls selected should be appropriate to the
hazard as well as the nature and scale of the activity. These controls must ensure that safe levels are not exceeded
and should be economically and technically feasible, and applied in both a holistic and balanced manner. In this
context, holistic means that every aspect of product and process is considered, including formulation, equipment,
and facility design and operation. All stakeholders should be considered when determining which risks exist and
what measures are necessary to ensure they are adequately controlled. Each control measure implemented and the
degree and way in which it acts as a control should be understood.
Risk control measures should be determined by a multidisciplinary team and care should be taken to ensure that the
risk to one set of stakeholders is not mitigated at the cost of exposing another stakeholder to an unacceptable risk.
To achieve balance, understanding the individual aspects of control and the interactions between controls should be
evaluated to meet multiple needs.
8.1.1 Operability (Function and Ergonomics)
A holistic approach should take account of all the operational parameters involved when designing control strategies.
Operability assessments should be conducted with the participation of workers and a full review of the operations
required in setup, operation, intervention, disassembly, upset recovery, and cleaning.
An ergonomic assessment should be conducted and should include evaluations, such as reach and force required to
perform tasks. Ergonomics is the science of fitting the task to the person rather than the person to the task.
8.1.2 Impact (Cost, Time, Disruption to Process)
A cost benefit analysis should be undertaken to ensure that the benefits of a well-designed system are maximized.
A lifetime cost analysis should be used to select the most appropriate solution. The time and disruption caused by
the implementation should be taken into account in the evaluation. For example, a closed system may meet all the
requirements for product and worker safety without dedication, but may have a very long time between campaigns
due to the cleaning process. An evaluation on the impact may lead some manufacturers to dedicate some equipment
for purely operational reasons.

#### 8.1.3 Minimize Over Engineering of Solutions

One of the largest costs, and a significant impact on operability, is the over-design and over-specification of
a solution. Complex engineering is much easier to achieve than a simple elegant, operational solution. The
unnecessary complication of controls, features, etc., is common and can lead to increased product or worker
exposure if tasks are too complicated. Workers may bypass the complex system and others, such as the cleaning
personnel, may have an increased exposure potential. With proper use of risk management tools, the solutions
should identify the areas requiring risk reduction, while indicating areas that are already within the acceptable risk
range, and therefore, do not require further attention.

=== PAGE 83 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Care should be taken in accepting vendor statements of performance such as “Equipment X performs to Y OEL”, as
the statement is incomplete without additional data such as duration of task. Vendors tend to receive little feedback
from users and the cost of the solution may not include any further development of the design after the award of
the contract. To achieve a practical cost effective solution, all stakeholders should be involved, and where possible,
knowledgeable “experts”, internal or external, should be included to evaluate conflicting challenges. The “keep it
simple principle” should be applied.

=== PAGE 84 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

=== PAGE 85 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
9	 Risk Reduction
ICH Q9 [1] states that risk reduction (see Figure 9.1) is the implementation of control strategies to mitigate or avoid
the risk. Risk reduction can be achieved by addressing the severity and/or probability of harm as well as provide
improvements in the detection of the hazards.

*[Figure 9.1: Risk Reduction]*

Where the results of a risk assessment have identified the need for control strategies to reduce the risk of exposure
to a safe level, a hierarchy for risk reduction (see Figure 9.2) should be applied to prioritize all significant risks. The
hierarchy is listed in priority order and should be considered and applied in this order. Each level of the hierarchy
should be applied in a way that is appropriate to the activity and consistent with the risk assessment, and each should
be implemented only after consideration of the preceding level. A holistic view of the product, process, and facilities
may lead to a strategy that includes partial mitigation at several levels in the hierarchy before overall risk is reduced
to acceptable levels.

=== PAGE 86 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 9.1 Hierarchy of Risk Reduction

*[Figure 9.2: Applied Hierarchy of Risk Reduction]*

#### 9.1.1 Elimination

Completely eliminates the risk. Since the hazard is a constant based on the compound, completely eliminating the
risk is not practical.
Elimination of potential points of exposure (process steps and/or transfers) should be considered as a method to
eliminate or reduce the risk. If an exposure point can be eliminated, then the risk associated with it is removed. For
information, Section 9.3 discusses potential opportunities to eliminate risk.

#### 9.1.2 Substitution

Substituting the hazardous material with a less hazardous material is also not practical since the hazardous material
is the compound. Substitution of the process or formulation should be considered as a method to eliminate or reduce
risk. In the pharmaceutical industry, existing product registrations may limit the feasibility of substitution options.
Addressing these issues early in development prior to fixing formulations or processes is recommended.

#### 9.1.3 Reduction

Limit material handling and reduce the potential for exposure through the use of engineering controls.

=== PAGE 87 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
In this context, engineering controls can include the use of high integrity closed processing equipment, as well as
additional containment devices such as extract-based systems and isolators. When employing these technologies,
the primary concern, either worker protection or cross-contamination, should be made clear. When holistically
applied, it is possible to address both aspects.
For more information, Sections 9.4 through 9.7 discuss potential opportunities to limit handling of a compound to
reduce risk.

#### 9.1.4 Administrative (Procedural) Controls

The proper use of risk controls should be described in SOPs. Administrative procedures should ensure that
personnel understand the practical implications of the risks set out in the SOPs. Failure mode procedures that clearly
indicate the actions to be completed, and the communication required in case of failures, also should be prepared.
Clear expectations should be published. Since success with the procedures is dependent upon adherence to the
procedures (compliance), attention should be paid to behavior management and tracking activities, such as cleaning
validation or purity testing.
A key element of a quality management system is to ensure the compliance of personnel with policies, work,
processes, and procedures. The quality culture of an organization generally fits one of the progressive descriptions in
Table 9.1.
Table 9.1: Quality Culture of an Organization
In Stage 4, where workers truly care for, watch for, and understand their accountability, (for their own and their
coworkers quality work in producing products), a mature interdependent behavior-based quality culture exists.

##### 9.1.4.1 The Impact of Culture

The quality culture progresses through the stages described in Table 9.1, and is proportionate to the perception
of management commitment to “Quality in the Workplace” by personnel. It is considered an excellent indicator
of overall program success. Engaged and capable personnel, coupled with well written risk control policies and
procedures (incorporating worker input) form the foundation of effective risk management. This may be enhanced
by well communicated targets that are observed and measured for personnel at all levels. Such visible management
commitment and leadership establishes risk-based management as the norm.
It should be noted that the level achieved may vary between sites and facilities within a company, as well as vary
from company to company, and that individual companies and individual staff may move up and down levels on
an ongoing basis as this is the nature and variability of human behavior and controls. In addition, staff turnover or
the use of temporary staff especially in critical areas, is also an important consideration in determining the quality
culture of an organization. As such, reliance on manual controls will increase risk and may require additional or more
frequent use of, and reliance on, detection methods.
Stage
Observable/Measurable Behavior
Employees do not follow SOPs even when they are being directly supervised
Employees follow SOPs only when they are being directly supervised
Employees follow SOPs even when not directly supervised
An employee will correct the nonadherent or noncompliant behavior of a coworker in the absence of a
supervisor

=== PAGE 88 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 9.2 Risk Reduction: Routes of Exposure

Risk reduction strategies for each of the routes for cross-contamination are discussed.

#### 9.2.1 Mix-up

Procedures, protocols, physical controls, and redundant monitoring can help reduce mix-up. Methods that ensure
detection of mix-ups are necessary, as it is unlikely that mix-up can be eliminated totally. Techniques to reduce the
risk of mix-up include:
•
Provision of redundant electronic verification of materials
•
Color coding
•
Bar coding and reading of materials, equipment, etc.
•
Clear labeling of room status (in use/product present) as well as of used equipment (cleanliness, contaminant
present etc.)
•
Removal of unused API and other materials from plant local stores at the end of a campaign
•
Using locked cages to store APIs with careful chain of custodian, control of the key
•
Radio Frequency Identification (RFID)
•
Separate redundant manual checks
•
Selecting transfer and utility connect technologies that are unique to the process
•
PAT and NIR verifications
•
Label creation at point of application, etc.
•
Physical separation of high risk products/materials
It is the manufacturer’s responsibility to develop and demonstrate a well thought out and executed strategy for the
prevention of mix-ups that takes full account of human fallibility.

#### 9.2.2 Retention

The development of a comprehensive cleaning program based on science, risk assessment, and statistical analysis
will provide the greatest level of safety for the patient. Equipment should be designed for ease of cleaning and for the
confirmation of the effectiveness of cleaning. Identification of hard to clean areas and areas that could retain material
is required in a robust cleaning program. Techniques to reduce the risk of retention include:
•
Clean to set limits consistently
•
Cleaning process design (Note: it is important to avoid damaging highly polished product contact surfaces
through activities such as scraping with inappropriate tools)

=== PAGE 89 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Certified visual inspection program
•
Overall cleaning program risk assessment
•
PAT
•
On-line and at-line monitoring
•
Process capability evaluations of swab sample data
•
Use single use disposable product contact parts
•
Dedicated product contact parts
A solution may involve one or more of these techniques.

##### 9.2.2.1 Cleaning Validation Program

A site’s cleaning validation program is often the most visible and audited part of its overall cross-contamination control
strategy. In order to be both effective and compliant, a cleaning process validation must:
•
Be based on a science and risked-based understanding of the hazards presented by a given compound and/or
its cleaning agents
•
Be executed using already qualified equipment systems such as water or CIP systems (where applicable)
•
Be performed following Quality Unit preapproved protocols containing sampling sites, methods and rationale for
selection, details of cleaning procedure followed, and both visual and analytical criteria for acceptance
•
Include multiple test repetitions to demonstrate consistency and reproducibility
Note: unlike manufacturing process validation for a drug substance or product, there is no current expectation
to formally justify the number of validation runs, that is, a three-run cleaning validation is still typical and
generally acceptable. That said, science and process knowledge should always be the first consideration, and
an evaluation of run-to-run variability should still be a factor in completion of pre-validation risk assessments to
determine the final validation protocol strategy, including the actual number of runs required to provide sufficient
assurance prior to commercial use of a new cleaning procedure. There are several characteristics of the
compound that could require additional runs such as hazard, the excipients, manual versus automatic processes.
•
Be monitored post-validation in a justified and effective manner to assure ongoing cleaning procedure
effectiveness. “Stage 3” or Continued Process Verification (CPV) monitoring for a validated cleaning process can
include analytical and/or visual components, applied in a risk-based manner that is dependent on very specific
and contextual aspects of a given product and its cleaning processes.

=== PAGE 90 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 9.2.2.2 Continued Process Verification for Validated Cleaning Processes

Once cleaning validation runs are successfully completed and approved, the cleaning procedure in place can be
implemented in commercial manufacture, subject to its post-validation or CPV monitoring plan. CPV plans for
cleaning processes should include both aspects of acceptability: analytical measurement of residuals against a
predetermined health-based safety limit and the visual observations of personnel inspecting cleaned equipment for
any visible residue.
Analytical Criteria: the methods and criteria for analytical monitoring of cleaning depend completely on the scenario
involved. Where PAT such as on-line TOC is available and able to directly measure (or correlate with) residual levels,
the question of when and where to sample may be moot. In many cases, however, the analytical sampling (by swab
or rinse) of “worst-case” monitoring locations may require disassembly or other equipment manipulations precluding
every batch testing plans. In such cases, a risk assessment informed by the cleaning validation run results can aid in
determining the most challenging and failure indicating sample locations (or types), as well as supporting or refuting
initial estimates of the “occurrence” or “probability” of certain residual levels occurring, which in turn can help justify
frequency of analytical monitoring. In cases where the acceptance limits are well above visual detection and the
cleaning process is robust analytical sampling may be reduced as justified by risk assessments. Equally where the
acceptance limits are well below visual detection, especially for manual cleaning processes, analytical sampling may
be increased as justified by risk assessments.

#### 9.2.3 Mechanical Transfer

Techniques to reduce the risk of mechanical transfer include:
•
Providing a boundary so that mechanical transfer is minimized (for further information, see Section 9.5)
•
Gown/glove change at the product boundary, hence the use of material and personnel airlocks and
decontamination procedures.
•
Workers should be gowned to protect the person, personal clothing, and the product from potential
contamination. The gown prevents contamination of personal clothing, inhalation, ingestion, and dermal contact
with the product and minimizes mechanical transfer.
•
Gloves should be worn at all times when in the product boundary and a second pair should be worn during
critical operations such as connecting and disconnecting transfer systems and cleaning. Potentially contaminated
gloves should be carefully removed and disposed of to prevent mechanical transfer.
•
Removing critical gowning items such as gown, hairnet, overshoes, and gloves at a defined point and using a
technique which avoids cross-contamination.
•
Providing flow routes that keep clean and dirty equipment, people, materials, etc., separate.
•
Using airlocks for personnel, materials, or both to segregate work areas.
•
Wiping or cleaning of the exterior of material containers when removing from production area/room.
•
Physically separate rooms to separate products and processes
•
Using “clean” construction to facilitate removal of potential contaminants from surfaces
•
Telephones and similar devices that require manual activation should be replaced by hands free or touch pad
devices to allow for ease of cleaning.
•
Providing electronic means to pass information and data between the room or suite and the GxP area.
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 91 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

#### 9.2.4 Airborne Transfer

Techniques to reduce the risk of airborne transfer include:
•
Closed process systems
•
Engineering controls to reduce the degree of openness of a process enclosure
•
HVAC arrangement to flush critical zones with clean air and move airborne product away from the room entry,
operator space, or other potential vectors for cross-contamination
•
Airlocks, single and double, with or without positive pressure cascades
•
Gowning/decontamination of people and materials passing in and out
•
Use of HVAC and pressure regimens to minimize airborne transfer to protect workers and product
•
Use of appropriate filtration to capture airborne particulate and prevent recirculation of the particulate
Note: that many of the techniques are similar to those listed to reduce mechanical transfer.
Wipe samples taken outside the GxP area may show contamination due to failures of gowning procedures or even
mechanical transfer on paperwork. While this is of greater significance to the worker rather than the product, the
presence of drug substance at or near the limits for non-product contact is an indication of a failed system and careful
assessment of all aspects of the hierarchy of risk control is essential to remediate these conditions.

### 9.3 The Role of Pharmaceutical Research and Development in Risk Reduction

According to ICH Q8(R2) [20], “The aim of pharmaceutical development is to design a quality product and its
manufacturing process to consistently deliver the intended performance of the product. … quality should be built in by
design.” Opportunities to reduce or eliminate exposure should be considered as early as possible in the development
of the product and the process.
Each API presents a specific intrinsic hazard resulting from the toxicological properties of the material. For the
purposes of this Guide, it is assumed that the toxicological nature of the active drug substance itself cannot be
changed. The toxicological properties of a compound can be assessed early in the development process, even if
based solely on animal data. The mechanism of action and intended use are known and the anticipated clinical dose
may already be established. An Occupational Exposure Band (OEB) is likely to have been assigned and it is possible
to estimate an ADE from the OEB, e.g., using the dose associated with the low end of the band, i.e., 10 µg/day for a
1 to 10 µg/m3 band assuming 10 m3 of air are breathed in 8 hours. This information may help influence formulation
design at an early phase of development.
Drug substances are rarely administered in their pure form and the pharmaceutical development department plays a
crucial role in formulating the finished drug product. Priorities for a formulation scientist include:
•
Bioavailability
•
Stability
•
Ability to manufacture
Physical form and particle size of the active drug substance together with the choice of excipients are considered.

=== PAGE 92 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
This Guide recognizes that drug product formulation is a complex subject with numerous interacting variables. The
detail of drug product formulation is outside the scope of this Guide.
Formulation scientists have an opportunity to consider additional measures to reduce the potential for exposure to an
API. For example, the level of risk can be reduced by considering how to contain the API at the earliest opportunity
within the manufacturing process through product formulation or process selection. An appropriate approach can both
help to improve worker protection and to reduce the risk of potential cross-contamination.
Typically, to be therapeutically effective, higher hazard molecules tend to require lower administered doses. However,
this may also mean that it is necessary to handle comparatively small quantities of the API relative to the overall batch
size of the finished product. Where a compound is designated as highly hazardous, handling that compound does not
necessarily present a high risk.
As part of the development of any dosage form, the formulator should strive to combine the API(s) with suitable
excipients as early as possible in the process.
Direct compression formulations can be employed to minimize the potential for product cross-contamination
and worker exposure by combining the active ingredient with suitable grade excipients. The formulator may also
consider selecting excipients that generate very little dust, such as silicified microcrystalline cellulose or mannitol,
as the primary carrier in the formulation. The number of processing steps can be significantly reduced using direct
compression formulations and well-designed formulations may simply be dispensed, blended, and compressed into
tablets or filled into capsule shells. This eliminates processes, such as fluid bed drying and dry milling, which might
have a higher potential for exposure.
A well-designed granulation process should offer similar benefits to those derived from a direct compression
formulation. The addition of a granulating solvent can dampen the API and reduce the risk of aerosols. If it is
necessary to mill the dried granules, the milling process should be carefully controlled to minimize dust generation.
Single pot granulation systems are now commercially available, which allow granulation and drying steps to be
performed in the same vessel, thus reducing the exposure potential by removing the need for transferring powder
from granulator to dryer.
Coating the solid dosage form can significantly reduce the risk of cross-contamination at certain process stages.
A film coating or sugar coating provides a significant barrier, protecting both the active drug from its surrounding
environment (air, moisture, and light), and reducing the risk of exposure to the active material in downstream
operations such as packaging. It also minimizes the risk to people in the downstream who may need to handle the
product e.g., pharmacist, nurse, or patient.
Encapsulation is an alternative approach which allows powders to be filled directly into a shell. Capsules can be
sealed at the joint, over encapsulated (capsule within a capsule), or film coated. Encapsulation can be a dusty
process and although modern encapsulators may be well contained, dust can adhere to the outer surfaces of the
capsule shell. Close attention should be given to dedusting. Broken capsules should be addressed in the same way
as broken tablets.
It may be possible to formulate the active substance in a liquid or semi-solid vehicle and fill into a capsule (hard or
soft shell). This is a particularly useful approach for high hazard compounds, as it removes any powder handling
stages (once the compound is solubilized in the appropriate vehicle). With formulations of this type, consideration
should be given to whether any risk of exposure remains from contamination from the liquid or semi-solid. Is there
a risk of exposure directly from the vehicle or from active product drying out from the vehicle and depositing on the
outer surface of the shell?
Overall, early containment of an active drug substance via formulation, especially for more hazardous materials, may
bring long-term handling advantages and risk reduction. There also may be cost benefit from reducing the need for
specialized containment technology or the need for dedicated equipment, dedicated rooms, etc.

=== PAGE 93 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Formulation scientists should pay close attention to the opportunities they have to reduce risk throughout the entire
manufacturing and supply chain. This should be done as early as possible in the development of the product. Once
formulations and processes are registered, it can be very costly and time consuming to make changes.

### 9.4 Process and Related Technologies

Properly designed and operated processes, equipment, and other related technologies can reduce the risk of
cross-contamination from mix-up, retention, mechanical transfer, and airborne transfer. Addressing the action items
in Figure 9.3 to minimize exposure during process and equipment design, can help to maintain risks at or below
acceptable limits. During process and equipment design, the reduction of one set of risks (e.g., IH) should not
negatively impact the risks to the other areas (e.g., GxP).

*[Figure 9.3: Action Items to Minimize Exposure during Process and Equipment Design]*

For most formulations, the only stage of the process in which the API is handled in its pure form is during dispensing,
API sampling, and API addition to the first process; therefore, these operations require close attention. The API
generally presents a lower risk in subsequent operations up to blend uniformity or final mix before dosage form,
as it is often a very small component of a batch. After blend uniformity or final mix, the risk increases because the
acceptable limit at single dosage can be much lower than the ADE, depending on the number of doses in a maximum
daily dose for a patient.
API addition in a liquid or slurry form should result in far lower exposure potential than dry powder addition, unless
there is a danger of the formation of an aerosol on addition of liquid, particularly under pressure, or the addition is in
a solvent base that is conducive to transdermal migration, in which case complete and appropriate dermal protection
should be worn.
Integration of unit processes: systems that integrate a number of process steps, such as “one-pot” processors (e.g.,
granulator/dryers), reduce the need for transfers between unit operations.
Use of closed process: a holistic review of the issues to be addressed should be performed. Equipment vendors
may claim a process is “closed,” but the detail of such claims should be fully evaluated. Will there be a need for any
process intervention? Examples of typical interventions include:
•
Manual scrape down
•
Performing process corrections

=== PAGE 94 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Changing contaminated or blocked parts such as mill screens
•
Extract samples
•
Determine process end points
•
Perform cleaning
•
Upset recovery
The impact of these interventions on the potential for exposure or material release should be evaluated in detail. If
they present an unacceptable level of risk, techniques should be evaluated that do not require intervention. In-line
measuring devices or automated devices to scrape down or clear blinded mill screens should be considered.
Where possible, sampling for end point, blend uniformity, etc. should be replaced with technologies that do not
require the extraction of samples; these systems often are described as PAT.
Selection of process equipment that reduces exposure potential, e.g., hammer (and other high energy) mills can
result in a far higher exposure potential than cone mills. Charging a bin blender can provide a lower risk through close
coupling than, e.g., charging a V blender.
Selection of process equipment that reduces the potential for retention should consider:
•
Minimizing surface area of product contact parts
•
Eliminating ledges and crevices on the product contact parts
•
Reducing the frequency of changeover and cleaning
•
Using polished product contact surfaces
Leakage: process equipment vendors should be challenged to ensure that nozzles, glands, or entry points to the
product processing zone are secure under operating conditions and able to contain the hazard. This includes
protection for over or under pressure; some process filters just perform the function of yield protection. Some leakage
is considered inevitable, and even at high leakage rates, with proper controls, containment should still be possible.
With legacy processes, there may be significant cost and regulatory hurdles that make change difficult to justify. In
these situations, there may be a basic level of engineering control providing a “closed” process. Control methods
should meet the following criteria:
•
Provide defined product and occupational protective levels consistently
•
Take full account of all necessary interventions
•
Be safe and ergonomic in use
•
Be able to perform functionally

#### 9.4.1 Redundancy

Redundancy is the provision of more than one element performing the function so that if one fails, the next takes up
the required duty and detection devices alarm the failure of the primary system.
If a system is dependent on a single critical interface, it is likely to fail; when redundancy is introduced, the likelihood
of failure diminishes. A robust system can be designed based on a series of non-optimal redundancies.

=== PAGE 95 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 9.5 The Role of Facility Engineering in Risk Reduction

A properly designed and operated facility can reduce the risk of cross-contamination from mix-up, mechanical
transfer, and airborne transfer. Proper flow of workers, product, equipment, materials, and wastes within a facility can
have a significant impact on maintaining risk of cross-contamination at or below acceptable limits.

#### 9.5.1 Spatial Configuration

In planning a facility, the relationship of areas should be carefully considered to minimize the risk of cross-
contamination. The purpose of this Section is to provide an overview of the basic principles. Individual ISPE Baseline®
Guides address this subject specifically for the type of facility being reviewed, such as those considering API and Oral
Solid Dosage (OSD).

#### 9.5.2 The Control Concept

Regardless of the hazard of a compound, it should be handled in such a way that the compound remains within the
room or suite in which it is processed. This area does not include corridors or other general areas unless they form
part of a product dedicated suite. The compound should not be detectable above acceptable limits outside the areas
directly involved in processing the product.

##### 9.5.2.1 Hierarchy of Boundaries

For the purposes of this Guide, there are a number of boundaries that contain the pharmaceutical compound. The
bubble diagram in Figure 9.4 explains the concept. These boundaries should be designed to contain the product to
prevent cross-contamination and worker exposure created by the adjacencies in the facility.

*[Figure 9.4: Boundaries Containing Pharmaceutical Compound]*

Process Boundary: the process equipment and the interface through which it is charged, discharged, sampled,
intervened, and recovered. This can vary from closed to open.
Room or Suite Boundary: this is the room, rooms, or suite in which one product is processed on a batch or
campaign basis, and which may then be released after appropriate cleaning and testing for use by another product. It
may be product dedicated at the owner’s discretion. These rooms should be able to contain the product at all times so
that the common GxP corridor is never exposed to unsafe concentrations. The product boundary may have material
and personnel airlocks and all HVAC and utilities should be protected to prevent ingress and egress of the product
dependent on the degree of risk to the boundary.

=== PAGE 96 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
GxP Boundary: this boundary is the boundary between the outside world and the pharmaceutical environment and is
designed to keep the outside world out of the pharmaceutical world and vice versa.

*[Figure 9.5: Facility with Alternate Arrangements]*

Facilities are generally designed in zones to match their usage, and the protection of product and worker. The zones
are indicated and the numbers indicated in Figure 9.5 refer to the item numbers listed:
1.
Office, administrative, cafeteria, corridors, and non-barrier change/restrooms. These areas are of out of the
scope of this Guide although:
•
They should not be contaminated by product to unacceptable levels including plant generated paperwork.
Consider electronic transfer of paperwork or data.
•
Product, in any form, should never be in these areas.
•
Street clothes are worn in these areas and plant dedicated clothing is never worn, including shoes, other
than in an emergency evacuation.

=== PAGE 97 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
2.
Warehousing for product related goods and materials. These are GxP areas. Raw API and intermediates should
be segregated. APIs should be stored to prevent mix-up, cross-contamination, and contamination in the event of
an upset condition.
3.
Controlled areas where gowning/material transfer takes place transitioning between the outside and the core
GxP zones. The warehouse is outside this zone.
4.
GxP corridors are the flow routes through which people and material access work areas within the GxP core.
Corridors should not be product contaminated. The presence of product at or above acceptable limits in non-
product dedicated corridors is a cause for immediate concern. The presence of visible residues is also a cause
for concern.
5.
Product rooms are where a single product is produced on a campaign basis (this may include different dosages).
The process may involve single rooms or suites so long as a barrier to the GxP corridor is provided. The process
room is the final product boundary. The system, open or closed, is located in these rooms. Where fully closed
systems are used, and where control and the prevention of mix-up can be demonstrated, more than one product
may be in a single room on a case by case basis.
6.
Laboratories for analysis and development should be provided with ways to prevent contamination. Restricting
access to the laboratories and preventing worker exposure within the laboratory is suggested.
7.
Technical space houses the utilities used for the facility and process. Adequate safeguards for operations such
as dust collection discharge or air handling filter change should be provided.

*[Figure 9.5 illustrates a facility with alternate arrangements. These arrangements are:]*

Single door to corridor: in this case, the room may be at positive or negative pressure to the corridor. Process
rooms that are positive pressure to the corridor can place the corridor at risk.
Single stage airlock: provides a significant improvement in containment, but may be over or under engineered.
Double stage airlocks: allows clean gowning and de-gowning in separate rooms with features to overcome airborne
and mechanical transfer. In certain situations, this design may represent over-engineering.

#### 9.5.3 Material and People Flows

The design of facilities to provide logical flow routes is complex. One common design is to segregate “clean” and
“dirty” corridors. In this ideal scenario, only “clean” personnel, equipment, and product that is robustly contained
and has acceptable surface concentrations can pass down the “clean” corridor, and only potentially contaminated
personnel, equipment, and product pass down the “dirty” corridor. Personnel pass into decontamination and de-gown
areas, equipment passes to the dirty side of a cleaning station, and product passes into another process room or into
“dirty” side staging areas. The problem of potential mix-up and cross-contamination in the dirty corridor should be
managed. While such a corridor system has merit, in some circumstances it can represent over engineering.
Application of the product boundary decontamination concept for personnel, equipment, and product removes the
need for segregated corridors.
Minimizing personnel and material flows is considered good practice, and for reasons outside the scope of this Guide,
segregation of people and material movement can be required for safety and security reasons.

=== PAGE 98 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

##### 9.5.3.1 Material and Personnel Air Locks

Airlocks may have several purposes, including:
•
To prevent mechanical transfer of product from the product boundary into the GxP corridor
•
To provide a means to maintain pressure cascades
•
To demonstrate entry into a controlled area
•
To prevent contamination of the facility and product by external contaminants
•
To provide an area for gowning/de-gowning
•
To provide an area for operator to don controlled use gowning to prevent mechanical transfer of product to
undergarments.
A key element of any Material Air Lock (MAL) or Personnel Air Lock (PAL) is that there is a clean side and a dirty
side with a physical barrier where the two sides meet. Techniques to gown and de-gown, or to manage movement of
materials through the MAL have to be selected to prevent cross-contamination. The method used for aseptic gowning
is the most common form. The transfer of PPE between clean and dirty side, and the retention of gowning to reduce
cost for a set period should be carefully reviewed to prevent worker exposure and mechanical transfer. Sticky mats
should be used to further prevent mechanical transfer out of the airlocks. Airlocks are frequently operated with a positive
differential pressure to adjacent rooms or corridors to prevent the transit of airborne contamination between these areas.
A MAL requires three zones to minimize the potential for cross-contamination:
1.
Clean
2.
Transit
3.
Dirty
Clean side personnel place material in the designated transit zone. Dirty side personnel collect materials without
entering the transit zone. Decontamination takes place in the dirty zone. Personnel should not transit through a MAL
because of the risk of mechanical transfer from contaminated clothing.

#### 9.5.4 System Failure or Upset

However good the system, occasional failure can occur as a result of human, mechanical, or electrical failure, and
these can lead to exposure. As part of the risk reduction process, the outcome of failure should be addressed to
minimize risk.
In a well-regulated closed processing environment, the only justification for airlocks is to provide redundancy. Since
failures of closed systems consistently show lower exposures than the same events in open operation, and because
monitoring systems will indicate that failure has occurred, it may be possible to make the boundary interfaces much
simpler.
Where open processing of high hazard compounds occurs, this does not apply.

#### 9.5.5 Finishes

All GxP areas should have hard, washable, coved, and crevice free finishes that are sufficiently leak tight to enable
pressure cascades to be created and maintained.

=== PAGE 99 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

#### 9.5.6 Dedicated Principles

One route to prevent retention is to dedicate equipment; this can vary from the dedication of an entire process train to
individual pieces of equipment that may be dedicated because of size and complexity.
Another route is to use disposable product contact parts on a batch or campaign basis. This is a very practical
solution in some cases such as aseptic formulation, but impractical with the current state of technology for many
other processes such as fluid bed drying.
Both dedication and disposable principles can be limited to just the product contact parts so long as the drives and
controls can be located outside the product boundary.

#### 9.5.7 Segregation Principles

If risk cannot be reduced to acceptable levels by dedicating equipment or change parts, it may be necessary to
dedicate or segregate the facility. For the purpose of this Guide, segregation means a facility in which:
•
GxP areas are physically separate
•
HVAC units are separate or arranged to prevent cross-contamination
•
Critical utilities are to be protected by backflow prevention and/or filtration at point of use
•
Access and corridors are separate

### 9.6 HVAC and Environmental Control

The key requirement of life science HVAC is to assure a suitable environment for the product and process with
regard to temperature, humidity, and most importantly, airborne particulate, molecular, and viable cleanliness. This
requirement is in addition to the usual industrial HVAC requirements of making workers comfortable with respect
to temperature, humidity, and objectionable odors, as well as protecting them from hazardous airborne fumes,
vapors, aerosols, and dust. Often, the need to control hazardous compounds complicates environmental control as
cleanliness and containment may require conflicting pressurization schemes.
As product potency increases, the need for control of airborne product/API (to reduce the potential for cross-
contamination) also increases. HVAC systems can provide useful tools to control the migration of airborne
compounds. The effectiveness of HVAC related contamination controls improve as proximity to the product increases,
that is, the closer to the product HVAC controls are applied, the more effective they become. For the purposes of this
Section we will consider three level of increasing proximity, room level, local level and equipment enclosure level.
•
Room Level HVAC
-
Life science room HVAC systems are generally designed to dilute airborne contaminants or cross
contaminants (primarily pharmaceutical powders) by entrainment of these particles in the air streams
which are then extracted from the room for disposal or treatment. The effectiveness of this dilution can be
enhanced via a directional flow of air from the non-product handling part of the room, across the process and
into the extract system to sweep airborne product particles out of the space. Such systems, while effective in
managing contaminants, are susceptible to behavior, technique, and operational failures.
-
This sweeping action can be further augmented by employing local exhaust ventilation which removes
contaminants near their source.

=== PAGE 100 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Local Ventilation
-
Local ventilation provides a “near field” effect, similar to room HVAC, by placing exhaust and/or supply
close to potential emission sources in order to dilute or extract particulate before it enters the larger room.
Since the air being handled near to the source has a higher concentration of particulate, processing of
this air through filters or disposal through exhaust can have a profound effect on ambient airborne particle
concentrations.
-
In order to be most effective, this type of arrangement can be coupled with local exhaust ventilation and
engineering control of the sources of contaminants.
•
Equipment Level Ventilation
-
Equipment level ventilation, such as is provided by an isolator, Restricted Access Barrier System (RABS),
vented balance safety enclosure, or closed equipment can be more effective than local ventilation as it does
not rely solely on air movement to provide control. Instead, these systems tend to provide a combination of
physical barriers encapsulating the process along with HVAC to control the buildup of particulate within the
enclosure.
-
The better the control of particulate escape from the process, the less stringent the requirements for
contaminant and cross contaminant control in the local area or room. In essence closing a process,
or enclosing it in an isolator or barrier system, reduces the size of the critical environment, making the
surrounding room a support area.
This triumvirate of control technologies: primary source control, local extraction and room particulate management,
can provide significant safety and product protection benefits, individually and working in concert with each other.
Application of these complementary controls can be used to safely handle highly hazardous materials that have very
low occupational exposure limits. Room and local HVAC have a role in handling highly hazardous compounds, even
if only to control the intermittent or fugitive emissions from well closed equipment and to protect against airborne
spread of spilled materials.
At the HVAC equipment level, the removal of undesirable airborne contaminants can be achieved through the
disposal of contaminated air, via exhaust, or through the treatment of air to remove particulates. The preferred
method of airborne particulate removal is generally depth filtration.
Filters can provide a reduction of airborne particulate matter, and can also serve to provide an enhanced boundary
to protect against contamination of duct systems/fans and airhandlers, as well as unintended backflow or migration
of active pharmaceutical dust through duct systems in the case of a loss of HVAC. For this reason, filters are often
applied as a terminal installation at the air inlets and outlets from processing rooms.

#### 9.6.1 Once Through versus Recirculation

Recirculated HVAC systems offer energy savings and operational cost benefits over once through systems. The air
recirculated from manufacturing areas is often cleaner and much closer to the desired temperature and humidity
conditions than fresh air. However, recirculation of air from powder handling spaces, especially in multiproduct
concurrent manufacturing facilities, can convey airborne product particles to other areas and may maintain aerosols
in suspension, depending on particle size and room air patterns.
Regulations suggest that if air is to be recirculated, that is reused, when potentially contaminated with airborne
product particles, it should be adequately treated. The definition of adequate treatment is amenable to a risk-based
approach, as described in Section 9.6.2.

=== PAGE 101 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC

#### 9.6.2 Quantitating the Risk of Airborne Cross-Contamination

Control of cross-contamination is primarily a matter of understanding the mass of “product A” which might be carried
into “product B”. For contaminants spread through the air, the process is a matter of understanding the potential
maximum mass of airborne product that may be produced and how much of this mass could be delivered to other
spaces. Further, the process should include steps to evaluate upset cases (e.g., spills) and potential failures of the
abatement equipment (e.g., failure sensitivity evaluation). In this manner, appropriate means of abatement can be
calculated and the risks appropriately addressed (Goldschmidt, 2011 [104]).
The method outlined herein is similar to the method used by Industrial Hygienists, who perform studies to assure
worker safety. In the industrial hygiene approach, the actual quantity of product aerosolized during a working day is
measured and correction factors for personal protective equipment are applied, to assure proper control.
Properly done, a quantitative evaluation of cross-contamination potential provides a more robust understanding
of potential contamination risks, appropriate controls, and verification measures through the life of a facility. The
information generated can be used in investigations, to help event response planning, and other critical management
tasks. The method can also help an organization assure that the resources applied to airborne contamination control
are commensurate with the risk and are neither inadequate nor wasteful.
In performing these evaluations, it is important to understand that regulations and best practices call for abatement
systems to be regularly tested or monitored to assure their effectiveness. This requirement may be addressed by
providing adequate filtration testing, effective monitoring systems (such as pressure monitoring), particle counting,
regular integrity testing, and appropriate alarms.
The first step to any quantitative evaluation is to understand the potential contamination sources. Generally speaking,
the potency of the product and the potential maximum concentration of airborne product drive the need for more
rigorous abatement (up to and including double High Efficiency Particulate Air (HEPA) filtration, closing the system,
or 100% exhaust). The highest particle concentration air streams, such as from dust collectors, and especially when
handling potent products, require the most stringent form of abatement and may not be candidates for recirculation.
Quantitative information on the quantity of product airborne in a processing space is often available from the
Environmental Health and Safety studies mentioned in the preceding Section. These results are generally expressed
in µg/m3, which couples well with the method outlined herein.
To determine the appropriate level of filtration, an assessment of the filters’ effectiveness to arrest particles at various
sizes versus the potential mass and expected particle size distribution of airborne contaminant can be performed.
When particle size distribution is known the data needed to determine the protection factor from filtration is available
as fractional removal efficiencies in twelve particle size ranges from ANSI/ASHRAE Standard 52.2 [105], EN 1822
[105], EN 779 [107], JIS Z 8122 [108], IEST-RP-CC001 [109], or ISO 29463-1 [110] testing performed by filter
manufacturers.
The following tables present an example of the effect of filters on the concentration of airborne product.

This simple analysis shows that a properly installed and maintained Minimum Efficiency Reporting Value (MERV) 15
(~95% ASHRAE) filter can provide a 3 log reduction in contaminants. More importantly, a MERV 11 (~50% ASHRAE)
gives a 1.5 log (20:1) reduction in airborne contamination.

Other factors to consider:
Analysis should always consider failure causes including:

Filter and/or frame leakage
2.
Spill and other upsets which case an excess of airborne API
While small particles (< 10 µ) are of interest in worker safety, due to their greater respirability, large (> 10 µ) particles
are of greater interest in the prevention of cross-contamination. This focus on large particles is due to two factors:
1.
In any heterogeneous dispersion of particles the greatest mass is represented by large particles. This is easily
understood by considering the relationship between mass and volume, as shown in Table 9.6.
