One can convert this volume to mass by applying a specific gravity (density) which is common among
pharmaceutical formulations. In sizing pharmaceutical processing equipment, a specific gravity of 0.6 g/cm³ is
commonly used; however, for this evaluation we will use 1.0 g/cm³ in order to compensate for the greater density
of individual particles of product. This calculation is demonstrated in Table 9.7.
Table 9.7: Expected Particle Mass Calculation
Used with permission from Genesis Engineers, Inc., http://www.geieng.com/.
Note: For this evaluation assume that particles are spherical

=== PAGE 105 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
From these calculations it can be seen that a 10 µ particle is almost 10,000 times heavier than a 0.5 µ particle
and 1,000,000 times heavier than a 0.1 µ particle.
At the example specific gravity, it takes 15,000,000 of these 0.5 µ particles to make a single microgram available
for cross-contamination. By contrast, only 2,000 10 µ particles are required to deliver a single microgram of a
contaminant.
2.
The lower buoyancy of large particles (higher settling rate) makes them more likely to contaminate a product by
falling out of an airstream as its velocity decreases in a production room (see Figure 9.6).

*[Figure 9.6: Particle Settling in Still Air]*

Source: Adapted from Baron, Paul. “Generation and Behavior of Airborne Particles (Aerosols).” Centers for Disease
Control and Prevention (CDC). National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health (NIOSH). Division of Applied
Technology. www.cdc.gov/niosh/topics/aerosols/pdfs/Aerosol_101.pdf.
The greater focus on larger particles is good news when using filtration as a means of reducing risk as even
moderate efficiency filters are quite effective at removing these larger particles.

#### 9.6.3 Directional Airflow and Pressure Regimens

In order to control the migration of product aerosols from one room or area to another, it is important to manage the
airflow between spaces. Two approaches are possible to assure adequate transport of product aerosols in these
airstreams:
The first approach is to entrain the aerosol in a large mass of air, typically moving at 0.2 m/s (40 fpm) or greater. This
approach is typically used in open downflow booths and similar devices, but is not preferred for rooms due to the
large and rapid changes in airflow that are required when doors are opened or closed (typically 400 m/s (800 cfm)).
The second, and more popular, approach is to control the direction of airflow control and assure adequate velocity to
transport particles (typically in the range of 0.5 to 1.0 m/s (1 to 200 fpm)). This technique also has two approaches.
The first is to maintain a fixed offset between air entering and leaving the room via the HVAC system (via fixed
balance or airflow tracking). The second is to measure and possibly control the pressurization of rooms, typically
in steps of 10 to 15 Pa (0.4 to 0.6 in. W.G.), but no lower than 5 Pa (0.02 in. W.G.), to assure proper control. The
high-pressure difference across doors results in very high velocities at all leak points, on the order of 3 to 3.5 m/s
(600 to 700 fpm). These higher velocities easily provide kinetic energy in excess of what is needed to entrain product
aerosols at much lower airflows.

=== PAGE 106 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
ADVANCED TOPIC
The pressure cascade and direction of airflow in a facility should be designed to be protective of product, personnel,
and environment. The principle in this case is to prevent aerosols migrating from area to area. These aerosols may
be contaminants (environmental dirt) or cross contaminants (other drug substance); therefore, the production area
should be positive in pressure with respect to the surrounding environment. However, a positive manufacturing
space can result in the spread of product aerosols, presenting a risk to other products and personnel; therefore open
product handling or manufacturing areas should be negative with respect to other GxP areas that should not be
contaminated, such as the GxP corridor.
The boundary between the production space and the corridor tends to be problematic; single door configurations leading
from the production room to the corridor are challenged when the door is open and the pressure difference vanishes.
To provide greater protection, airlocks with interlocked doors (to assure that one is always closed) can be introduced;
airlocks can have pressure cascades from the corridor (positive) to the production area (negative) to provide control
of aerosols. Alternatively, the process room can be positive to the airlock and the corridor is also positive to the
airlock, forming a sink-type airlock. Airlocks that are positive pressure with respect to the corridor and process room
(bubble-type airlock) have shown greater emission potential in testing than the simple pressure cascade from corridor
to process room.

*[Figure 9.7: Airlock Configurations]*

=== PAGE 107 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 9.7 Containment Related Technologies

Containment related technologies are typically used to make a process step, or a process a closed system, keeping
the product and the room environment separate. Employing containment related technologies can reduce the risk of
cross-contamination by airborne or mechanical transfer routes.

#### 9.7.1 Selection of Appropriate Containment Systems

The equipment selection process should result in individual equipment items or systems of equipment being identified
which give a high confidence that the design target/performance expectation will be met. For successful performance,
an early understanding of the full range of activities, including normal operation, sampling, and cleaning, and to
share this with the full team, including supplier(s) and integrators is important. Planned and breakdown maintenance
activities will be related to the equipment selected and should be discussed at an early stage. The requirement
for some activities, e.g., sampling, should be questioned, as they may present significant difficulties in effective
containment design, and may present an exposure risk that outweighs other benefits. All process activities that might
be considered “nice to have” should be rigorously questioned as to their requirement.
The success of this approach depends upon appropriate information being available for:
•
The detail of all the activities to be undertaken including normal operation, sampling, and cleaning
•
An appreciation of the rate of movement of the particles or the energy with which they may be released
•
The variety of containers in and out of the equipment
•
The way waste and contaminated equipment will be removed from the equipment
•
The need for aids for heavy, awkward, or restricted space lifting
•
Operators understanding of the risks and how this affects their behavior. Behavior can increase or reduce
equipment containment performance with the impact being felt most for local extract and enclosures and least for
closed system and isolators.
•
Understanding of the ergonomics of the activities, especially with repetitive actions, access requirements,
working position, heavier, larger, or awkward loads, and reaching and twisting movements. The range of stature
of the operators should be considered.
•
Knowledge of the containment or emission performance of equipment in similar applications
•
The way containment performance may change with time due to ineffective cleaning and/or wear
•
Understanding of the problem by the design team
•
Is there potential for the powder to ignite or support a dust cloud explosion? This can impact the design as it will
influence the containment controls selected. Contained systems have smaller volumes and low air change rates
and there is a risk of achieving explosive concentrations. In some cases, the contained system can be made
inert by using nitrogen.
•
To dissipate electrostatic buildup, all metal parts should be grounded (earthed), including elements docked and
insulated by plastic wear elements.

=== PAGE 108 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 9.8 Personal Protective Equipment and Gowning

Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) refers to gowns, gloves, booties, hair covers, hoods, respiratory protection,
face masks, etc., whereas Respiratory Protective Equipment (RPE) covers devices designed only to protect the
mouth and nose. The distinction is important because PPE, without RPE, is recommended for protecting the product
from contamination and cross-contamination as well as for dermal protection for the operators at all times.
Whenever RPE is used it is important to consider the use, storage, maintenance, and decontamination of the RPE,
and whether or not it could lead to a risk of cross-contamination from mechanical transfer.
PPE is normally worn to:
•
To help limit mechanical transfer of materials
•
Provide appropriate outer clothing to protect the product, e.g., made of non-shedding material
•
Provide a psychological and physical indication of transfer from the outside environment into a GxP facility where
specific procedures, rules of conduct, and action apply
•
Provide dermal and chemical protection to the operator so that any drug exposure related effects are minimized

=== PAGE 109 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
10	 Risk Acceptance
ICH Q9 [1] states that risk acceptance (see Figure 10.1) is a decision to accept a defined level of risk. This can be
a formal documented decision process with residual risks specified or a more informal process where the residual
risk is not specified. The documentation and the acceptable residual risk should be commensurate with the risk.
Acceptance of residual risk based on the health-based limits described in this Guide should provide adequate
protection of both patient and the worker.

*[Figure 10.1: Risk Acceptance]*

Where risk reduction strategies are implemented in order to get to a risk acceptance decision, the manufacturer
should be able to demonstrate to all stakeholders that the risk reduction strategies are effective in maintaining cross-
contamination and worker exposure at or below acceptable limits.
This requires demonstration that:
•
Procedures and detection devices are in place to minimize the potential for mix-up even in the case of accident
or error
•
Any residues on product contact and nonproduct contact surfaces fall within the health-based limits in support of
cleaning processes

=== PAGE 110 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Flow of personnel, product, material, and HVAC are designed to minimize the potential for mechanical transfer
•
Any product that may escape the process is maintained below levels that could impact the potential for cross-
contamination and worker safety

=== PAGE 111 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
11	 Risk Management Tools
Risk can be assessed and managed using risk management tools (see Figure 11.1). The level of effort or the
tools used should be based on the level of potential risk and knowledge of the process. It is important that each
manufacturer set standards for risk assessment tools including, but not limited to, the types of tools to be used, when
to use certain tools, defining the categories, setting acceptable risk levels, action levels, etc. It is essential that the
numbers or designation are carefully chosen and defined and are not modified because the outcome is unwelcome.

*[Figure 11.1: Risk Management Tools]*

ICH Q9 [1] refers to a number of risk assessment tools that may be used. Organizations should determine which tools
they wish to use or they can create custom tools:
•
Basic risk management facilitation methods (flowcharts, check sheets, etc.)
•
Failure Mode and Effects Analysis (FMEA)
•
Failure Mode, Effects, and Criticality Analysis (FMECA)
•
Fault Tree Analysis (FTA)
•
Hazard Analysis and Critical Control Points (HACCP)
•
Hazard Operability Analysis (HAZOP)

=== PAGE 112 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Preliminary Hazard Analysis (PHA)
•
Risk ranking and filtering
•
Supporting statistical tools
The tools shown in this Chapter are examples that can be used, but ideally these analytical tools should be
developed and used by a team of stakeholders working together.

### 11.1 Risk Ranking or Risk Matrix

Qualitative risk assessments may be performed at manufacturing sites for the purpose of determining which unit
operations pose the greatest risk or the greatest opportunity. A risk ranking (see Figure 11.2) or risk matrix (see
Figure 11.3) provides a very simple high level objective process to consistently evaluate all the unit operations at a
manufacturing site and rank the operations by risk.
To apply the risk ranking, all the unit operations (i.e., manufacturing processes, compression room, charging a
reactor, discharge from a dryer, etc.) at a site are identified. To complete the ranking, the following parameters should
be considered:
•
Health Effects: ADE value, residue levels for cleaning, inactivation, etc.
•
Product Considerations: percent active in batch, batch size, and product form which will impact API becoming
airborne and maximum daily dosage
•
Operational Considerations: number of employees involved, frequency of the process, energy input, open or
closed processing, surface area of product contact parts, batches per year, relative positions of source and at
risk materials, and duration of process

*[Figure 11.2: Risk Ranking]*

For each parameter a numerical value, or high, medium, and low indicator is assigned, and for each unit operation,
the scores are totaled. After all unit operations have been assessed, the risk rankling scores can be compared and
activities prioritized based on an assigning system.

=== PAGE 113 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
11.2 Cause and Effect Diagram (Fishbone Diagram)
Use the Ishikawa Fishbone diagram to brainstorm how the four modes of cross-contamination can occur. In addition,
a second diagram can be used to identify how each of the four modes of cross-contamination can be or are
controlled. This can be a helpful precursor to more detailed analysis or data gathering exercises.

*[Figure 11.4: Fishbone Diagram]*

Examples of Unit Operation Risk Ranking Airborne Concentration
High (H): sampling, weighing and dispensing, sample testing (analytical), charging, milling, granulation, extruding
(cleaning), compounding
Medium (M): mixing, blending, extruding, compression, capsule filling
Low (L): coating, charging, drilling, printing, packaging
Negligible (N): membrane coating, discharging cores, drying, pouching, gel dispensing, curing, assembling,
inspection

*[Figure 11.3: Sample Risk Matrix Format]*

=== PAGE 114 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

### 11.3 Failure Mode and Effects Analysis

Many pharmaceutical companies use FMEA techniques for risk ranking and have standard protocols for its use. It is
used in Six Sigma evaluations and can be used for a wide range of risk assessments.
FMEA was originally developed by the US Military in the 1940s and further developed by the automotive and
aerospace industries. Where companies do not have a formally documented system, it is recommended that they use
a recognized system such as in IEC 60812 [111]. Customization is usually necessary.
An example modified form is presented in Table 11.1. The changes to the standard form include the addition of a
column to indicate facility so that the location of the process is identified and the potential failure mode is split into
mode and potential failure.
Table 11.1: Sample FMEA Form Headings
Facility
Process Step
Potential Failure
Potential Effect(s) of Failure
Severity
Potential Causes
Occurence
Current Controls
Detection
Risk Priority Number (RPN)
Actions
Who
By Date
Results
Severity
Occurrence
Detection
Risk Priority Number (RPN)
The column headings are as follows:
Column Heading
Description
Facility
Descriptors of the facility in which the process takes place including:
•	 Room or suite in which the process occurs
•	 HVAC details such as filtration, pressurization regimen, recirculated, or once through air
•	 Air locks (material and/or personnel)
•	 Corridor
•	 Access control
Process Step
Starting at receipt and sampling and ending with dispatch, each process or the process that
is the subject of the evaluation is identified, as are the controls to mitigate exposure and
risk. Each process in the same facility zone (room or suite) should be captured.
Typical processes are sampling, subdivision (pharmacy), formulation, charging, granulation,
milling, blending, filtration, compression, coating, filling, lyophilization, etc.
Potential Failure
The potential failure is listed. Here the primary categories are the means by which exposure
occurs for each mode type. For example, mix-up may be due to human error.
The critical issue is product exposure. If product exposure is controlled at source, then
occupational exposure (subject to a case-by-case evaluation) has been addressed. If
product exposure is controlled by dedication and segregation, then a quantitative or
qualitative assessment of occupational exposure is required if the occupational exposure is
not controlled to the OEL.

=== PAGE 115 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Column Heading
Description
Potential Failure
(continued)
For the patient, the differences in population and route of delivery should be taken into
account. Occupational exposures may be based on inhalation whereas the patient delivery
may be parenteral. The route may be beneficial or detrimental to the assessment and the
bioavailability of the different routes.
Potential Effect(s)
of Failure
A listing of effects of failure on the worker and patient populations. In the case of cross-
contamination, the effects of failure are the four possible exposure modes for cross-
contamination (mix-up, retention, mechanical transfer, and airborne transfer). For each
process step and stage, review the four modes. Each mode is analyzed on a new row. For
occupational exposure the effects of failure are the possible exposure modes (inhalation,
dermal, mucous membranes, skin exposure).
Note: for each facility element there may be multiple process steps. For each process step,
there may be multiple potential failure modes and for each failure mode, there may be
multiple potential effects. For each potential effect, there should be responses and values
for all the remaining columns up to the first Risk Priority Number (RPN) which is the value
of the severity, occurrence, and detection columns multiplied by each other (Severity ×
Occurrence × Detection = RPN).
Severity
Based on numeric values, the severity of the risk is assessed. The examples in this Guide
use 1, 3, 5, 7, and 10. A sample table to guide evaluations is given (see Table 11.2).
Potential Causes
List causes of the failure. There may be multiple causes in which case the effect of failure
and the severity is repeated.
Occurrence
Value based on the actual or assessed rate of occurrence (see Table 11.2).
Current Controls
Identify the current control method by which the event is controlled if at all, again this field
should be filled in.
Detection
Using the table, the ability to detect failure is recorded. If the failure is not detectable, the
score is 10 and if the failure is obvious the score is 1 (see Table 11.2).
RPN
This is the product of severity, occurrence, and detection.
This number generates two factors:
Factor 1: Risk assessment. Each manufacturer should set zones of acceptability for RPNs.
The zones should include scores that are satisfactory. A zone that includes a range of
scores that is increasingly marginally satisfactory. A zone where the range of scores is
borderline and requires review and remediation. A zone where the score shows a failure
and immediate remediation is required. The last zone is where the any score in this range
should result in discontinuation until remediation is complete. For an example, see Figure
11.4.
Factor 2: Risk prioritization. By ranking the numeric values, a prioritization can be created
so the highest number equates with the most important issue to correct.
The FMEA form can have additional columns to list remediation control and evaluate the
completed remediation.
Actions
Lists controls (engineering, administrative, procedural, and formulation) that can be
implemented to reduce the RPN to within the acceptable range.
Who
Identifies the project manager/responsible person who will implement the action.
By Date
Sets a goal date for the remediation to be complete. The date will be driven by the RPN and
other factors.

=== PAGE 116 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Column Heading
Description
Results
This allows the outcome to be listed, preferably by quantitative evaluation.
Severity
Based on the same numeric values, the severity of the risk is assessed after the
remediation is complete.
Occurrence
Value based on the actual or assessed rate of occurrence after the remediation is complete.
Detection
Using the table, the ability to detect failure after the remediation is complete is recorded. If
the failure is not detectable, the score is 10 and if the failure is obvious the score is 1.
RPN
Finally the severity, occurrence, and detection are reevaluated. If the RPN is within the
criteria of acceptability, then the process is complete other than routine performance
monitoring. If the value is above the zone of acceptability, then the remediation would be
subject to a rework until an acceptable value is achieved.

#### 11.3.1 Scoring Severity, Occurrence, and Acceptance

Table 11.2 shows one approach to scoring. Severity is the impact of exposure to the target population, either the
patient or the occupational population. The definitions require some interpretation, and for this reason, the FMEA
should be carried out by a team.
Each score should be carefully evaluated, e.g., in the Severity column, if possible outcome due to the hazard is
death, then the score should be the highest value, regardless of other mitigating factors. In the Occurrence column,
mitigating factors can be taken into account, for instance, if the potential cross contaminant and the target are
separated by campaign, then this may on evaluation reduce the occurrence significantly, finally Detection is scored
based on the ability to detect that exposure has occurred or that a control system has failed.
For each line, the Severity, Occurrence, and Detection are multiplied together S × O × D = Risk Priority Number (RPN).
Table 11.2: Sample FMEA Scoring
RPN Value
Severity
Occurrence
Detection
Injury to a patient or employee;
ADE < 1 µg/day
More than once per batch
Not detectable by current
methods
Cause extreme customer
dissatisfaction; ADE = 1 > 10 μg/day
Once per batch
All manually inspected
Something likely to result in a
complaint; ADE = 10 > 100 µg/day
Once per six months
Statistical sampling
Minor nuisance resulting in no loss;
ADE = 100 > 1000 µg/day
Once every one to three
years
100% inspection
Be unnoticed and not affect
performance; ADE ≥ 1000 µg/day
One occurrence in greater
than five years
Obvious or controlled and
monitored and alarmed by
control system
The possible scores in this example range from 1 (1 × 1 × 1 = 1) to 1,000 (10 × 10 × 10 = 1,000). Figure 11.4 shows
the range of possible results using this format. Each organization should determine the scoring systems to be used
in FMEAs. These scoring systems should be consistent across the organization and from project to project to help
establish the quality system throughout the organization. Scoring or RPNs should be viewed as a general indicator/
aid for risk ranking and not treated as an absolute indicator of risk. Caution should be used when prioritizing risks
or areas for remediation on just the RPN number. The RPN numbers represent at best a qualitative relative risk

=== PAGE 117 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
relationship and even items with the same RPN number may not be considered equal in terms of actual risk. For
example, a process step that has 10 for severity, 10 for occurrence, and 1 for detection does not really have the same
level of risk as a process step that has 10 for severity, 1 for occurrence, and 10 for detection although they both have
the same RPN. One method to address the possible inequalities of RPNs is to require review of any item (severity,
occurrence, or detection) that has the highest value regardless of the resultant RPN.
Detectability is a vital issue, if reliable means exist to detect a failure, then increased occurrence may be tolerable;
if the event cannot be detected, then the occurrence and severity have to be low and in order to determine if
severity and occurrence are low additional data gathering studies may be necessary. In conducting FMEA analysis,
detectability has the same impact as severity and occurrence.

*[Figure 11.4: Range of Possible Results]*

### 11.4 Statistical Analysis

Statistical analysis is used to analyze quantitative data to identify trends, variations and likelihood processes
will provide the desired results. Typical outputs of statistical analysis are Process Capability (Cpk) and Process
Performance Indices (Ppk). Statistical analysis capabilities are available in Excel or software packages such as
Minitab. It is very important that the statistical analysis of data including interpretation of output from the software
packages noted is completed by a properly qualified statistician or similar.
ADVANCED TOPIC

=== PAGE 118 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

=== PAGE 119 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
12	 Risk Review
ICH Q9 [1] states risk management should be an ongoing (see Figure 12.1) part of the quality management process.
A mechanism to review internal processes and external events should be implemented.

*[Figure 12.1: Risk Review]*

Periodic reviews should be performed on all aspects of the risk management system to ensure that it is being applied
effectively and that any potential improvements are identified and incorporated into the process. This should include
a periodic review of the ADE, especially if established early in the drug development timeline, as new information is
being gathered from preclinical and clinical studies on a continuous basis. Any manufacturing deviations or system
changes that could impact the potential for exposure (product or personnel) or might compromise the conclusions
drawn from previous risk assessments should be closely reviewed. The system may need to be modified so that
it does not present an increased potential for exposure. Any review or, where necessary, reassessment should be
documented accordingly.
An organization’s quality management processes also should consider need for any reassessment to ensure
that original assumptions and performance data remain valid. This may include or require auditing of processes
and activities, and qualitative or quantitative monitoring of performance. Any change to the risk profile should be
communicated as described in risk communication.

=== PAGE 120 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,

=== PAGE 121 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
13	 Risk Communication
ICH Q9 [1] defines risk communication (see Figure 13.1) as the sharing of information about risk and risk management
between the decision makers and others. Organizations should document, within their quality management systems,
who needs to communicate what, to whom, and at what frequency, in support of a risk management process.

*[Figure 13.1: Risk Communication]*

Different levels of information will be appropriate to different stakeholders who may be internal or external to the
organization. It is important to have an escalation process that allows management to be informed of areas that have
risk that is unacceptable or near unacceptable. The holistic approach that is described in this Guide is predicated
on the fact that all of those involved have received appropriate education or training and have ready access to
supporting risk assessment and management documentation.
Risk Communication can be enhanced by incorporating linkages within the change control, product quality review and
CAPA quality systems.
It is important that companies have a means to widely and effectively communicate to all parties that might be
affected including company manufacturing sites, contract manufacturers and QA departments any identified
amendments to ADEs (and OELs) or other items that may affect the risk assessment. In addition if a company
chooses to have product-specific ADEs (where the following product is known) or ADEs for specific routes of
exposure or dosage types (such as OSD or parenteral) rather than address these issues in the risk assessment, the
company should have a robust process to manage these values and make clear to all that may depend on these
values what the criteria used to determine the ADEs are and how they should be modified for other usage.

=== PAGE 122 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products

in the facility.]

Appendix 1
14	 Appendix 1 – Risk-MaPP Application

Examples

### 14.1 Introduction

The examples provided in this Guide are intended to illustrate four scenarios showing the application of the Risk-
MaPP tools. Examples were selected to illustrate suboptimal facility and process configurations. This is intended
to demonstrate how a well conducted Quality Risk Management Plan (QRMP) can be used. None of the examples
should be seen as setting a precedent, nor are they a benchmark of an acceptable standard. The examples are
intended simply to illustrate that when science and risk-based approaches are applied diligently, facilities and
processes that seem to present unacceptable levels of patient risk can consistently deliver product within defined
margins for patient safety.
If the risk assessment is for a new product being brought into an existing facility, the QRMP should be part of the
change control process.
There are four scenarios which can be summarized as:
•
Scenario 1: Obviously Acceptable
•
Scenario 2: Apparently Acceptable (but was not)
•
Scenario 3: Apparently Unacceptable (but was)
•
Scenario 4: Existing facility with a large product portfolio
To make comparisons easier, examples 1 to 3 use the same basic facility layout, processes, and existing product
range. The API being introduced varies between each scenario.
Table 14.1 lists the compounds that are currently manufactured in the facility.
Table 14.1: Compounds Currently Manufactured in the Facility
Compound

Wt API/Batch (kg)
Daily Dose (mg)

2,000
Anti-hypertensive
0.5
NSAID
The ADEs were set by a qualified toxicologist.
The compounds being introduced in each scenario are:
•
Scenario 1: Another Anti-hypertensive
•
Scenario 2: Anti-neoplastic
•
Scenario 3: Sex hormone
A steering committee was set up of the stakeholders including management, legal, regulatory, quality, operations,
validation and cleaning specialists, engineering and EHS, and with significant input from toxicology.

14.2 Scenario 1 (Obviously Acceptable)
In this example, the owner wishes to introduce an anti-hypertensive product into the facility. The owner has an
excellent history of compliance with the agencies that cover the territories in which the products are sold.
After the FMEA review, the final version of a QRMP summary was produced to show how ICH Q9 [1] had been
applied to minimize or prevent risk to product/patient.
Quality Risk Management Plan for Technology Transfer and Commercialization of AntitenseTM
(An Anti-hypertensive)
Overview
The document covers the introduction of AntitenseTM into an existing multiproduct manufacturing facility.
Detailed material to support the statements made are available for review (not included in this example).
A formalized risk management approach was applied to the Kingswood, CT manufacturing facility. This involved a
holistic assessment that identified the potential hazards and risks to product and operators for all products handled in
the facility to ensure that appropriate controls were in place to manufacture these products safely. The assessment
supported the development of a Quality Risk Management Plan (QRMP) that addressed the manufacture of the
following products: antacid, anti-hypertensive, and Nonsteroidal Anti-inflammatory Drug (NSAID).
A cornerstone of the QRMP is the establishment of Acceptable Daily Exposure (ADE) values for the active ingredients
handled in the manufacturing facility. See Table 14.2. The ADEs serve as the starting point for the rest of the risk
assessment process as described by ICH Q9 [1], which ultimately leads to risk controls sufficient to provide adequate
protection of workers handling the compound(s) and patients.

Standard Operating Procedures, Personnel, Validation Plans
The facility has an excellent history of compliance with the agencies that cover the territories in which the products
are sold.
For a listing of SOPs and Validation Plans, refer to document SOPLIST (not included in example).
Risk Assessment
Risk assessment consists of the identification of the hazards and the analysis and evaluation of risks associated with
exposure to those hazards.
A risk assessment process was used to derive criteria to support decision making and control of risks to patient
via product cross-contamination. The risk assessment involved an evaluation of all data relevant to support hazard
identification, assessment of potency (dose-response) and severity, exposure assessment, and characterization of
risk. The ultimate decision on the appropriate controls implemented relied on both qualitative and quantitative data.
Risk Identification
Hazard Identification and Dose-Response Assessment
A review of all available data on each API (e.g., the data included in the Clinical Investigators Brochure or New Drug
Application or the scientific literature) was completed to identify all possible adverse effects that may be produced
by the compound. For each compound, the critical effect, i.e., the first adverse effect observed at the low end of the
dose-response curve, was identified. The ADE value was then derived by dividing the no effect level for the critical
endpoint by appropriate adjustment (uncertainty or safety) factors. The ADE value was used to derive product contact
surface cleaning target values. In this case, additional calculations were made to reflect important characteristics
of the process such as batch size, product contact equipment surface area, and maximum daily dose of the next
product, to arrive at the final surface target value or rinse concentration to support cleaning validation.
Establishment of an Acceptable Daily Exposure for AntitenseTM
An Acceptable Daily Exposure (ADE) value of 0.3 mg/day was established for the new anti-hypertensive agent called
AntitenseTM. The rationale for this ADE recommendation is summarized in the quality monograph dated DD-MMM-
YYYY (not included in example) that supports the company’s cleaning validation program. Briefly, the ADE was based
on the low clinical dose of 10 mg and incorporates appropriate adjustment factors to address interindividual variability
(AFH = 10) and extrapolation to a no effect level (AFL = 3).
Risk Analysis
A review of monitoring data for the existing products and processes was completed to ensure the facility can safely
manufacture the products.
Risk Evaluation
The analysis quickly showed that the new compound posed less risk than some compounds already in manufacture,
for which data existed showing that they posed an acceptably low level of risk to patient or products.
Summary
The new anti-hypertensive has an ADE that is slightly lower than the current anti-hypertensive agent which is
manufactured under existing QRMP No. 170129. The new anti-hypertensive can be cleaned to acceptable levels
using the current cleaning protocols and methods. The introduction of this new anti-hypertensive poses no additional
risk of patient exposure.

Segregation Philosophy
Following the FMEA analysis, changes were made to the facility to protect the existing products from cross-
contamination by the proposed anti-neoplastic. The philosophy behind these changes is summarized below.
The processes were closed by the retrofitting of engineering control methods. API handling for sampling and
subdivision takes place in an isolator for anti-neoplastic Agent C. Closed transfer using bins and split butterfly valves
was introduced for all transfers for all compounds.
Other engineering controls were introduced to processes showing risk of product exposure. The addition of fan
assisted safe change room boundary filtration has been introduced to reduce the risk of distribution of fugitive API by
the HVAC system to an acceptable level.
Additional gowning has been introduced. Operators wear disposable coveralls, bootees, and hair nets in rooms where
anti-neoplastic Agent C is being processed. This gowning is removed at the room boundary. This is an additional
precautionary measure intended for an upset condition.
Access to process rooms is controlled by electronic verification. Only the correct materials and personnel are allowed
to enter. The cleaning bay has been divided into clean and dirty with a pass through for equipment. Personnel are
segregated between clean and dirty side. Gowning to prevent cross-contamination is in place for dirty side personnel.
Anti-neoplastic Agent C equipment is subjected to a closed in place clean, before leaving the process room.
All walls, floors, and ceilings are of hard washable coved construction.
Material flows are controlled by electronic verification allowing separation in time between material transits in the
corridor. Movement of more than one compound at a time with a 1 minute dwell is enforced by the bar code readers

=== PAGE 131 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Appendix 1
Process and Manufacturing Equipment Description
The range of operations and equipment used includes:
•
Sampling of incoming API in an isolator with a sample sent to the QA lab in a closed transfer device; Rapid
Transfer Port (RTP).
•
Weighing to batch weight of the API in an isolator closed transfer in a bin with a Split Butterfly Valve (SBV).
•
High shear granulation and wet milling prior to charging to a fluid bed dryer. The active is layered into the
granulator and the heel retention is very high, requiring manual scraping for yield. Process conducted in an
isolator, transfer in a closed bin to the next process.
•
Discharge into the fluid bed dryer bowl in an isolator from the transfer device referenced above. The fluid bed
dryer is a closed operation: vacuum discharge to an isolator with a hammer type mill, in an isolator, collection in a
weigh hopper, and vacuum transfer to a bin via an SBV.
•
Bin blend or bin to V-Shell transfer using transfer valves and bins.
•
Lubricant is added via the transfer valve on the side arm of the V-shell or via the charge using SBV to a bin for
products requiring compression.
•
The V-Cone® is discharged into SBV bins or the blending bin is used without transfer. The bins are tested for
blend uniformity using PAT.
•
Products that are tableted are passed to compression with the bin being connected via an SBV.
•
Products B and C are coated; A is not. Data shows no risk of exposure.
•
Products are bottle or blister packed.
Following the steps in the logic diagram (see Section 14.6), manufacture of the compound was possible.

Risk Assessment
Risk assessment consists of the identification of the hazards and the analysis and evaluation of risks associated with
exposure to those hazards.
A risk assessment process was used to derive criteria to support decision making and control of risks to product and
workers. The risk assessment involved an evaluation of all data relevant to support hazard identification, assessment
of potency (dose-response) and severity, exposure assessment, and characterization of risk. The ultimate decision on
the appropriate controls implemented relied on both qualitative and quantitative data. Risk controls were implemented
in a manner that is commensurate with the risk.
Risk Identification
A review of all available data on each API was completed to identify all possible adverse effects that may be produced
by the compound. For each compound, the critical effect, i.e., the first adverse effect observed at the low end of the
dose-response curve, was identified. The ADE value was then derived by dividing the no-effect level for the critical
endpoint by appropriate adjustment (uncertainty or safety) factors or to extrapolate to an acceptable level of cancer
risk. The ADE value was used to derive product contact surface cleaning target values.
Establishment of an Acceptable Daily Exposure (ADE) for Anti-neoplastic Agent C
Anti-neoplastic Agent C is an alkylating agent used in the treatment of numerous myeloproliferative and
lymphoproliferative disorders and solid malignancies. It disrupts nucleic acid function by interfering with DNA
replication and transcription of RNA. The recommended oral dosage for treatment of various cancers ranges from 1
to 5 mg/kg/day for adults or 10 to 15 mg/kg, administered intravenously every 7 to 10 days.
Anti-neoplastic Agent C is considered moderately toxic orally and produced adverse developmental effects in
animals at dosages as low as 2.5 mg/kg/day. It was shown to damage DNA in spermatozoa from adult male rats and
increased the incidence of malignant tumors in rats and mice by several routes of administration. The compound also
has been shown to be a human carcinogen based on studies from patients using the drug for treatment of cancer.
The critical endpoint for deriving a health-based value for Anti-neoplastic Agent C is cancer. Because it is a direct
acting genotoxic material, the ADE value was derived by extrapolating cancer risks from the animal and human tumor
data. Using these data, an ADE of 1 µg/day was calculated which corresponds to a 1 × 10-5 excess cancer risk. No
adverse effects on organ systems or the developing fetus are expected at this level of exposure (i.e., protecting
against cancer should protect against all other adverse effects).
Risk Analysis
Monitoring data were collected in the manufacturing facility to assess residual levels of each API on both internal
and external equipment surfaces and surfaces in the manufacturing area(s) and adjoining non-production areas.
These values were compared to the ADEs established for these compounds to characterize the risks to patients and
workers.
Risk Ranking Techniques and Exposure Assessment
Qualitative exposure assessments were used to provide an initial estimate of exposure and to prioritize quantitative
exposure assessment activities.

Plausible Pathways
Assessments were made of the following plausible pathways:
Mix-up:
•
Bar code reading control of all equipment and materials where they pass into the processing rooms
•
Separation of clean and dirty equipment by room
•
Tagging of clean and dirty equipment
Retention Product Contact Surfaces:
•
Swab monitoring of cleaning performance against the limit procedures were set up based on health-based limits
•
Visually clean inspection
•
Routine monitoring shows consistent performance well below the health-based limit set by scientific evaluation, a
synopsis of performance against limit is shown in Figure 14.6.
•
Retention non-product contact surfaces on a campaign and product change out basis; verified by swab testing
•
Dedication of the blender bin
Mechanical Transfer:
•
Critical transfers are closed
•
All equipment and materials are wiped down or bagged before leaving the process room
•
Tack mats are used at room boundaries
Airborne Transfer:
•
All processes occur in rooms protected by airlocks from the corridor, the doors are interlocked and a dwell period
for concentration reduction is provided
•
The corridor is positive in pressure to the process room so that airflow flows into the air locks when the corridor
door is open
•
Exhausts are protected by redundant HEPA filtration (two passes through 99.997% filters with pressure
differentials indicated and alarmed. Routine challenge testing is performed.
•
For product to cross contaminate another product, it has to:
-
Escape the process
-
Pass though into the airlocks, against a low velocity flow from the airlock toward the room
-
Pass from the airlock which is purged before the door is released into the corridor against a low velocity flow
from the corridor into the airlock
-
Travel into the corridor

Cleaning Review
Cleaning Program Description: the Cleaning Program for this facility is comprised of a Cleaning Process
Development and Cleaning Process Risk Evaluation for all cleaning processes used in the facility. The activities for
these steps involve:
Cleaning Process Development:
•
Cleanability assessments
•
Cleaning agent(s) selection
•
Design of cleaning processes
•
FMEAs for each cleaning process
•
Modification of cleaning processes to reduce risks of cleaning process failures based on results of the FMEAs
Cleaning Risk Evaluation:
•
Collection of swab sample data on each cleaning procedure
•
Process capability evaluations of swab sample data
•
FMEAs of overall cleaning program to identify high risk cleaning procedures
•
Improvement/optimization of high risk cleaning procedures to reduce risks
Cleaning Process Review: a cleanability assessment was performed on the product and the product was found to
be easily cleaned by the existing processes. The cleanability rank was a 2, with a rank of 4 for the hardest to clean
product. The current cleaning agents were found to be appropriate for solubilizing and suspending the product for
effective cleaning. The designs of the Cleaning Processes were also found to be appropriate for cleaning this type of
product (oral solid dosage).
Risk Evaluation
New Product Review: the new anti-neoplastic product was evaluated against the existing program for its compatibility
with the cleaning processes and its ADE was assessed against the existing swab sampling database for the facility.
The FMEA analysis of cleaning showed a significant risk occurring in the V-blender which, due to its significant
surface area, required a level of residue significantly below the historically achievable results.

=== PAGE 138 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Cleaning Risk Evaluation Review: the existing swab sample database was statistically evaluated against the new
product’s ADE to determine the level of risk presented by the ability of the cleaning processes to clean the new
product. The swab data for all cleaning processes were found to have high Process Performance Indices (Ppk) with
undetectable levels Defects per Million Opportunities (ppm) calculated with the exception of the blender. The Ppk
for the blender was 0.34 with the potential for Defects per Million Opportunities of 14,994. This evaluation indicated
that the current cleaning process for the blender is not satisfactory to safely clean the new product. The results of the
evaluation of the blender data can be seen in Figure 14.6.

*[Figure 14.6: Process Performance Capability of Hypothetical Cleaning Process]*

In order to introduce the new product into the facility, the cleaning process for the blender would need to be improved
or a new blender would have to be purchased.
Risk Control
In the risk control step, a conclusion was made that the risks to patients with the existing manufacturing process was
unacceptably high. This was due to the potential carryover of residuals of Anti-neoplastic Agent C to other products
from the V-blender.
A Design of Experiment (DoE) was performed to improve the Blender Cleaning Process. Cleaning agent
concentration, cleaning time, cleaning temperature, and rinse time were evaluated. The new data indicate that the
blender cannot be effectively cleaned to safe levels for the new product. The introduction of a dedicated blender was
not possible and so the proposal was abandoned and the anti-neoplastic will not be manufactured at this site.
Risk Communication
No further communication on the proposed introduction of Anti-neoplastic Agent C is required because the proposal
was rejected.

Appendix 1
Risk Review
No additional review required.
Summary
The stakeholder team has used the range of tools proposed in ICH Q9 [1] and this Guide to carefully evaluate the
facility. During the initial qualitative analysis, deficiencies were identified and actions were implemented to mitigate
these deficiencies. An evaluation team spanning relevant disciplines ensured that all hazards and exposure pathways
were assessed and controls maintain the risks at acceptable levels.
The FMEA analysis of cleaning showed a significant risk occurring in the V-blender which due to its significant surface
area required a level of residue significantly below the historically achievable results. Since the other processes
required a V-blender and no space was available for a dedicated blender, the owner decided not to manufacture Anti-
neoplastic Agent C.
14.4 Scenario 3 (Apparently Unacceptable (but was))
In this example, the owner wishes to introduce a sex hormone into the facility. Sex hormones have been under
increased scrutiny because of the risk of effects on fertility and birth defects. The owner has an excellent history of
compliance with the agencies that cover the territories in which the products are sold.
A steering committee was set up of the stakeholders including management, legal, regulatory, and quality, operations,
validation and cleaning specialist, engineering and Environment, Health, and Safety (EHS), with input from toxicology.
The first task of the committee was to conduct a risk analysis, beginning with the logic diagram (see Section 14.6)
followed by the use of FMEA tools. All stakeholders took part in these exercises.
As soon as the committee had assembled sufficient data, they sought informal discussion with the regulatory
agencies and discussed the proposal in concept. Supporting scientific data was made available and a draft of the
QRMP document was tabled.
Following the logic diagram, segregation was possibly required for this product because it is being supplied to a
country that has historically required segregation for sex hormones. An FMEA analysis was then undertaken with a
team of stakeholders, this technique was chosen because it addresses a wide range of ICH Q9 [1] topics, including:
•
Risk identification
•
Risk ranking (using the RPN scores)
•
Exposure control assessment
•
Risk analysis
After the FMEA review, the final version of the QRMP was produced to show how ICH Q9 [1] had been applied to
minimize or prevent risk to product and operators.

Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Quality Risk Management Plan for Technology Transfer and Commercialization of Sex Hormone Agent H
Overview
This document summarizes the QRMP for XYZ Pharma at the Kingswood, CT site. The plan embraces the principles
of ICH Q9 [1] and uses risk management tools to support manufacturing strategies designed to minimize risks to
product and operators.
The proposal is to introduce a sex hormone Agent H into the facility. The existing compounds all have QRMPs in
place. Detailed materials to support the statements made are available for review (not included in this example).
A formalized risk management approach was applied to the Kingswood, CT manufacturing facility. This involved a
holistic assessment that identified the potential hazards and risks to product and operators for all products handled in
the facility to ensure that appropriate controls were in place to manufacture these products safely. The assessment
supported the development of a QRMP that addressed the manufacture of the following the products listed in Table
14.4.
A cornerstone of the QRMP is the establishment of ADE values for the active ingredients handled in the
manufacturing facility. The ADE values serve as the starting point for the rest of the risk assessment process as
described by ICH Q9 [1], which ultimately leads to risk controls sufficient to prove adequate protection of workers
handling the compound(s) and patients.
Table 14.4: Scenario 3 – Values for Active Ingredients Handled in the Manufacturing Facility
The ADE values were set by a qualified toxicologist.
Facility Description
The facility under review is a single corridor facility with access from a GMP gowning area for personnel and a
material airlock to a warehouse, in which a pharmacy and sampling area is located. See Figure 14.7. Each of the unit
processes is located off the common corridor with both MALs and PALs; both are single chamber and are pressurized
to create a pressure cascade between the processing area and the corridor. A suite of rooms are provided for dirty
side wash, clean side dry, and separate clean storage of equipment. The area is served by a single Air Handling Unit
(AHU) with a positive pressure corridor. Air is supplied on a once through basis (no recirculation). Room boundary
redundant filtration is provided to protect the ductwork and technical plant. Up to two compounds are processed at
one time.

Segregation Philosophy
Following the FMEA analysis, changes were made to the facility to protect the existing products from cross-
contamination by the proposed sex hormone. The philosophy behind these changes is summarized below.
•
The sex hormone API processes were closed. Surrogate testing showed that all other exposures provided an
acceptable level of risk and that the plausible pathways had sufficient redundancy to be protective of other
products.
•
Additional gowning has been introduced. Operators wear disposable coveralls, bootees, and hair nets in rooms
where the sex hormone is being processed. This gowning is removed at the room boundary.
•
Access to process rooms is controlled by electronic verification; only the correct materials and personnel are
allowed to enter.
•
Sex hormone handling equipment is subjected to a closed in place clean, before leaving the process room for
final GMP washing of small parts.
All walls, floors, and ceilings are of hard washable coved construction.
Process and Manufacturing Equipment Description
The range of operations and equipment used includes:
•
Sampling of incoming API in an isolator with a sample sent to the QA lab in a closed transfer device Rapid
Transfer Port (RTP)
•
Weighing to batch weight of the API in an isolator closed transfer in a bin with a SBV

=== PAGE 142 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
High shear granulation and wet milling prior to charging to a fluid bed dryer. The active ingredient is layered into
the granulator and the heel retention is very high requiring manual scraping for yield. Process conducted in an
isolator transfer in a bin to the next process.
•
Discharge into the fluid bed dryer bowl using an SBV
•
Vacuum discharge to an in-line cone mill transfer to a bin via an SBV
•
Bin blend
•
Lubricant is added via the transfer valve on the bin
•
The bin is tested for blend uniformity using PAT
•
Compression with the bin being connected via an SBV
•
Product is coated; data shows no risk of exposure
•
Product is bottle or blister packed

Product Flow
Product is passed in to process rooms and is left. See Figure 14.9. Process personnel collect materials once
the delivery has been made and the door to the corridor closed. Once processing is complete, the materials and
equipment are wiped down and placed at the door. Tack mats help prevent tracking on equipment and feet in the
event of an upset.
When in transit, all material containers for the sex hormone Agent H are closed.

HVAC

*[Figure 14.10 shows the pressure cascade and airflow direction in the rooms. The flow is always away from the]*

corridor and the operator zones and toward the rear wall. Supply is through HEPA filters and exhaust via roughing
and HEPA filters to protect the ductwork and technician accessed equipment. The dotted lines at the entrance to
process rooms are hanging vinyl strip screens. The MALs and PALs create lock chambers with a door operation
delay to allow the airlock to be purged; this gives excellent room to room separation. The system is once through so
there is no risk of recirculating particulate.

Appendix 1
Risk Assessment
A risk assessment process was used to derive criteria to support decision making and control of risks to product and
workers. The risk assessment involved an evaluation of all data relevant to support hazard identification, assessment
of potency (dose-response) and severity, exposure assessment, and characterization of risk. The ultimate decision on
the appropriate controls implemented relied on both qualitative and quantitative data. Risk controls were implemented
in a manner that is commensurate with the risk.
Risk Identification
Risk identification is a systematic use of information to identify hazards. The standard chemical risk assessment
process begins with the establishment of health-based limits and ends with a determination of whether potential
exposures are below these acceptable levels. The primary value that underpins the entire risk assessment is the
ADE. The ADE is the daily dose below which no adverse effects are expected following exposure, by any route for a
lifetime, by the most sensitive individuals in target subpopulations (e.g., patients or workers). Derivation of an ADE
includes both hazard identification and a dose-response assessment.
A review of all available data on each API was completed to identify all possible adverse effects that may be produced
by the compound. For each compound, the critical effect, i.e., the first adverse effect observed at the low end of the
dose-response curve, was identified. The ADE was then derived by dividing the no-effect level for the critical endpoint
by appropriate adjustment (uncertainty or safety) factors. The ADE value was used to derive product contact surface
cleaning limits.
Establishment of an Acceptable Daily Exposure for the Sex Hormone
An ADE value of 1 µg/day was established for the sex hormone Agent H. The rationale for this ADE recommendation
is summarized in the quality document entitled “Risk Assessment for Sex Hormones” dated DD-MMM-YYYY (not
included in example). Briefly, the ADE was based on the low clinical dose and incorporates appropriate adjustment
factors to address interindividual variability, extrapolation to a no-effect level, differences in bioavailability, and
residual uncertainties regarding potential developmental effects. The permitted exposure by the ADE does not exceed
a fraction of the daily production rate for this endogenous estrogen.
Risk Analysis
Monitoring data were collected in the manufacturing facility to assess residual levels of A, B, C, D on both internal and
external equipment surfaces and surfaces in the manufacturing area(s) and adjoining non-production areas. These
values were compared to the ADE values established for these compounds to characterize the risks to patients and
workers.
Risk Ranking Techniques and Exposure Assessment
Qualitative exposure assessments were used to provide an initial estimate of exposure and to prioritize quantitative
exposure assessment activities. Based on the results of the qualitative exposure assessment, it was necessary
to perform a quantitative exposure assessment involving the collection of data. These data on the potential level
of exposure to the subpopulation of concern (e.g., patients, operators) were then compared to the ADE value or
cleaning validation limit. The exposure assessments conducted to evaluate the potential for cross-contamination and
operator safety were performed differently. In both cases, however, it was important to identify the most plausible
pathways that could lead to exposure. An FMEA was used to identify all plausible pathways for exposure. For
patients, impurities may be present in a pharmaceutical product due to carryover from the prior product manufactured
in the same equipment. Airborne or surface deposited material present in the manufacturing area were also
considered in the exposure assessment. For operators, the industrial hygiene monitoring using personal monitors,
area samples, or surface samples from equipment or facility surfaces is fairly straightforward.

=== PAGE 146 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Plausible Pathways
Assessments were made of the following plausible pathways:
Mix-up:
•
Bar code reading control of all equipment and materials where they pass into the processing rooms
•
Separation of clean and dirty equipment by room
•
Tagging of clean and dirty equipment
Retention Product Contact Surfaces:
•
Swab monitoring of cleaning performance against the limit procedures were set up based on health-based limits
•
Visually clean inspection
•
Routine monitoring shows consistent performance well below the health-based limit set by scientific evaluation, a
synopsis of performance against limit is shown in Figure 14.11.
•
Cleaning procedures are detailed including disassembly of equipment as required with adequate training of
operators
•
Retention product change out basis; verified by swab testing
•
Dedication of the blender bin
Mechanical Transfer:
•
Critical transfers are closed
•
All equipment and materials are wiped down or bagged before leaving the process room
•
Tack mats are used at room boundaries
•
Outer gowning is removed at room boundaries
Airborne Transfer:
•
All processes occur in rooms protected by airlocks from the corridor, the doors are interlocked and a dwell period
for concentration reduction is provided.
•
The corridor is positive in pressure to the process room so that airflow flows into the air locks when the corridor
door is open.
•
Exhausts are protected by redundant HEPA filtration (two passes through 99.997% filters with pressure
differentials indicated and alarmed. Routine challenge testing is performed.
•
For product to cross contaminate another product, it has to:
-
Escape the process
-
Pass though into the airlocks, against a low velocity flow from the airlock toward the room

-
Pass from the airlock which is purged before the door is released into the corridor against a low velocity flow
from the corridor into the airlock
-
Travel into the corridor
-
Pass though into the airlocks, against a low velocity flow from the airlock toward the corridor
-
Pass from the airlock which is purged before the door is released into the process room
-
Enter another room
-
Enter the other process
•
Gowning protocols and access restriction prevents operators and materials entering other product areas without

FMEA Analysis
Cleaning Review

•
Cleanability assessments
•
Cleaning agent(s) selection
•
Design of cleaning processes
•
Equipment design and ability to disassemble for cleaning
•
FMEAs for each cleaning process
•

•
Collection of swab sample data on each cleaning procedure
•
Evaluate the variability of the manual cleaning process
•
Process capability evaluations of swab sample data
•
FMEAs of overall cleaning program to identify high risk cleaning procedures
•
Improvement/optimization of high risk cleaning procedures to reduce risks
Cleaning Process Review
A cleanability assessment was performed on the product and the product was found to be easily cleaned by the existing
processes. The Cleanability Rank was a 2 with a rank of 4 for the hardest to clean product. The current cleaning agents
were found to be appropriate for solubilizing and suspending the product for effective cleaning. The designs of the
cleaning processes were also found to be appropriate for cleaning this type of product (oral solid dosage).

=== PAGE 148 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Risk Evaluation
Prior to the FMEA analysis, it was important to understand which compounds posed the greatest risk and which were
the most vulnerable to contamination.
The most vulnerable compound is A because of the size of the unit dose. Therefore, the worst scenario is the sex
hormone Agent H contaminating compound A. The FMEA analysis focused on this scenario. The analysis of plausible
pathways, the FMEA analysis, cleaning validation data, facility monitoring data, and the ADE value for the sex
hormone Agent H, all showed that the introduction of this new API into the multiproduct manufacturing facility would
not result in a risk to patients receiving the other products manufactured in this facility.
New Product Review:
The new sex hormone Agent H product was evaluated against the existing program for its compatibility with the
cleaning processes and its ADE was assessed against the existing swab sampling database for the facility.
Cleaning Risk Evaluation Review
The existing swab sample database was statistically evaluated against the new product’s ADE to determine the level
of risk presented by the ability of the Cleaning Processes to clean the new product. The swab data for all Cleaning
Processes were found to have high Process Performance Indices (Ppk) with undetectable levels Defects per Million
Opportunities (ppm) calculated with the exception of the blender. The Ppk for the Blender was 0.34 with the potential
for Defects per Million Opportunities of 14,994. This evaluation indicated that the current Cleaning Process for the
blender is not satisfactory to safely clean the new product. The results of the evaluation of the blender data can be
seen in Figure 14.11.

*[Figure 14.11: Process Performance Capability of Hypothetical Cleaning Process]*

=== PAGE 149 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Appendix 1
In order to introduce the new product into the facility, the Cleaning Process for the blender had to be improved or
a new blender would have to be purchased. A Design of Experiment (DoE) was performed to improve the Blender
Cleaning Process. Cleaning agent concentration, cleaning time, cleaning temperature, and rinse time were evaluated.
The new data indicate that the blender can be effectively cleaned to safe levels for the new product. The Ppk for the
blender was now 2.01 with the potential for Defects per Million Opportunities of 251. This evaluation indicated that the
current Cleaning Process for the blender is now satisfactory to safely clean the new product. As these data indicate,
a small possibility of product residue, the blender will require a visual inspection and monitoring after each cleaning.
The results of the Cleaning Process Improvement for the blender can be seen in Figure 14.12.

*[Figure 14.12: Process Capability of Cleaning Procedure – After]*

Cleaning Review Summary: The review of the Cleaning Program indicated that all cleaning processes were
robust enough to support introducing the new product into the facility with the exception of the blender. To minimize
regulatory impact, it was decided to dedicate the blending bin.
Risk Control
Based on the analysis of plausible pathways, the FMEA analysis, cleaning validation data, and facility monitoring data
it is was concluded that the sex hormone Agent H could be introduced into the multiproduct manufacturing facility
without causing a risk to patients receiving the other products manufactured in this facility.
The control measures adopted were based on a belief that technique and skill-based procedures have a tendency to
fail. The controls implemented are of a procedural, mechanical, and electronic nature and are designed to fail safe.
Implemented controls include:
•
Electronic access and inventory control so that entry and exit from any room was only permitted for material and
personnel who were required in that room at that time. Secondary systems ensure that the system cannot be
bypassed.
•
The critical processes were closed and these systems were tested to ensure that they afforded both operator and
product protection robustly.

=== PAGE 150 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
•
Automated cleaning was introduced to minimize the variability from manual cleaning processes. Small parts are
still manually cleaned. The manual cleaning procedures will be updated to include procedures to reduce the risk
of manual errors and increased verification.
•
Air flows and pressures were reversed to help maintain security in the event of an upset. Redundant HEPA
filtration protects the rest of the facility.
•
The control system effectiveness was verified with quantitative data.
•
The hierarchy of controls was closely followed to ensure the selection of appropriate technology.
•
Systems are designed to alarm when out of range and to default to a safe condition.
Risk Communication
A stakeholder team was set up at the outset and comprised toxicology, formulations, development, operations,
quality, regulatory, environmental health and safety and senior management; this team appointed a project manager.
The purpose of establishing this team was to share information about risk and risk management between the decision
makers and others.
The team benchmarked with other companies and attended a number of courses on the subject. A web-based bulletin
board was set up to allow all the documents to be made available collaboratively to the team.
Key stakeholder members representing regulatory, quality, operations, and management were present during the
FMEA sessions.
Risk Review
A mechanism to review or monitor events has been implemented as an ongoing part of the quality management
process.
The Quality Risk Plan is reviewed every six months and the stakeholders are rotated so that new members join the
team for each review, assessments from those who operate the system are conducted and a log of excursive events
is reviewed, as is the operability of the system. An incentive program is in place to solicit input from the operators in a
way that improves performance and minimizes risk. These ideas are evaluated and subjected to stakeholder scrutiny.
Where the suggestion leads to risk reduction, operational efficiency, or quality improvement, it is implemented and
evaluated continuously.
Summary
The stakeholder team has used the range of tools proposed in ICH Q9 [1] and in this Guide to carefully evaluate the
facility. During the initial qualitative analysis, deficiencies were identified and actions were implemented to mitigate
these deficiencies. An evaluation team spanning relevant disciplines ensured that all hazards and exposure pathways
were assessed and controls were implemented to maintain risks at acceptable levels so that the introduction of the
new sex hormone Agent H product could be manufactured within the existing facility.

The facility uses a matrix approach to cleaning validation so therefore the cleaning limit used as the acceptance
criteria for validation and routine verification/monitoring is 0.1 µg/cm2. This value corresponds to the lowest cleaning
limit combination (Anti-hypertensive1 and Anti-hypertensive2)
The cleaning procedures are all manual based with only a visual inspection by the operator and a supervisor to verify
the equipment is cleaned to the limits (0.1 µg/cm2).

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Appendix 1

#### 14.5.2 Risk Assessment

The logic diagram (see Section 14.6) was used to walk through the risk assessment steps.
Is there a specific requirement to handle the product(s) in a dedicated facility?
Regulations for the regions where the product(s) will be provided were reviewed. Due to the current cleaning
limit(s) using a matrix approach and the current limits of detection the products cannot be made in shared facilities
per EU GMP Section 3.6 [4] where “iii. relevant residue limits, derived from the toxicological evaluation, cannot be
satisfactorily determined by a validated analytical method.” The team is tasked with improving the analytical methods
to product limit of detections below 0.1 µg/cm² (per the cleaning matrix).
Limits of detection provided by the new validated analytical methods are shown in Table 14.7.
Table 14.7: Limits of Detection for the Cleaning Methods
Based on the new analytical methods using the matrix approach for the cleaning limits, the limit of detection is still not
adequate to meet the new EU GMP Section 3.6 [4].
The team was tasked with reviewing the cleaning matrix to determine if the overall cleaning limit could be increased
by pulling out the scenarios that are below the updated limit of detection from the cleaning matrix.
The team determined that only one scenario (Anti-hypertensive1 followed by Anti-hypertensive2) needed to be pulled
out of the cleaning matrix. Every time Anti-hypertensive2 is produced after Anti-hypertensive1 a chemical analysis will
be completed to assess if the results are below the limit (0.1 µg/cm2) before confirming the equipment is clean. The
new overall cleaning limit based on the matrix approach is 2.0 µg/cm2. The updated limits of detection are sensitive
enough to detect this limit.
Internal quality system documentation was reviewed and the review indicated that the company does not have a
requirement for any of these products to be produced in a dedicated facility.
Obtain Acceptance Criteria
The team gathered this information prior to beginning the risk assessment and is outlined in the tables above.

Risk Analysis
The team completed risk analysis of the four modes of cross-contamination; mix-up, retention, mechanical transfer,
and airborne transfer to be able to answer the remaining questions on the logic diagram (see Section 14.6).

1.

Analysis of Cleaning Data
Historical cleaning results indicate that one cleaning event failed to meet the acceptance criteria over 30 cleaning
events. As a result of investigations the root cause of the failure was determined to be that the procedure was not
followed. Corrective action (additional training) was taken.

*[Figure 14.13: Process Control Testing]*

Determining Worst-Case for Overall Cross-Contamination Risk Assessment (highest risk compound)
A worst-case approach is used to determine which combination of products will be used in the risk assessments. To
determine the high risk products combine the hazard properties (ADE, %API, volumes manufactured, and frequency
of manufacture) and the process properties (process, equipment used, openess of equipment) by API.
Based on above, the APIs are ranked as shown in Table 14.8.

=== PAGE 156 ===

Factors that contribute to make the opioid the high risk product are:
1.
The ADE is fairly low compared to the other compounds
2.
The product is manufactured fairly regularly
3.
The maximum daily dose is in the middle range for the products
4.
The process does not have a huge impact as most of the products are using the same process and those using
more processing steps have greater batch sizes, ADEs, and MDDs
Based on the above risk ranking, the risk analysis used the opioid as the worst-case product for assessing cross-
contamination risks (not to be confused with worst case product for cleaning validation). Note: that all the process
steps are covered in the opioid’s process so no additional products need to be analyzed.

Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Appendix 1
Risk Evaluation
The corporate guidance on Risk Management states the following actions for RPN ranges:
1.
Acceptable Risk: RPNs less than 125
2.
Risk Reduction: should be applied for RPNs 125 to 343
3.
Unacceptable risk: cease until risk is reduced for RPNs above 343
Risk Control
Activities must cease until risk reduction is implemented for the following items:
1.
Mix-up: staging in the corridor
2.
Retention: visual not adequate to meet acceptance criteria
3.
Mechanical transfer: gowns not changed when changing API
4.
Mechanical transfer: residue on materials and equipment brought into/out of process room
Activities can continue but risk reduction should be implemented for the following items:
1.
Mix-up: wrong label on container
2.
Retention: equipment not clean/did not follow procedure
3.
Airborne transfer: loss of pressure differential
Risk Communication
Immediate notification was provided to senior management on the unacceptable risk areas. Weekly updates on
progress will be provided to senior management and operations until the risk is reduced to allow production to resume.
Change control will be used to alert of possible changes that could alter the assessments, conclusions, and risk
profile of the facility.
CAPA will be used to manage all risk reduction activities.
Training on how to manage the risk of cross-contamination will be provided to all staff involved in the manufacture
and/or packing of the products.
Risk Review
The risk assessment will be reviewed:
1.
When products are added or removed from the facility portfolio
2.
When facilities, equipment and/or processes change or are added
3.
When there is a change to a product’s properties (MDD, ADE, etc.)
4.
At least annually

=== PAGE 160 ===
Miss Olga Chung
Taiwan,
Appendix 1
Risk-Based Manufacture of Pharmaceutical Products
Risk Summary
There are several items that must be remediated in order to resume production. These are staging of materials in
the corridor, visual detection for clean equipment is not always adequate based on the acceptance criteria, gown
changes when changing API and residue escaping rooms on materials and equipment.
The team is preparing remediation plans and will update senior management and operations on a weekly basis until
the risk is reduced to allow production to resume.
